The Red Page
Adola Pankhurst, Pro-Hun
Put up the Sword, by Adela Pankhurst,
published by The Women’s Peace Army—
whatever that is! —2ls Latrobe-street,
Melb., is written by a pro-German, and as
such is kindly commended by this page to
the attention of the Censor. It is true that
the author qualifies—in her first chapter,
titled “Pro-German”: "If to deny the
theory that Germany prepared for 40 years
to destroy the British Empire and conquer
the world is to be a pro-German, I fear
that, being in possession of ordinary criti-
cal faculties, I must plead guilty to the
childish charge!” In the next paragraph
she speaks slightingly of "the apologists for
the Allies.” Then she asks with a snort,
“Am I to he labelled ‘Pro-German’ because
I dispute" certain suppositions? And when
asked, in reference to Belgian atrocities,
What about the Bryce Commission? she
makes this reply:—
"Suppose I have been in Australia for 18
months, and I have been ‘investigating’ Aus-
tralian conditions. I go into the police
courts day by day, and I discover that Aus-
tralian mefi murder men and women and
rape little children under horrible circum-
stances ; every one of these atrocities being
investigated by a court composed of the
criminal’s own oountrymen, and he himself
heard in his own defence. Having collected
these cases, I put them into a book, and
publish it as a picture of Australian man-
hood and of their behavior in times of peace.
Would this convince any impartial observer
that the-Australians are a nation of devils?”
Now, what inference does Adela Pank
hurst, whose book we courteously com-
mend to the attention of the Defence
authorities, mean us to draw from
that statement? Can it be anything
but the conviction that the Bryce
Commission points to nothing worse than
what happens in times of peace in the most
civilised country of the world? Is this
slighting of the Belgian atrocities anything
but an acquittal for the Hun? And she
remarks that the British South African
methods were responsible for far more
deaths of women and children than Ger-
many’s submarine campaign, and defiantly
adds, “Does this opinion prove me Pro-
German ?” That, this page politely sug-
gests, is a matter for the military authori-
ties.
J*
The general charge against Germany that
she aims at the construction of a great Con-
federation of States, including Germany,
Austro-Hungary, the Balkan States and
Turkey, Adela Paakhurst, who apparently
does not read her newspapers, calls a
"sweeping assertion lacking any shadow of
proof.” And she brands General lan Hamil-
ton as a man who loves war for war’s sake.
"The very horrors of it are what he likes
to see.” This from a few extracts from
A Staff Officer's Scrap Book, which, only
by straining at the meaning of certain quo-
tations, can be made even remotely to mean
what Adela Pankhurst declares they mean.
And she asserts that "England is fighting
for supremacy, to impose not our rule but
our domination over the world in the name
of liberty.” And she proclaims that Ger-
many was forced, willy-nilly,' to attack our
shipping, because we established a navy of
such colossal size, "which was to be used
for piracy and not for defence”!
Reverting to the atrocity question, the
author calmly asks her readers to “refuse
to be confused by atrocity commissions into
forgetting that war was determined upon
and declared before a solitary act of
cruelty had been committed.” Well, the
Huns had not much chance until they in-
vaded Belgium.
“We cannot fail to see that public opinion
.... only half convinced as to the righteous-
ness of the war, has been whipped up to the
necessity by hatred, and a desire to re-
venge acts which they believe have been
committed by the enemy.” Not believe, Miss
Pankhurst: know. And she states that
“even the Bryce Commission reports con-
tain no acts more disgusting than” certain
acts committed by our soldiers in South
Africa. And Adela Pankhurst’s case can
be judged by the fact that in these state-
ments quotea there is no mention of a soli-
tary act committed against a single woman
or child. Evidently women and children
do not matter to Adela Pankhurst, intent on
proving that our soldiers were sinners com-
pared with the German saints. Subsequently
the author admits that the Bryce Commis-
sion gives horrible details of murder, rape
and robbery. What is her answer? "The
violation of women and children is an almost
daily occurrence in Australia, where there
is a population of only five millions, and
where there is a settled social order.” And
she asks us kindly to "consider the deli-
cately poised nerves and brain of a young
man confronted for the first time with
death in its most awful shape... .When life
is so cheap....con we expect that to the
poor , forced murderers even the flesh and
blood of women and ohildren will be
sacred P” The Huns will be pleased to find
that they have so sympathetic an apologist
and defender in Australia, and that apolo-
gist a woman!
And she hopefully suggests: "It is useless
for us to try to comfort ourselves that only
one army amongst the belligerents is com-
mitting atrocities. 1 * She finds, too, that
drowning innocent children and women, in
the Lusitania, it, compared with the bom-
bardment by big guns or air bombs, a merci-
ful act. And atrocities are being committed
“even at Gallipoli" by the warships of the
Allies, because "the great guns sweeping the
coasts take in their range... .villages and
farmhouses where children cower and trem-
ble, where women weep and starve, and new
lives come to birth.” Compared with this
infamy, of course, the doings of the Huns in
Belgium are angelic. That, at least, is the
tone of Adela Pankhurst’s plaint. •
And thus Adela Pankhurst lays down her
policy for Australia: “First and foremost,
her relations toward other European nations
must be clearly defined. It must consist in
respect for their language and their institu-
tions, for their human qualities, and in abso-
lute neutrality in European politics... .Eng-
land must give way to some wiser people
unless she makes a speedy peace and removes
the barriers which block the natural outlet of
other European peoples.” Including, let us
hope, the starving Belgians wider the iron
rule of that “wiser power.”
There is much in this book that is well
said; there is acute criticism that does not
step beyond the domain of the loyalty that
is necessary for all British subjects in this
crisis; but all this side of the book is viti-
ated by suffragist bias, inaccurate statements
and a point of view that, to any unbiased
reader, can only be termed pro-German. Such
a book cannot be regarded as a representative
voice, of the women of Australia, though on
the face of it, it is evidently the representa-
tive voice of the publishers. “The Women’s
Peace Army,” which, with Adela Pankhurst,
we cordially recommend to the Censor and
the Australian Defence authorities.
• •
Everybody in Maoriland knows The
Triad, which has brightened the existence of
the inhabitants of the Dominion, chiefly by
annoying them, for the last 24 years. The
sixpenny monthly, devoted to “literary, pic-
torial, musical and dramatic art,” bears
throughout the impress of a twin-personal-
ity, that composed of C. N. Baeyertz (the
editor) and Frank Morton. Its chief and
comprehensive aim, judging by its contents,
is to teach teachers of elocution how to elo-
cute, reciters how to recite, actors how to act,
and singers how to sing. And it has been
blithely doing this, for Maorilanders,
for 24 years. Now The Tbiad has
come to Australia; and its first Aus-
tralian issue, true to type, contains
articles on Facial Expressions, A Remark-
able New Gramophone—no, that’s an advt.,
False Emphasis and Faulty Diction, some
brusque remarks about Lawrence Campbell,
Douglas Ancelon, J.C.W. Ltd., the Syd-
ney Harmonic Choral Society and other
annoying circumstances, stories by various
M.L. and Australian authors, a few book re-
views, some pictures and music. Doubtless
The Triad will find its special public—for
there is a public, usually young and with
aspirations towards the amateur stage, wait-
ing to be emphatically told they are all right
or all wrong. . But the first issue to those
who know the M.L. issues seems hardly
snappy or cheeky enough. There is a faint
flavor of provincialism about it. The edi-
tors have yet to learn, and doubtless will
quickly learn, for they are bright boys, that
Australia is a continent.
Louis Stone in reply to “Masetto’s” criti-
cisms :—'
“Masetto” la justly entitled to my scalp oa three
points—the method of setting a soundpost, the rela-
tion of conductors and soloists, and the applause of
orchestras when they are pleased. “Masetto” has
taught me a lesson, and for the future I shall
choose subjects with which 1 am tolerably familiar.
But wait a minute. I should not have made these
ludicrous errors. For twenty-five years I have
played the violin; for twenty-five years 1 have paid
good money (they wouldn’t take the other sort) to
hear grand operas, orchestras, quartets, singers and
players. I ought to have known what 1 was talking
about. And now I come to think of it, a sound-
post is not mentioned in Betty. It was not the
soundpoet but the soundbar, occasionally called the
bass bar, which was loosened when Walter dropped
the little “Duke.” Can it toe that “Masetto,’' that,
learned critic, doesn’t know the difference between
these parts of tire violin? The soundpost Is a small
round piece of wood like the stump of a pencil,
wedged between the back and the belly. The sound-
bar is a flat piece of wood eleven inches long, and
glued under the belly. They are as different in
shape and function as the rudder and keel of a boat.
■‘Masetto” cannot have mistaken one for the other,
for I have described the process of removing the
belly to get at the soundbar to glue it on again.
Also glue is mentioned several times. Can It be
that this learned critic of the violin raised no demur
because he thought the soundpost was glued in its
place? Again, any fool can see the soundpost, but
the soundbar is hidden out of sight under the belly.
Can it be that “Masetto” was not aware of the
soundbar’s existence? It begins to look as if
“Masetto” has had the nerve to credit me with his
own abysmal ignorance to discredit me in the eyes
of my readers.
The next item concerns Betty’s keeping in touch
with (but not following) the conductors beat. 1
know that this Is against custom, but I had to
make allowance for the fact that Betty was not a
Paderewski, and that Ricordi, the conductor, had
coached her In the Concerto. "Masetto” may take
half a point for that, If he chooses.
The last of these delectable items concerns the
method by which an orchestra shows its approval
of a performer. I will auote from Betty. “The
second violin, remembering his grand-opera days,
applauded In the traditional manner by tapping the
back of his violin with his bow.” To be called on
to prove this statement Is like being called on by
the Blind Institution to prove that eclipses of the
moon occur. Help, fellow-fiddlers, the Philistines
•re upon usi But the titbit of all, the piece of
green turtle in the soup, remains. “Masetto” gravely
announces that violinists applaud by hitting the
music-stand with the bowl Now a violin bow is a
(lender rod of wood with a delicate spring near the
head, and costing anything up to five or six guineas
tor a genuine Tubbs or Tourte. And “Masetto"
orders you to beat this fragile rod on the Ire*
edge of a music-stand. Ye gods and little fiddlers,
what a sight!
And now I think l have finished with dear
"Masetto.** But never again shall I (peak unkindly
of the hesthen In his blindness, remembering
“Masetto." Still I think that "Masetto” has »
future before him as a certain type of critie not yet
extinct Bis chief and only asset is a colossal and
abvsmal ignorance of hia Subject, coupled with a
childish malice and perversity, and knowledge would
only spoil him, even if he were capable of learning.
Australian
J. J. Malone, author of Wild Briar and
Wattle Blossom, a pleasant book of verse,
publishes in book form certain of his Talks
about Poets and Poetry (W. P. linelian,
Little Collins-street, Melb.). The selection
comprises lectures upon Gordon, Kendall,
Goldsmith and Anthologies of Irish Verse.
The lectures upon Gordon and Kendall were
delivered over 23 years ago, and are repub-
lished in their original form. Their value,
therefore, is that of an almost contemporary
judgment of these two writers ; and consid-
ered from that standpoint, Malone’s appreci-
ations are wonderfully acute, and, in their
main lines, still acceptable. That is a tri-
umph which few literary critics can con-
fidently foresee. In these and the other essays
the author’s style is lucid and ductile, and
his criticism comes from a twin fund of keen
appreciation of poetry and an alert sense of
literary values.

Although it did take a good while I
Effie E. Barker’s new booklet of Australian
Wild Floicers (T. H. Hunter, Melb.) con-
sists of a series of artistic paintings of our
characteristic flora, reproduced by the three-
color process. The artist has chosen some of
the less-known flowers, and beautiful and
unique they are.
Margaret Fleming’s little Mirrors of Silver
(Watson, Ferguson, Brisbane, per Angus and
Robertson; Is.) clearly reflect some tiny
rays of poetic truth. Her verse never
sprawls: the author has a neat and tidy
soul. Her work has thought in it, and that
thought is emotionally expressed, without
loose ends. Sometimes Margaret Fleming
attempts too close a condensation, tries to
shut Life up in a tidy quatrain. But her
modest offering is very welcome; and if her
fancies are sometimes merely pretty, they are
prettily expressed. This page has only praise
for a poet who knows her own limitations
and yet can do such precise and admirable
work within them. Here are three of her
fragile, close-packed fancies:—
accurate: there is just one appearance of a
woman in the whole book. He tells of drov-
ing, of horse-breaking, of the characteristics
of dogs, of the lonely camp of men drilling
for water, and of strange facets of life about
the Pacific—Conrad’s country. Here is a
sample of his prose:—
“ ‘Droving’s the Devil,’ repeated the old
man.
“Ever since boyhood most of them had
been setting out on such journeys, and knew
that droving had no intimate connection
with romance or excitement, that it chiefly
demanded an infinite patience and endur-
ance, promising little but parched, dusty
throats in the summer, and a very wet camp
when the rains began to falL Yet there was
a sense of freshness and adventure in the
eyes of everyone as though that day had
brought a release. They seemed filled with
the quiet exaltation deep-sea sailors feel
when the coastline dips,.though their senses
pleasantly remember the comfort of this bar
or that fireside. Before us all lay the long
road, the quiet days of following the feed-
ing herd through the barley-grass, and the
quieter nights of watching the dark mass
beneath the stars.”
But it is the poet, not the prose Aus-
tralian Tchekov, that this page prefers.

Under the modest title My Recollection
"The Hon’ble John Mildred Creed, Member
of the Legislative Council” (with portrait,
Jenkins; 10s.), publishes a large volume of
—well, his recollections. They are rather
dull recollections; but by those who like
recollections, and are prepared to pay lGs.
for them, some interesting facts may possi-
bly be dug out from these recollections of
the Hon’ble John Mildred Creed, Member
of the Legislative Council. But it is hardly
J. M. Creed’s fault that his volume about
himself should have been published just at
the time of the extraordinary slump in the
public antimation of Australian politicians.

"Ah Gee” wants books to be declared a
necessary commodity for Bulletin poets,
Aboriginaliars and himself:—
I want to buy a book, an article which Australian
booksellers are rapidly making a luxury. Consider
their ways. Some English books at la. net are
la. 3d. out here, others Is. fid. The 2s. fid. net book
becomes Bs. fid.; ss. net equals o*. fid. in Australia,
and 6a. met becomes 3s. fid. If you live in the
country postage generally makes these prices into
is. fid., Is. Bd>, 3s. 9d., fis. lOd. and 8«. 10d. After
December SI the postage rates will be doubled, so
you may pay Is. 7d. or Is. lOd. for the bob book, 4s.
for the half-crown voL, 7a 2d. for the 55., and
Bs. 2d. for the 6s. book. In fact, if you want about
three booke it will be almost as cheap to come to
Sydney as to have them posted.
“Te Babarantiko”: “Literary Taste in
the Fo’c’s’le” (B. 30/9/’l5) proves how
utterly ships’ forecastles have altered during
the last 20 years or less. I am asking in
dazed helplessness: What is a watch’s “boss”
and a “palm-needle”? The language is the
(alleged) talk of the shearing-sheds-—even to
the “blokes”; an expression I will take my
affidavit is new on board ship, and no im-
provement on the one-time “chaps” or
“boys”—provided the yarn deals with real
sailors and a real forecastle. As a
matter of fact, the average sailor is
as well spoken, and, on the whole, better
educated, than the ordinary shore-workman.
An omnivorous reader, you will find his bunk
stuffed full with books—surprisingly good
books, too. These books are usually ex-
changed, with ships abroad, for others he has
not read. If you wish to see this system of
swapping books on a large scale you should
go to one of the big South American West
Coast ports, such as Iquique, where some-
times there were moored a hundred or more
of the largest and finest sailing-ships in the
world. A fair number of sailing-ships carry
a small library, from which the men
can choose their favorites. The sailor
is particularly fond of the better class
of magazines—and there is no bet-
ter gauge of omnivorous reading than
that. Have travelled in steamers where it
was not safe to leave a good magazine out of
sight if one wanted to finish reading it.
It should be borne in mind that it is
nothing unusual for men with mate’s tickets
waiting a chance in an overcrowded profes-
sion to serve in ships’ forecastles.
“Myola” points out, what this page had
forgotten, that Germany has already claimed
the Belgian poet, Emile Verhae'ren, as a
fellow-Han. Stefan Zweig, a Hun, published
a critical volume upon Verhaeren early in
1914, since translated into English. Zweig,
in the course of his enthusiastic critical
study, asserts that Yerhaeren is most truly
appreciated in Germany. In considering him
a perfect type of “The New European”
Zweig actually wrote, “Verhaeren is part
and parcel of German Kultur”! What will
Zweig and company now have to say of that
bitter product of moret German Koltur,
Belgium''a Agony ?
We must add to our list of minus poets
the names of John Roycroft and J. E.
French. Rhyme and Rhythm, by John Roy-
croft (Brisbane), is neither. His verse is too
bad even to quote. J. E. French publishes A
Pearl of Price, a “western jewel once stolen
from the gems of an eastern crown by the
author” (Melb.), and The Aftermath, an
answer to Kipling’s “Recessional.” This page
is unable to understand what either book is
about. In case there is anybody who can,
here is the poet’s “Finale”:—
I AM.
With you in making to the moat
Of, till this day, her very ghost
Herself let well alone, entoast,
Th’ Lady till this day aboast,
With ne’er a word to tell the tale,
Enfrantic’d, even sure to fail
But nothing l —l’ve the sea to sail
Within mine egg shell useful—quail.
If from the wall it fell, awail
Should it not on the sea assail:
The enemy, encompassed, quell,
Before the victor of the shell.
BROWN RIBBONS.
Brown, brown ribbons—lengths of thread
In Life’s tangled skein,
Woven in the schemes of nations
For a country’s gain.
Brown, brown ribbons —tatters they
After battles fought,
Tom and ruined by the Weaver,
Yet the Weaver wrought I
Brown, brown ribbons—these are men
Marching with firm tread;
After Peace is bought—with ribbons —
Gather broken thread.
THE EMPTY CUP.
I gave a cup of wine to you
Drawn from Love’s bowl;
The wine—the nectar of the gods,
Tha cup—my souL
And cups seem very useless things
Without the wine;
Maybe their dust will find the grape.
So scatter mine.
A STRING OF PEARLS.
Tw ••me. You brought to me a string of pearls;
Each pearl a happy thought, a joyous gift,
A song within my heart, a living love
That came to me within a world adrift.
The glowing jewels long since have lost their glow.
Their love dissolved and vanished with, the years.
The pearls became a limpid, liquid pool:
1 now present to you a cup of tears.
Vance Palmer, whose poetry this page has
recently appreciated, proves himself an in-
teresting writer of prose. The World of
Men (Euston Press, London) is a series of
impressions of Australia and the East that
betray a wide experience of the Australian
Outback and a pretty facility in description.
Further confirmation comes with this small
volume of Vance Palmer’s ability, without
effort, to picture the essential Australian. He
describes men (white, mottled and black),
horses and dogs in vivid phrase, and he ad-
mirably evokes an atmosphere. His title is

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Bunch of Wild Flowers.
Not the great tame blooms from the tended
plots that nestle behind the hedge.
But bravo little busli-flowers gathered and
brought from many a crest and ledge :
A charm to watch by the evening lamp when
the sweets of the day are won
And I’m living again the Sunday stroll I
took with the little son.
Good luck to the gatherer, fast asleep and
dreaming under his rug!
He brought them all the way homo to “Mum,"
and was paid with a splendid hug.
May his thoughts bo ever as sweet as you—•
may his life like yours he free,
Dear blooms he picked for his only love when
he went for a walk with me!
N.S.W.
E. O’F.
Deeper than Death.
“Miss Annie 8., Wattle Grove, X Vale, Aust.”—
Address on soldier’s letter recovered from between
the trenches.
I see you gaze ’ncatlj shading hand,
Against the wattle's golden glow,
Along the main road’s dusty band,
Where he went out long months ago.
Sadder than all sad things is Love,
Miss Annie 8., of Wattle Grove.
The great grim ships swing' to and fro,
The great shells roar, the brown earth
flies;
The drab battalions ebb and flow,
Where Hell rains screaming from the skies ;
Greater than all great things is Love,
Miss Annie 8., of Wattle drove.
The yellow blooms drop soft along
Your lifted arm, high ir the tree;
A grey thrush pipes his mating song,
A love-sick magpie carols free:
Sioeeler than alt sweet things is Love.
Miss Annie 8., of Wattle Grove.
Across the scarred and battered hill.
The stretcher-bearers slowly creep.
Bearing- him down, so calm, so still.
Taking at last the soldiers’ sleep.
Beeper than Sleep or Death is Love .
Miss Annie 8.. of Watile Grove.
SOMEWHERE.
JACK SOMMERS.
Breaking Camp.
Bale stars are speeding down the sky
. Towards the plain’s dark rim ;
Grey forms on noiseless feet move bv ;
The firelight flickers dim.
Gold mists wreath round the stunted trees
On billabong and creek,
And through the grass the dank dawn-breeze
Steals silently and bleak.
A subtle earth,; fragrance comes
From Nature’s dew-deck’d lawn,
And stark and black the gnarled gums
Are etched against the Dawn.
The horses greet the coming day
. With clank of hobble chain f
The browsing sheep like phantoms grey
Move shuffling o’er the plain.
Behind a clump of salt-bush hid
A plaintive mother bleats :
And on the flame the billy-lid
A fierce staccato beats.
freed from Night's o’erwlielming shroud
ihe East resplendent grows.
And ev'ry castellated cloud
Burns with a heart of rose.
Before his steeds Apollo hurls
.One quiv’ring golden ray;
Then forth in flaming splendor whirls
The Chariot of Day.
VICTORIA.
GORDON J. HOLMES.
Lanes to His Mistress.
Your moods are variable as the moon
(The phrase is stolen from some trickier
poet) ;
As some ripe rose kissed by Arcadian June
You’re wonderful, aud very well you know it.
No other girl’s so exquisite to kiss
1 know, who in my time have known
a-plenty,
And when we go to woo the ecstasies
I have, the ardor still of two-and-twenfv.
But still I wonder sometimes, on my life,
I low I’d findure j ou, if you were my wife.
Adorable ! Your body’s witchery
Moves to despair the covetous beholders
Aho see your face. The sweets they cannot
see
Are mine....the Coyly-dimpled white sleek
shoulders,
Tiie light slight feet, the ankles’ fragile grace.
The round proud knees that scorn demanded
kisses,
The hips exuberant that quite efface
The oest attempts of prim, slab-sided misses.
Bat would you answer as the sort one
marries?
II ow should I fee] if you were Mrs. Harris?
Heart of my heart! Dear Fancy’s luring
star!
Unquiet eyes, curled lips, ensnaring tresses.
Hot breath a-quiver, curving waist!....yon
are
My luck-in life, as all my blood confesses!
Your words arc- spice and honey when you
talk,
Your fondling hands set all my manhood
singing,
Your mouth —oh. on incorporate air I walk
When mouth to mouth I have you, hold you.
clinging!
But I admit it, dearest —for my sins,
I simply can’t imagine you with twins!
N.S.W.
JOSEPHUS HARRIS.

THE NEW CRUCIFIXION.
Jerusalem, Samaria and the Mount of Olives have been turned into Turkish drill areas under German control; and at Golgotha (or Calvary) rifle butts
have been erected, where the Turk may learn to shoot the Christian. —Cable.

The National Australian Newspaper.
“Australia lor the White Man.”
Printed and published by William Macleod, of
Musgrave and McLeod Streets, Mosman, for The
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the
Co., 214 George Street, Sydney, N.S.W., Australia.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
. The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending. MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line, or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
To Travellers Abroad.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in Lon-
don, after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs.
Gordon and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.
South Africa. — The Central News Agency, Cape-

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 28, 1915.
POT-HUNTING.
The English, slow, heavy and unrevolu-
tionary by instinct, are at last taking notice
of the excess luggage which the nation has
to carry in war as in peace. A few enthu-
siasts in the times of calm were continually
pointing to this antiquated lumber and
urging that it should be thrown out; but
only under the stress of German bombs and
shrapnel have the eyes of John Smith and
William Jones really been opened to the
relics of barbarism which have had to be
humped from platform to platform in the
national effort to catch the trains of pro-
gress. The request has gone forth —it is
only a request as yet, but any moment it
may develop into an order to be enforced
by the police—that deer parks should be
utilised, that game should not be nursed to
the detriment of agriculture, and that bird
and beast kept to feed the sporting
instincts of the wealthy should go into the
pot to feed the people. A year ago such an
idea would have been regarded by British
Fat as an attempt to strike at the roots of
the Constitution. Was it not laid down in
the unwritten code of every true patriot
that a pheasant was more than a peasant
and a salmon better worth preservation than
the life of a cottager’s baby? Was not a
fox a sacred thing, and a rabbit a creature
whose tufted tail must be guarded against
the teeth of the hungry? He who spoke in
contempt of these things was a traitor to
tradition, and it was but right that he
should be taken out and socially stoned.
Now, after a year of fighting, a change has
swept over the whole snobscape, and the
nation without a blush of shame goes pot-
hunting like a poacher.
* * * *
Pot-hunting in one form or another is
the serious business of life. Ever since the
first anthropoid creature standing on power-
ful but crooked legs learned to make a fire
and hollowed a vessel in which to boil
water, the necessity of filling the pot has
been first and foremost in the human mind.
Poets might sing their soulful songs, musi-
cians toss their tufted locks over the ting-
ling keys of a machine of subtle melody,
lovers press lip to lip in the moonlit rose-
garden of dreams, statesmen shadow their
great reforms and emperors plan their far-
reaching campaigns of murder, but close
behind them all walked (he day’s need, and
the essence of the day’s need was something
to put in the pot. England, mixing her
ideas of industrial supremacy with the
notion that she was a museum for the pre-
servation of ancient customs, kept the form
of antique pot-hunting under the guise of
sport. The ancestors of the nation had
chased game because they were hungry, and
the conviction was deep-seated that game
must be chased to the end of time in much
the same fashion, when the demands of hun-
ger could be more cheaply and better satis-
fied with tame beef and cultivated mutton.
Indeed, it came to be regarded as a crime
to be hungry, and the idea of killing game
because it was needed was held to be so
wicked and wanton that men were trans-
ported to penal settlements because they
caught a rabbit by stealth to fill an empty
pot. Multitudes of creatures were shot or
dogged to death by the well-fed who did not
want them, and the line was sternly drawn
between use and amusement, until slaughter
for pleasure became a virtue and the mere
hint of the food-value of wild things a crime
against the moss-incrusted glory of ances-
tral custom. Rotten ideas were fostered by
rotten feeding, and the table garbage of the
chase kept the feeder close in thought to
those carrion-eaters of antiouity to whose
holy and mouldering memories he sacrificed
the wholesome cleanliness of a modern pal-
ate. _ The conversion of game-preserving
Britain to the plain, natural virtue of the
pot-hunt marks a revolution far greater
than the mere suppression of the House of
Lords or the dusting out of the shelves of
the War Office.
* # * *
The rival claims of the pot-hunt and the
game preserve are closely linked up with
the question of the land. Years ago when
Bbadlaugii, that fierce rebel against the
shows and shams of custom, was denied
admission to the House of Commons on his
refusal to take an oath which to him had
no meaning, simple people marvelled at the
storm aroused by his action. For genera-
tions Quakers and some Presbyterians had
taken their seats on making a simple affir-
mation ; but no such favor was shown to
the big. revolutionary who called himself
an atheist. The explanation lay in the
fact that Bradlaugii’s first political aim
was to promote a Bill for the examination
of all land titles acquired within the pre-
vious two centuries, and that it was his
avowed desire to bring all the unused lands
of England into cultivation. He could have
believed or disbelieved what he liked about
the Lord had he only been sensible enough
to believe the right things about the land-
lord. It was his denial of the sacred rights
of land grabbers and game preservers that
roused the opposition to his presence in Par-
liament. But what Bradlaugh could not
accomplish with voice and pen, the Kaiser
is bringing about with cordite and poison
gas. The immense demands of the army
force upon Britain the need of using herself
to the last rabbit warren and grass blade,
and conscription of both lives and property
is every day drawing nearer. The last
appeal for voluntary enlistment is being
made, and with it goes the polite but firm
request that owners of game preserves and
deer-parks will realise that the days of the '
Plantagenets are over, and that all the
resources of the land must be used to hurl
at Wilhelm the Nark if he is to be kept
from subverting civilisation. And here is
shown one of those anomalies which from
age to age have made the page of history
the dazzling burlesque it really is. A year
ago Britain set out to fight for civilisation:
and to-day is still fighting for it. Yet the
civilisation which is now in view is a thing
utterly different from that against which
the German guns boomed in August of last
year. England went to fight for fair play
handicapped with all the shackles of musty
and mouldering tradition. But in the pro-
cess of the fight she sloughs the ancient
wrappings which kept her from realis-
ing herself, and now fights for fair play
and a lot of other things which the war
itself has taught her. When the last gun
is fired and the first big cheer goes up be-
cause the German is down and out, Eng-
land promises to be a different nation. That
she will be a better and cleaner and more
wholesome nation is a fact that may make
even the bitter blood of her sacrifice worth
while.
* * * *
The proof that a nation is alive is found
ia the possibility of its revolution from
within. While the world goes round the
sun and spins on its own axis all things
that are living in the world of thought and
action must revolve also. The fixed German
belief that Britain couldn’t revolve blinded
the Teutonic person to the fact that his own
private top was spinning the wrong why
round and whirling back at great speed to
plain barbarism. Now he scans his bread-
card doubtfully and rejoices mechanically at
alleged victories which only mean more
hunger. Meantime Britain has wakened up
to truths about the pot-hunt. Down under
the shows and shams of things lies the fun-
damental fact that a nation must be fed.
The half-fed nation may have a gilded top
and ermine sides and a trumnet in its mouth,
but it is a falling nation. The first business
of Statecraft and Parliaments and all the
machinery of national organisation is to
see that the pot is filled. On that founda-
tion build what you will of comfort, beauty
and delight, but without that foundation ail
else is vain. The British revolutionaries
have reached the point of seeing this —or of
nearly seeing it —and therein is cause for
rejoicing and hope. If Ensland. with her
heavyweight fashion of doing things, will
hoist the pot into its true place and make
the filling of it the first national business
all the future is assured. Let it be felt that
the honor of the whole people is involved
ia the feeding of its last and least family,
and the other things of value to nationhood
will 'follow.
THE WHIRLWIND TN THE POST-
OFFICE.
Nothing much more ironical has occurred in
the public life of Australia than the recent
exchange of incivilities between the Post
Office employees’ unions and Postmaster-
General Spence. In the history of the
Labor movement in this country W. G.
Spence has a place of high honor. He was
really one of its founders, and he fought the
long fight with fine courage and tenacity.
In the early days when Spence carried on
the propaganda the way was harder than it
is now ; and partly on that account his loy-
alty to unionism and all that it stood for and
should still stand for has never been ques-
tioned. Most Australians who know the man
and his earlier work will therefore be sorry to
see the veteran in this position of an Aunt
Sally bombarded by unionists; yet taking all
things into consideration, and viewing the
recalcitrant postboy without any enthusi-
asm whatever, this paper can come to no
other conclusion than that Spence is only
getting what was coming to him. And for
him the tragedy is that he knows it. He
has seen his party, first for the sake of tem-
porary tactical advantages, and later because
it had erected fresh images in the temple,
barter away its principles until little has
been left but the husks of the old creed. A
few months ago Spence mournfully com-
plained that Australian trades unions have
been expecting too much from Parliaments.
This was true enough in a sense; but the
people who have been in the wrong are not
the general body of workers. The greatest
blame rests with the politicians. Parlia-
ments have encouraged the people to expect,
not too much exactly, but too much of the
wrong thing.
* * * *
The Labor movement aimed to secure for
the man who toiled the full product of his
labor; to eliminate agencies which prey upon
the toil of others; to limit the exploitation of
the citizen and the State; to banish extrava-
gance in administration and government; to
build up an Australian nationalism on the
industry and enterprise of self-reliant
Australians. It was a very fine ideal
and still stands as the one from which the
people of the Commonwealth will alone derive
the impulses to carry them to better things.
But it is an ideal that was soon smirched
in the rough and tumble of politics, and now
scarcely finds a place in the politicians’
creed. A retrospective survey shows that
Labor has accomplished many things worth
fighting for, has perpetrated many things that
were worth fighting against, and has left
undone more things that needed doing. The
economic problems that confronted the nation
a quarter of a century ago are still in the
same place, and most of them more menacing
than before. Barnacles and parasites flour-
ish everywhere. Public enterprises, with a
few exceptions, are managed with scandalous
inefficiency. Public extravagance is so ag-
gressive that our cost of government is one
of the wonders of the earth. And there is in
addition the damning circumstance that our
foreign debt has grown to staggering size.
It is over £400.000.000, and the tribute to
Cohen somewhere about £15,000,000 a year.
Twenty years ago Spence would have said
that it was to prevent such calamities that
Labor was girding up its loins. To-day
he has the calamity and some of the conse-
quences that have grown out of it, including
hard words from the unionists of the Post
Office.
* * * *
Much of the legislation of recent years
has been built upon the conditions created
by this orgy of borrowing, in which Labor
Governments have led the procession. The
States and people have lived not upon their
own resources but partly on what they pro-
duced and partly on what they could bor-
row, thus getting themselves into the posi-
tion of the man who adds to the income he
makes growing potatoes by securing an
advance every Saturday on the spuds he
hopes to grow next year and the year after.
The broad result has been to inflate values
in every direction; to induce a bogus pro-
sperity ; to flood the country with imports,
largely of luxuries. By borrowing cash in
London and building a railway from God-
elpus to Dingbats, and then cadging some
more for a bridge at Dead Dog Creek, and
losing half a million a year running the
Post Office, it has of course been easy for
Governments to avoid constructive states-
manship resting on Work. And the boom-
ster and the land shark and the multitu-
dinous middleman and all the rest of the
right-thinking population who get away with
the fat have found Australia a very agree-
able place to be in. Meantime a consider-
able section of the population, knowing no
other conditions than those of Bloat, imagine
that they are normal; that it is the main
function of the Government to borrow and
provide a steady job with the proceeds ; that
anyone who takes the contrary view is a
dingo or a Turk or something equally un-
pleasant. Thus it happens that politicians
who betrayed their principles once felt com-
pelled to do it twice, and. like the opium
smoker, becoming progressively flaccid, have
gone on doing the same thing some more.
Having fed his bulldog on rump steak that
hasn’t been paid for, Spence has no reason
to expect very much sympathy when the
animal takes a bit out of his leg.
* * * *
This borrowing to hide political shirk has
been one of the wickedest things perpe-
trated by any country in the world. Every-
body connected with it has been demoralised,
and the politicians—Lib. and Lab. —who
have been responsible shrink from the job
of carrying on without it just as basely as
they did from that task of regeneration they
originally sought authority to perform. Yet
there must be an end of it some time. The
day must come when Australians will have
to earn their own living. Every addi-
tional year the recognition of this
distasteful fact is postponed makes the
job ahead more difficult. No country
can go on piling up debt indefinitely,
giving doles out of the loans to workers
on one side and monopolists on the
other. The right course for Australia to
follow is as clear as ever it was—to so
fashion the economic life of the nation that
the Australian people will depend upon what
they can earn from their own land and in
their own mines and factories and work-
shops. instead of drifting aimlessly along
subsidised by the eafnings of foreigners and
the pawning of the public estate. If Labor
had stuck to its guns there would have been
no p.n., and Spence would not have been
tearfully bemoaning the unreasonableness of
a unionism whose false ideas have been fos-
tered on the long-dated debenture and the
abnormal conditions of boom which it pro-
duces.
THE AUSTRALIAN VIEW OF THINGS.
The cables have been suggesting that Lon-
don is anxious to hear what Australia thinks
of the war in general and the Dardanelles
campaign in particular. It is said to he the
great desire of various unspecified people
that the State Premiers and a member of the
Federal Government should take passage for
the distant city and explain Australia’s
views. As the first thing the Premiers would
do on arrival would be to lead John Bull
into a secluded place and ask him for the
loan of a couple of bob to build a railway
with, this paper hopes that no such emigra-
tion will be permitted. Australian opinion
can be much more decently and effectively
expressed by the wounded soldiers who are
now in England, than by a delegation of im-
pecunious and mostly-discredited politicians
with a kitful of debentures. Otherwise the
opinion can be put into two words
which the cable can carry: “Fight on!”
If any person in London thinks that
because Billjim ran against a snag at Gal-
lipoli he is looking round for the towel, such
person is making the mistake of his life.
And he makes a greater mistake still if he
thinks that the discovery of the snag will
hold back the other thousands who are
getting ready for Gallipoli.
* * * *
On the other hand, if J. Bull imagines
that Australia likes this business of carry-
ing on war with the doors shut and the
blinds pulled down he is off the track. The
policy that has kept the people of this coun-
try on a diet of thistles has not been accept-
able to the Australian stomach, and if per-
sisted in is going to cause bitterness. Aus-
tralia is unable to understand why in rela-
tion to that part of the campaign with which
her troops are directly associated elaborate
machinery should have been established for
securing misrepresentation. This sort of
thing, suddenly introduced into the life of a
democratic people, to whom it. was quite
alien, has provoked resentment and distrust.
Australians are not accustomed to be —and
positively refuse to be —treated like menials,
to be thrown a few bones from the official
table. In relation to their public affairs they
insist upon being dealt with as principals,
and where information is withheld their
suspicion is measured by the importance of
the issues involved. In this war the issues to
the Australian people, enjoying the widest
liberty in government, are of the most vital
character. They are ready to defend that
liberty to the last gasp, and to resist with
the utmost of their strength a premature
peace; but they certainly are not ready to
agree that the struggle should be continued
in an atmosphere from which candor has
been eliminated. The essence of it all is
that the Australian mind is disposed to fear
that John Bull’s economy of frankness
towards the people in this part of the world
is evidence that he is not as frank with
himself as he ought to be.
* * # *
Britain has done some big things in the
last 12 months. Her navy, for instance, has
been even a greater factor in the war than
the German army. Its power is, so far, the one
outstanding feature of the mighty struggle.
But it is a war that called for great effort —
for the utmost of which the participants are
capable—and what Australia is unable to see
is that the full force of the Empire’s strength
is being exerted to pull the enemy down.
Six weeks ago Lloyd Geokge put it this
wav:—-
Every hour counts; every hour means death;
every hour takes us further from victory and nearer
defeat, unless it is an hour spent by the nation in
putting its whole strength into this great struggle
for victory and freedom for the democracies of
Europe.
Since then the position has grown no
brighter, the need for men no less;
but Australia has not heard that the boilers
are now carrying a full head of steam. What
it has heard is that “England is over-run
with soldiers,” and it has been told various
other vague, meaningless things, combined
with frequent assurances that there are enor-
mous numbers of men available if they will
only come forward. As the Germans are
beginning to over-run Servia and the Allies
are not over-running Turkey, and the enemy
is still somewhere about the old address in
Flanders, all this information tends to create
in Australia the opinion that if John Bull
has these resources it is time he made use of
them.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Men, Not Rabbits.
In one fertile valley in the Blue Mountains
of N. S, Wales, where almost anything can
be grown or reared, there were, 25 years or
so ago, at least 20 families who all made a
fair living in a primitive way by running a
few sheep and cattle on their holdings and
growing maize and potatoes on the fertile
creek flats. But they were a thriftless, old-
fashioned, happy-go-lucky lot; and one by
one the old men died, the rabbits invaded the
district, the young people, tired of the
monotonous life of a locality far from tele-
phones and railways and trams and bustle,
drifted away, and now the land is held by
a very few people, and they are people who
make poor use of it. The few old hands
sadly attribute the downfall of the place to
rabbits, and infer that the collapse was
inevitable. Apparently the fact that energy
and enterprise can dgal with even Brer Rab-
bit never dawned on them. Their fathers
or their grandfathers had built the old brush
fences, good enough for cattle and sheep,
and agreeably convenient to cross on horse-
back, and they never attempted to
rise above that standard in delineat-
ing the boundaries of their proper-
ties. Brush fences being, so to speak,
a dispensation of Providence to Bunny,
he scored an easy win when it came
to a contest, and behaved as if he had come
(o stay. But recently a bold invader
dropped in, 'reckoned there was money in
farming, purchased a property (inclusive of
all briars, thistles, rabbits, moss afad other
r>v’o- ? s-hrae'). and went in for new methods.
Totally disregarding the gloomy prophecies
of old hands, he wire-netted the place.
■ hopped out the briars and other irnpedi-


menta, fumigated and trapped the rabbits,
and this year has splendid crops of potatoes,
wheat, peas and other agricultural products,
besides pigs, sheep, cattle and horses feeding
on the land that before carried _ no stock
except rabbits. Even rural public opinion
is changing, and admiring old hands now
say: “If only more men like him would take
up land here!” All of which serves to show
that the old hand might have been very
well in his way, but as the backbone of a
country he is nowadays not much chop. The
main point attaching to this case, from a
national point of view, appears, however, to
be totally missed by the fcs. M. Flerald and
other news-distributors, and almost totally
missed also by politicians of both the Liberal
and Labor camps. This is that, under the
prevailing system of stacking as much as
possible on to income taxes and as little as
possible on to unimproved land-value taxa-
tion, for every hundred pounds this man
raises from his land he will be called upon
to contribute substantially to the revenue,
while his neighbors, holding infinitely more
land and raising very little from it, will, by
virtue of their lesser income, _ contribute
practically nothing. Were the incidence of
taxation so reversed that the value of the
land itself, and not the income derived from
it, would be the factor aimed at, the indolent,
unenterprising person holding much more
land than he put to proper use would be gal-
vanised into activity, or he would be obliged
to allow someone else to get busy instead.
In a great empty land like . Australia it
always was the time, but is more than ever
now the time, to make things as unpleasant
as possible for people who hold land that
they don’t use, or only very partially use,
and to make them as pleasant as possible for
the man who does his best with brain and
hands to produce the uttermost fraction
possible from the territory he owns. This
was, in the days when whole-souled demo-
crats were battling for the Labor party, a
firmly-held creed; but now, when place and
party success weigh more with political
leaders than principles or platforms, it is
almost forgotten. The time is therefore ripe
to insist that the man who makes a large
income is frequently not so much an enemy
to his country as the person who makes very
little, and prevents many others from making
any at all. Taxation of a super-Dread-
nought type is what the latter class require.
The holding of large tracts of valuable land
as rabbit farms should be seriously dis-
couraged in a country which already has
more than sufficient rabbits and now needs
more men.
The Aldermanic Creed.
Melbourne City Council was lately agi-
tated by a simple scheme for increasing its
electric-lighting business at the expense of
the Metropolitan Gas Co. Atkins, a com-
paratively young councillor, moved that the
lighting department undertake the installa-
tion of the current in any premises whose
tenant would agree to refund the cost by
30 monthly instalments. Thus far only 25
per cent, of city buildings get their light
from the municipal purveyor, the other 75
per cent, being mostly deterred by the
initial cost of substituting electric light for
gas fittings. They go on burning gas
because the alteration cost an imme-
diate £3O or £4O. Atkins proposed to
finance new customers on exactly the same
system as the Gas Co. finances customers
who buy stoves or the Board of Works
finances its sewage-rate payers. The Council
would not be cEUed on to take a risk, and
it would get good interest on its money. Yet
the plain, homely proposal alarmed the old-
est and stodgiest of the Tories. The aider-
men, being probably the most interested in
defeating attacks on the co., were up in
arms. Antique Alderman Snowden snorted
at it. Alderman Weedon suggested that
“a lot of small consumers would give an
infinite amount of trouble”—as though
“trouble,” otherwise business, was some-
thing. to be avoided by a commercial con-
cern. Alderman Creighton, more honest,
was “dead against nationalisation or muni-
cipalisation of any industry.” And Aider-
man Jeffries capped the senile piffle by
describing the simple commercial proposal
as ‘‘a beautiful Socialistic scheme wnich
would make the Council a monopolist of the
worst sort.” All because they were asked
to expand the business of the ratepayers
on exactly those lines that their compe-
titors follow! The plain fact for the citi-
zens is that they have put hundreds of
thousands of pounds into a business to sup-
ply light and power, and the body to which
the management of this most important
business is committed refuses to apply ordi-
nary commercial principles to it, in the
interests of a rival concern. There is only
one word to apply to such misconduct.
These people are traitors to their trust.
What is to be done with them? Under pre-
sent municipal conditions there appears to
bo no chance of shifting them. The only
hope is for Parliament to reform the fran-
chise and give common-sense a chance of
representation; and that is only practicable
through a Greater Melbourne Bill. The
urgency of that measure was never made
more apparent than by this discussion.

Ten O’Clock Closing.
The compromise of the Holman Govern-
ment by which pubs are to close at 10 o’clock
instead of continuing their discussions until
11 is not satisfactory, and will not be endur-
ing. The weakness lies in the fact that the
Assembly itself named nine as the hour at
which the beer-pump should stop work. When
a majority of members of Parliament have
agreed qn the earlier hour, and the Ministry,
ignoring their views, fixes a time of its own
which is likely to be more pleasing to Bung,
it is pretty solid evidence that Bung has got
hold of that Ministry somehow. Further,
there is not the slightest doubt, as Holman
and his colleagues perfectly well know, that
if earlier closing of hotel bars were put to the
public vote there would be an overwhelming
majority in favor of nine o’clock or an even
earlier hour. How far is a Ministry justified
in thwarting the expressed wishes of Parlia-
ment and the general trend of public opinion?
Holman and his brothers have dodged a
good many issues in their time; but their
latest action clamorously demands explana-
tion. The abolition of the Second Chamber
may be postponed indefinitely by one as
skilled as Holman in the art of political
contortion; a party pledged to non-borrowing
may be dragged again and again to the
pawnshop by one of his special gifts; for in
dealing with such matters there are obvious
modes of talking round difficulties which the
N.S.W. Premier knows and employs. Here,
however, is something of an altogether differ-
ent nature. The Ministry, ignoring both
Parliamentary and public opinion, takes a
course of its own, and the only conclusion
available is that that course has been chosen
i i the interests of a small but wealthy section
of the commur ity which has a solid cash in-
terest in an additional hour of hotel trading.
What will be the conclusion inevitably
drawn from this? The Ministry must know.
But apparently it has reached the point
when it doesn’t care.

Gentleman One, Embezzler.
The case of William Coleman, of Auck-
land, adds one more to the long list of solici-
tors short in trust funds in Maoriland.
Coleman resided on the topmost peak of
utter respectability in Auckland, and was
usually referred to as one of the “good old
school” of practitioners opposed to taking
risks, and too conservative to touch many of
the slender-margin mortgages which are
popular in the light-hearted Rickety Islands.
The deficiency is at present estimated at
£40,000. The majority of Maoriland solici-
tors are loan-brokers and nothing else, and
the moneys handled by even the most obscure
country Gent One run to staggering amounts.
A Timaru man led off in 1912 with a defici-
ency of over £50,000. He had been handling
some £300,000 or £400,000 yearly. Then
came in quick succession half a dozen, with
deficiencies running into many thousands,
and there are a dozen or more of small fry
who have been struck off for smaller offences,
mostly shortages. In one case a young man
of about 30 was handling cheerfully and
casually between half and threequarters of
a million of loan moneys each year. Often
—as in the last-mentioned case —there seems
to have been no intentional theft; yet always
flagrant muddling is disclosed and haphazard
tumbling and twisting of moneys and securi-
ties. The Government some time ago dealt
with the thing in the tender way Govern-
ments have with the profession, and every
solicitor must now have his trust account
audited by an accountant. This has been
the law for two years, and Coleman’s case,
following on the others, proves it quite
useless. If Parliament is not pre-
pared to say that this financial busi-
ness is not lawyers’ work at all, the
least it can do is to institute Gov-
ernment audit of trust accounts, with power
to examine the solicitor’s private dealings and
accounts. Every Gent One looks hurt when
this course is mentioned, and the proposi-
tion when brought forward has always been
shrapnelled out. But it is the only way.
Apart from the actual diversion of trust
funds from their propter channels, there
are a hundred and one devious little games
which would have to be dropped if an audit
inspector was liable to be on the job at
any time. The expense could be divided
between the practitioner and the investor,
and a fee could be charged that would be
sufficient, after paying the net cost of the
audit, to create an insurance fund to pro-
tect the next investor whose moneys wan-
dered out of the trust account. Another
solution might be to do without mort-
gages ; but this suggestion is received
with a silence in which you could
hear a mangle drop. There is an
average of two mortgages to each farm
in the Wellington province, and there
are plenty of instances of six and seven
successive plasters on one place. The stock
in some districts have their work cut out
nosing about among the mortgage deeds to get
at the pasture. So for some time yet the
merry old game of calling in, refending,
borrowing on the old mortgage to lend again,
exchanging, selling and thieving will go on;
and while it lasts the regulation of the
industry is badly vvanted.
The Triumphant Policy of Making a
Mess.
Thebe is only one thing to feel glad about
in the Commissioner’s report on the work-
ing of the Yanco irrigation area —that the
capacity of the N.S.VV. Government to get
things into a mess and lose public money
emerges absolutely unimpaired. Otherwise
the situation is one of chaos. There seems
to be muddle in connection with everything
relating to the scheme. The butter factory
has been a failure; the canning factory a
joke; the joinery and carpentry business
such that the Commissioner recommends the
Government to shut the wretched thing up
on the spot. The system of keeping accounts
is cumbersome and full of inaccuracy. The
imported dairy cows and bulls have been
turned into sausages, or something. Any-
how, they have disappeared. The experi-
ment farm has proved worthless. The
officials have apparently spent as much time
fighting one another as in overcoming
the other plagues. The bulk of the
settlers are a long way behind in payments
of rent, loan money and water charges. And
over everything there broods the great shadow
of Loan —somewhere about three and a half
millions of Cohen’s good cash for which
the credit of the State has been pawned.
Of course, there should have been no need
for appointing a Royal Commissioner to dig
up this fresh supply of bones. The facts
were well enough known to Ministers long
ago. But they couldn’t make up their minds
what to do. What they will do now hardly
interests a weary public which has got to
a stage of cynicism where it is ready to
back whatever may be done as a sure loser.
How long this dreadful fumbling with pub-
lic enterprise is going to continue Heaven
only knows ; but as things are now it looks
as if the poor old Ma State had joined a
conspiracy to show how many holes a Gov-
ernment which applies itself whole-heartedly
to the job can knock in the public cashbox.
Obviously Yanco requires overhauling from
top to bottom and the administration vested
in a man with courage, sympathy and vision.
Unless this is done promptly the official pic-
tures of Cocky sitting under a figtree there-
abouts will have to be withdrawn, and the
whole concern will either pass over to a
shindykit or go back to the big squatter’s
sheep.
The officers of the permanent forces who
have been departmentally barred from volun-
teering for service abroad lately pointed out
that modern methods of warfare were so
radically different from those of the text-
books that they would presently be incom-
petent to instruct soldiers, unless the De-
fence Department afforded them a chance of
seeing the real thing. The battlefields of the
world are swarming with amateur and semi-
amateur soldiers who are rapidly being trans-
formed into hardened veterans; and the men
who have had to stay at home clearly see
that there will presently be no demand for
the warrior who last smelt powder in the
Boer War. The thing the men who have
given their whole lives to the job of soldier-
ing rightly fear is already looming up. It
made its appearance in the following news
telegram of last week :
If returned soldiers care to apply for positions as
area officers when vacancies are advertised, they
will, provided they can prove their suitability for
the work, receive preference. All other things being
equal, the- man who has seen active service will be
appointed before an officer who has remained at
home.
That is the writing on the barrack wall for
the stay-at-home. Denied the opportunity
of going after the first-hand knowledge of
modern fighting which every competent in-
structor should have, he will presently be
passed over for not possessing it.
From “Maxim,” one of the little Australian
crowd —airmen mostly—who are in Meso-
potamia :
“W.A.R.” (B. 29/7/’l5) makes a wild statement
when he says that “the war can be won only by
the machine-gun, and not by that archaic hand-
weapon—the rifle; and, further, that it is time Aus-
tralia woke up to this fact.” After long experience
with both weapons I come to these conclusions: —
(1) The machine-gun has the power of letting
loose at the rate of from 450 rounds per minute a
concentrated fire which can be rapidly directed
against any object.
(2) On account of its small frontage it is valu-
able in cramped localities.
(3) The power of being able to slew round in any
direction with the least movement makes it almost
impossible to enfilade ; and as fire can be opened at
any time, the gun can be laid by day to fire at
night.
But —■
(4) The expenditure of ammunition, the transport
of which to the firing-line is one of the greatest
difficulties in war, is out of all proportion to the
results obtained, unless under very favorable cir-
cumstances.
(5) Owing to the concentration of machine-gun
fire, errors in ranging or sighting limit the number
of hits much more than with rifles.
(6) Being more conspicuous than infantry, especi-
ally when brigaded or massed, or when on the
march, machine-guns attract more attention; and
by their peculiar rattle when firing draw the fire of
hostile artillery, by which they can soon be out-
ranged and, put out of action, the. effective range of
a machine-gun being only the same as that of a
rifle. Moreover, when on the move, they are use-
less.
(7) Owing to the liability of their delicate
mechanism to fail at critical moments, which
necessitates their being employed in pairs (although
there is no doubt that this chance is minimised with
the Caldwell gun), and also owing to the great
expenditure of ammunitior, the gun is not suited
for sustained fire action.
(8) Rifle fire can be combined with shock
action— i.e., the charge with the bayonet—and can
be used with effect against any targets, especially
against scattered ones, such as an extended firing
line or gun detachments.
To sum up, the machine-gun is a powerful
auxiliary to, and well adapted for, close operations
with infantry, but it is essentially a weapon of
opportunity, and its power can be best utilised to
develop unexpected or surprise fire against favorable
targets.
I agree with “W.A.R.” that more guns than are
laid down —two per battalion—are necessary. That
was proved in the Russo-Jap war. In the present
war, as was the case also in the Russo-Jap, more
guns actually are used than are shown on establish-
ments. I’m surprised to learn, by the way, that
there are five makes of machine-gun in use in the
British army. I have seen no other than the Maxim,
although in Australia we also at one time used the
Colt with Light Horse.
Infantry and the rifle will always be the deciding
factors in a campaign, and the other arms and
weapons will always be auxiliary to them.

“Mokimoto” : Notv that Japan is pouring
its cheap-labor goods into Australia, where
the importing worm dieth not, it is neces-
sary to say again that Nippon’s merchants
have trade methods which are —Japanese.
Possibly they have, from our point of view,
improved, but since the Japanese business
code is merely a result of the Japanese moral
code, which is entirely different from our
own, the change will only be in degree. A
favorite plan was to order goods from
Europe, say, and then to refuse to take
delivery for some trivial reason. The con-
signor’s only hope of cutting his loss was
to put the goods up for auction in the
Japanese port, when the consignee or his
nominee promptly bought them up at sacri-
fice price. A Japanese firm once asked for
a sample from an English maker of a cer-
tain patented article with a view to big
orders. The sample was sent, but no orders
came. Frequent inquiries from the pro-
prietary firm having been met with dead
silence, a representative was despatched to
investigate. He was amazed to find beauti-
fully-made copies of the article sold far and
wide in Japan. In cases like that foreign
firms would rather suffer than take their
case to the Jap. courts of law. They would
only be throwing good money after bad.
After the small brown nation had learnt all
it wanted, a steady discouragement of for-
eign enterprise began. Japan saw her
necessities clearer than did Great Britain.
With a poor soil and a growing population
her only course was the swift creation of
secondary industries, and no hampering
principles were allowed to stand in her way.
That Australians now should buy the pro-
duction of these most sweated people in the
world, for lack of a tariff to stop the rush,
is a splendid advertisement for Australian
statesmanship—especially the sort that
brands itself Labor !
The Labor party’s selection of Ministers by
Caucus is a long way from what it ought to
be; still it is a vast improvement on the ola
plan, and the nearest approach we have to
Elective Ministries. People who approve of
the abolition of the present bad old system
of Party Government can’t therefore have
much patience with the personal attack
which is being made in Adelaide on the
members of the Vaughan Government.
Their effenee is that a majority of them are
members of one family. There are two
Vaughan brothers and a brother-in-law.
But they were all chosen by the Caucus, and
all Ckawford Vaughan, the Premier, had
to do was to allocate their duties. To attack
them for being Ministers is therefore to
attack the elective system; and one has to
swallow most of one’s democratic principles
to do that. It is quite a different matter, of
course, to charge them with battering or neg-
lecting the Labor platform. In that respect
they are as much open to criticism as any
and every other Ministry.
♦
The question as to whether the personal
opinions and enthusiasms of the individual
soldier count much or little in modern war-
fare has not yet been definitely settled.
Certainly the retreat from Mons did look
as if it depended almost entirely on the
steady valor of the few British soldiers who,
against such terrific odds, refused to be
broken, as by all the rules of warfare they
should have been; but there is just a possi-
bility that perfection of training and not
superior courage was the factor that really
counted. Now, however, the question is
likely to be settled as to how far the soldier
is a machine and how far his feelings
matter. Bulgaria rushed the Turks in the
recent Balkan war with the energy of wild
bulls driven by infuriated tigers, and crum-
pled the Ottoman person quickly and effec-
tively ; but when her arms were turned
against her sister States in the Balkans her
fighting appeared quite second-rate. That
might have been due to the_ fact that her
armies were tired or stale; but now after a
reasonable rest, Ferdinand, ignoring every
natural tie and sentimental claim, has
ordered his soldiers/to turn their guns upon
Bulgaria’s old deliverer and steady friend,
Russia, in order to crush the Servian who
stands in the road of the Kaiser’s plans.
The common Bulgarian soldier' can have uo
heart in the war against such a foe, but it
remains to be seen whether that 'will have
any real effect on his fighting power once
the blood is fairly set flowing. If the Bul-
gar puts uo the same sort of scrap that he
did aaainsL Turkey under the changed con-
ditions, it will go far to prove that feelings
and sentiments are of no account in battle.
Well, What Then?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hard towards the foe we drove our fist,
The firm, trained knuckles clenched and
white.
The sky was full of battle mist;
Who knew if we had struck aright?
But this, at least, we had to show —
We barked our knuckles with the blow!
The praises of the world we won;
Our blood the battered trenches splashed;
No deed of death our arms might shun
Though steel and lead the ridges lashed ;
And through the cannon’s ceaseless roar
We guessed the tasks that lay before.
Always the dream of victory urged
Our hopeful spirits to the fray;
Flashing with steel our charges surged
O’er barriers set our march to stay ;
And always while our wounded groaned
The victory was marked “postponed.”
We cannot yield to aught but death
The ground our bitter strife made good.
The nraise of men is but a breath,
But in our soldier hardihood
We stand, that young Australia’s name
May know no hint of wrong and shame.
What have we won? Where have we failed?
Strange murmurs run about the world
Of heights of death no foot has scaled,
And legions to destruction hurled ;
And blunders past our power to heal
To other eyes these hills reveal.
Are we downhearted? Tell them, No!
Blind we may be, and full of pride.
The hills are high ; the grim, set foe
Our forward march has still denied ;
But we are game to face again
The toil, the danger and the pain.
Send us Australians! With our own
To aid us in the rugged task,
The Turks’ chief stronghold overthrown
Shall yield at last the thing ye ask.
This is our faith: through deaths and hells
We yet shall win the Dardanelles.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

Kultur and a Game of Poker.
The German Government had made its
excuses regarding the trial and execution of
Nurse Cavell, and thereby removes any
possible suspicion that, in the report on
this atrocity furnished by the-Ameifican Min-
ister at Brussels, material facts had, owing
to imperfect knowledge, been omitted. The
Minister’s account of what took place is not
contradicted, and the best that can appar-
ently be said for the bloodstained brutes who
carried out the degrading business is that
Frightfulness must prevail wherever the
German armies make war. Of course, there
is no arguing with people in this frame of
mind, and as the subjects of the unmajestic
Kaiser have to be butchers as well as
soldiers there is nothing to do but let it go
at that. Apparently the execution of this
nurse was designed by the Teutonic heroes
to have a depressing moral influence on the
Allies—to show them that the Hun is as
inflexible as iron. What it really suggests to
them is that outbursts of this sort are evidence
of terror on the part of the perpetrator —
that the execution of Miss Cavell is proof
of German fears that the hold on Belgium is
as precarious as the Allies know it to be.
Meanwhile, if the position generally is not
particularly cheerful for the opponents of
Kultur’s spread, the multitudinous Fritz is
litiding his barrow pretty hard to push. Even
the Servians are putting up a fairly success-
ful fight, and if the Allies can get a punch
in on the Bulgar within reasonable time and
thereupon dig themselves in, the various
Vons will find themselves with a fresh job in
a new place. But guessing as to whether
the Allies can deliver the goods, or whether
the Iloumanian will come in directly, or how
much longer the Greek is going to play
’possum, or trying to arrive at even tempo-
rary conclusions about the state of Abdul’s
health is, in the midst of a great darkness,
about as profitable as chasing a cat across
the roof at midnight.
“ Jwosp”: Pluck seems to be the principal
ingredient of Maoriland’s first V.C. of the
war, Corporal Cyril Bassett, of Auckland,
the bantam son of a dancing-master.
When he was in his teens he one
night went back to the bank that
employed him and found himself in
front of two armed burglars. He couldn’t
fight them, so lie took a chance and hollered
for help. Bassett got the V.C. for coolly lay-
ing telephone wires under heavy fire, and
the decoration was the first information his
parents had that their son had done any
work at all.
Melbourne has been the scene of the last
chapter of a romance. Six weeks ago a
young couple, since identified as Albert
Edward Greenwood and Lottie Williams,
both previously employed at the State Audit
Office, Perth, took rooms in chambers in
Collins-street, and devoted themselves to the
primrose way. Then, after nobody had seen
either for a few days, other residents of the
chambers and the caretaker were constrained
to take notice, and broke in to find the
couple lying in bed hand in hand, and very
dead. The key was in the locked door, in-
side, and egress from the windows was im-
possible ; there were no signs of a struggle,
and the remains of a meal on the table and
the placid appearance of all parties sug-
gested concerted suicide: only, the girl was
apparently much deader than the man. Also,
there was by the bedside one glass and a
little whitish fluid. The inquest will dis-
close the immediate cause of death, and pro-
bably no more. Whether the man came to
the end of his financial resources and decided
to assure his monopoly of his ladylove, or
whether they agreed to drop into the long
sleep together, will never be known for cer-
tain —and doesn’t matter much, anyhow.
Thus Maurice Blackburn, who won Willie
Watt’s seat in the Vic. Assembly :
The Bulletin’s outline of my political past (B.
21/10/’l5) does not mention that in my first cam-
paign at Essendon I declared myself a Socialist, and
said that I had been for 12 months the unpaid editor of
the Melbourne Socialist. I was attacked as a “red-
rag Socialist” and won notwithstanding. My letter
has been considered by two of my branches, who
have carried unanimous votes of confidence in me. T
have not been a member of the Socialist party of
Victoria for over two years. I resigned when the
present editor of the Socialist committed the party
to opposition to the Citizen Army. Many members
of the Labor party, including union officials, Labor
municipal councillors, past and prospective Parlia-
mentary candidates, are also members of the Socialist
party of Victoria, which is purely a propagandist
body. I am a Socialist who holds that reforms within
the capitalist system are palliatives only, but still
necessary to make the system endurable by those who
are not yet strong enough to overthrow it. I belong
to the Labor party because it is controlled by the
workers, and because it has no place for leaders. If
the workers are to do anything politically it is only
through such a party. I signed the Labor platform
because I believed in it. But I adheie to the paitv
not for what it has done, or for what it proposes to
do, but for what I hope and believe it will do in the
days to come.

An innovation in the programme of “A
Pair of Sixes,” at Sydney Criterion, is the
placing of the characters, not in the order
in which they appear, but in the order in
which they are named by the characters on
the stage. The result is to confuse an audi-
ence which has acquired the easy habit of
identifying each character as he or she
appears by simply referring to the pro-
gramme at each new entrance. The mere
naming of a character in the dialogue may
be missed ; and in the case of a new com-
pany this would lead to confusion. Another
matter that is not an innovation is the men-
tion in large letters that the typewriting
machines have been lent by a Sydney firm.
J.C.W. Ltd. ought by now to have saved
enough money to invest in a stage type-
writing machine or two; since every play
they produce nowadays has one or more of
these instruments in operation. The sug-
gestion that the Firm has to go round beg-
ging for the loan of a typewriting machine
is hardly dignified.

Two amateur performances were given
last week at Sydney’s Little Theatre, in each
case a revival: “The Tyranny of Tears,”
by the N.S.W. Theatre League, and “The
Manoeuvres of Jane,” presented by Fred-
erick Ward. Both productions, though ad-
mirably staged, proved how quickly fashions
change in modern comedy. No wife nowa-
days tyrannises over her husband by copious
weeping, and no modern Jane has to
manoeuvre like this in order to marry the
man she wants. Both these comedies re-
quire delicate and subtle characterisation,
and both in production dragged. “The
Tyranny of Tears” was punctuated by
pauses that, to audiences accustomed to the
swifter pace of modern comedy, proved
wearisome, for the performers had not the
personality to inform those pauses with
meaning. Pearl D. Plogsted suffered from
suppressed emotion, without indicating that
she had emotion to suppress; and Mary
Butler, lacking charm as the weeping wife,
quite failed to suggest a wife that could do
anything else but weep. F. Robert For-
ster, as the husband, also lacked person-
ality, though his was a careful and, in
some respects, a clever characterisation of
that almost impossible person on the stage,
an author. Frank C. Grimsley and Hector
