There is nothing new to report from the
Palace (Sydney) sector this week except
that an attempt to crowd it out on Saturday
night was repulsed by Lytton
and General MacMahon. Large quantities of
loot have been taken, and the company is
strongly entrenched and in good spirits.
Three new turns at Sydney Tivoli this
week: No. 1, Coy de Trickey (“The Cinna-
mon Girl”) ; No. 2, J. and M. Harkins
(musical comedy nonsense) ; No. 3 Nolan
and Nolan (comedy jugglersj. The Harkins
gentleman-partner takes the audience into his
confidence about a stage love-affair he is
carrying on unknown to the lady-partner, and
shuffles through his programmed turn in a
way that looks perfunctory. The gag is clev-
erly worked. The other new turns are up to
sample, and Coy’s fallen hair (not falling
hair!) recalls all the restorer advertisements
that have been printed for 50 years. Mary
Law and her fiddle are still the best of the
new season’s goods; then come Ella Caspers,
Hector Goldsp.nk and the other show-people
who have received honorable mention here
before.

Gregan McMahon’s Repertory Theatre
Co put on a comparatively frivolous enter-
tainment at Melbourne Athemeum last
Saturday. “The Convict on the Ileaitn,
previously tried on the dog with howling
results, is a bunch of commonplace char-
acter sketches tied together with a Dickens-
esque ribbon. The dialogue is all human
and humorous, and the moral of the droll
story is that a young Cockney bloke who has
done three years in quod is likely to work
out his own salvation on a salary of
bob a week and upwards if a seraphic dis-
trict parson finds him a billet and restores
his red-robed donah to his fond clutches.
The optimistic spirit of Dickens pervades
the humble family gathering. The character
tvpes are cleverly grouped. Frank Keon, as
before, made a success of the shame-faced
penitent who complains that yer carn’t hitch
on to yer self-rerspect w’en fokes is always
forgivin’ yer. Mrs. Fowler was convincing
again as the anxious rnUvver, likewise Miss
Hughston, Miss Appleton, Miss Moverley,
McMurtrie (the seraphic parson), T. Skewes
and others in their respective places.
“Hiatus,” the second short piece, proved to
be a melodramatic revelation of an incident
in the career of a deceased literary man.
An artistic spinster, having put him on a
pedestal, is pained to hear of his former in-
fatuation for a vulgar Amy. Plump Amy
comes along and sells a bundle of the dead
genius’s letters for 50 guineas. A duologue
by Hugh McCrae and Helton Daniell intro-
The fickle J. and the dainty M. Harkins
providing “a feast of mirth” at Sydney
Tivoli.
duces the interview between the aesthetic
Miss Elsie Probert and the vulgarly virtuous
other woman (Miss Elsie Parker). This
other woman’s disclosure of how the
late keen analyst of female character
had gone barmy on a common wench’s
fleshly charms is easily the “hit” of
the performance. Nevertheless Miss Pro-
bert deserves praise for her correct
handling of the sentimental heroine char-
acter. The construction of the playlet is con-
ventionally theatrical, and the “atmosphere”
reeks of magazine short story. The third
item on the bill was “Lonesomelike,” a
three-character comedy. Gregan McMahon,
in a grotesque chawbacon make-up, came to
the cottage of poor old Sarah (Mrs.
Fowler), a widow with naowt but t’ work-
house to look forward to. Miss Gwen
Richardson, as a bonnie lass with a weird
dialect of her own, had rejected Gregan’s
offer to share his lonesomeness .wi’ her, so
he offered his heart and home to the old
woman, and she preferred him to the work-
house. The three principals and Hugh Rose
as an incidental curate made a good job of
the sordid little curtain-dropper; and the
Repertory audience went away feeling more
cheerful but less soulful than usual.
A six-nights revival of “Within the Law,”
at Melbourne Royal, is a reminder of the
exceeding fatness of the part in which Miss
Muriel Starr first struck Australia. She
seems to have lost a little of her early force
in her interview with draper Gilder, ter-
minating in her confident threat to make
that soft father and tough capitalist pay,
pay, pay for every hour she spends in prison,
but she runs her precious black-mail factory
much the same as before. Alterations in
the cast are improvements in two or three
cases. George Bryant presents a. Gilder
senior with more than one side to his char-
acter. Miss Boswell lends an air of realism
to Helen the thief, and Sydney Stirling car-
ries a little more melodramatic weight, per-
haps, than the previous Forger Joe. For
the rest there are Harry Sweeney, Plumer,
Blakiston (insufficiently boyish), and other
people new and old. “Bought and Paid
For” is for next Saturday, and then comes
“Madame X” once more:
& * *
At Melbourne Majesty’s the ancient
glories of “The Old Guard” will be revived
next Saturday. For two more nights “The
Dancing Mistress” provides Maggie Dickin-
son with opportunities larger than she can
grasp. She and Leslie Holland—especially
She —are unequal to the “Fly Away,
Jack” duet, which is the brightest of sev-
eral tuneful numbers. Moreover the little
lady has still a lot to learn in the arts of
“straight” acting and plain diction. Her
artificial method is a striking contrast to
the frank straightforwardness of Minnie
Love, whose Scawtch song in the second
act, with a Patagonian dance to follow, is
the jolliest feature of an uneven show. Last
week John Ralston appeared in Derek Hud-
son's part, and gave a more acceptable idea
of the sentimental villain. Ralston sings
“When You are in Love all the World is
Fair” like one who has dined well and feels
happy, whereas Hudson is a restless Baron
with a frown that invites suspicion.

A brief revival of “The Ninety and Nine"
is bringing the Bert Bailey synod to a close
at Melbourne King’s.' In this plain and
obvious temperance parable Guy Hastings
is again the gentlemanly boozer, while Miss
Adrienne appears as his soulful adorer who
drops a tactful sermon into every beer.

away the irresolution of the hardest pro-

The equestrian portion of the circus show
in Melbourne got a big lift on Saturday.
The Royal Wirth Family came to hand,
fresh from foreign touring, and May Martin
Wirth turned somersaults on horseback until
the congregation felt quite exhausted, with
watching her. Huling’s seals, Maximo on
the wobbling wire, and sundry bounding
brothers and muscular sistern are behaving
as before ; also the animals that live on
butcher’s meat are roaring for their daily
bones, and the hippopotamus is growing
all the time. On Cup night, according to
historic custom, the. hero jockey of the hour
will turn up in the arena to be presented
with the Whip of Wirth.

At the Tivoli (Melbourne edition) a
couple of good musical turns are sandwich-
ing Terry and Lambert in the first part,
whilst Duncan and Godfrey, of the coster
persuasion, are doing their bit among the
I’ollies. The Belgian Concert Party—vio-
lin, ’cello, piano and Goossens, the dramatic
baritone—are the people who lately con-
certed at the Auditorium, Warwick’s Bur-
lesquers, who do all things tunefully, are
new to Melbourne, and the city is going to
like them. All the men are comedians, more
or less, as well as capable vocalists, and
Miss Maud ITane, the only lady of the
crowd, combines a pretty sense of fun with
a positively beautiful soprano voice.

Adelaide theatres have brought off a double
in doubles. At the Tivoli, hitzheibeit
nightly strives to get Conway hanged for the
death of Fitz.’s brother—played by hits,
himself. At the Royal, \ alentine Sidney
plays two sisters cleverly, but unaccountably
takes little trouble to differentiate them.
Press-agent stuff has the nerve to assert
that: audiences think two separate actresses
are at work ; so far is this from being true
that the audience can be heard asking itsei
which sister Miss Sidney is representing at
the moment. A strange play, this Rosaij
—excellent in what is written round the
jolly priest (finely played by Harrington
Reynolds) ; simply awful in its American
comic-relief; and as to its general action
defying any analysis. There is a basebal-
player with a batting average of 32U—a
trifle in itself, but it serves as a sample ot
carelessness. The players are better than
the play. The new Pink Dandies are a
capable crowd at the Exhibition; fiord \yal-
tham, Brennan, and the Fitzgerald sisters
are among them. Across the road there is
keen opposition from a just-arriving team, of
“Ideals,” who will have to be great to. live
up to their advertisements. Good music is
everywhere, Clara Serena’s Town Hall con-
cert ranking first. The King’s, has a new
bill in Variety, with the quick-changing
Marion at its head.
Parliament has practically made up its
mind, and unless it twists at the last
moment the opening and closing hour for
pubs in Westralia will be nine o’clock. Rent
reductions to compensate for the curtailment
of hours of trading are to be allowed, and an
anti shouting clause has been added by the
Council.
The State Government of Tasmania has a
bad head after a year’s financial jag. Accord-
ing to Treasurer Lyons’s statement, for the
financial year 1914-1915 it raked in from all
sources, including a £90,000 instalment of
the Commonwealth charitable- grant,
£1,244,094. It ran through £1.384,150, so
that there was a shortage of £140,056. For
the preeed’ng year there was an alleged sur-
plus of £35.284 ; deducting this, the accounts
are still £104.772 short. The debt on June
30 stood at £13.128,046, a trifle of £65 per
head of population, and during the 12
months the non-borrowing Labor Govern-
ment had put on £869,534. For the present
year it proposes to sober up and get along
with a loan of £350,000 at 44 per cent, (the
Commonwealth Bank is to find this), and
the balance of the £1,000,000 loan from the
Commonwealth, of which there is about
£lOO,OOO still to come. The main reason for
this virtuous resolution is. of course, that
there’s no more beer in the barrel.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“R.F.” : “E.N.M.,” , who is evidently no
musician, says that after the “English
pitch” the ‘'Continental pitch sounds dull
and tuneless. What bosh! The low (or
Continental) pitch, used everywhere in
Europe, is now adopted by all the leading
orchestras in the United Kingdom and by
the Carl Rosa, Moody-Manners, Quinlan
•and other opera companies, as well as at
Covent Garden, where it has been employed
for many years. The high . (or Philhar-
monic) pitch, introduced 6y Costa, is re-
tained only by military bands and by some
firms ,of pianoforte makers. Brilliancy is
not the sole consideration in either vocal or
instrumental tone, and the high pitch has
been found most detrimental to the voice.
Such great artists as Patti and Sims Reeves
have flatly refused to sing to it, and thus
helped to bring about its abolition. More-
over, it means that the works of the great
classical composers are played and sung
half a tone above their original pitch, an
E flat symphony being thus virtually trans-
posed into E natural, which was not what
the composer intended. “Elijah,” originally
sung by a heavy bass, is at the high pitch
only possible to a baritone, and the effect
upon choral singing is manifest in the
strained high tones of our local tenors and
sopranos. Mr. V erbrugghen . should be
thanked for endeavoring to bring Sydney
into line with other musical communities,
and it is to be hoped that eventually the
Town Hall organ will be re-adjusted, so
that choral works can be given under proper
conditions.
“Trooper Bluegum”: I’ve been listening
to the weird and wonderful noises which
emanate from the! trenches and dug-outs on
Gallipoli Peninsula. Some of the Austra-
lians can sing, and some make you wish the
Turkish artillery would open fire, not neces-
sarily to kill the singers but to drown the
discord. But now and then we hear pleas-
ing little excerpts from the old, loved
operas: “Maritana,” ‘'Faust,” Pinafoie,
“Bohbme.” “Tipperary,” thank Heaven,
is nearly dead. But “Little Grey Home in
the West” is still going strong. Now and
then I hear a burly trooper beefing out
“This Bit of the World Belongs to Us. ’ or
“So-Long.” Other items that are popular
are “Red Wing,” “My old Shako,” “The
Trumpeter.” “Barcarole, ’ “Mary of Argyle,
“Robert E. Lee,” “You Made Me Love
You.” and various ragtime melodies. Hymns
are popular on Sundays, even when the
chaplains are absent. Said a trooper to me
of his pal: “There was Bill snugghn up
’longside the padre, and yellin’ ‘Nearer my
Gord to Thee’ in barrertone.”
“J.F.H.,” from the other end :
Oscar Asche’s new play, “The Spanish Main,’ has
had its first production at the Wimbledon, a London
suburban theatre, where it has enjoyed so much
popular favor for a week that the boss has arranged
to transfer it to a West-end house. It is a breezy,
bustling, piratical yarn obviously owing its inspira-
tion to Stevenson’s “Treasure Islam).” Lily Brayt.on
is a beautiful Spaniard, a charming Juanita, the
daughter of a villainous old pirate, and she has the
route to the buried treasure tattooed on her arm,
the only known clue to its whereabouts. Every
Spanish girl has to smoke on the stage, and Lily
conscientiously uses up several cheroots in the course
of the voyage. Oscar is the jovial, rollicking Cap-
tain Pat O’Gorman, the. master of the Irish barque
Albatross, chartered to go in quest of the treasure.
He. makes energetic efforts to encompass an Irish
brogue, and by constant practice may succeed, but
at present his accent would not pass muster -among
the O’Gradys. The scenery is first-class, arranged
in the best “Kismet” style. According- to the play-
bills the piece was written by “Vasco Marenas,”
but-there is a growing suspicion that Vasco’s other
name is Oscar. Asche is developing a good deal of
literary activity. He has completed a new Arabian
Nights play and a revue, and Fred Norton is now'
supplying them with music.
The Hoyt’s Picture people, headed by
Arthur Russell, have now secured a site for
their Sydney house in George-street;
and will commence operations about the end
of the surfing season. They project a well-
ventilated theatre capable of holding 2000
people. It is oiWthe cards that they will
extend their operations still further. Mean-
while manager Nicholson is holding big con-
tracts for exclusive features.
“Mid-Channel”: “0.K.” says (B.
21/10/T5) lie is “not struck on the cold,
unbending mannerisms of Millward”; fur-
ther, that Millward “belongs to the Cuyler
Hastings school?’ If Millward is the pupil
he has surpassed the master. His Kayton,
of “The Argyle Case,” was vastly superior
to Hastings’s cocaine fiend. In supposedly
tense situations Cu.vler’s formidable efforts
to maintain his outward calm marred
his performance. This could not be said of
Mill ward’s Kayton. Moreover, Millward's
deliveries sound as if coming from a man
and not from a machine. Just think of what
Hastings was like in “If I Were King”!
Generous Geoige Marlow hands out
another example. Some of the hands at
the Adelphi (Sydney) have left for the
Front, and others are preparing for the
journey. All of them have been told (1)
that their places will be available for them
on their return; (2) that those coming back
safe and sound will also be handed £25;
(3) that those disabled will receive £100;
(4) that any amongst them winning the
V.C. will get £250 and the boss’s admiration.
“Y.Z.”: Sitting out “Playing the Game”
at Melbourne King’s, I am convinced wo
have had too much of nature’s gentlemen, a
curious American product possessed of all
the virtues essential to the money-maker,
and investing those virtues with the highest
moral values for the gratification of men
who make money no matter how. The sub-
lime hero of “Playing the Game” runs gamb-
ling hells, buys votes wholesale in pursuance
of his desire to become senator, and has been
made mayor on similar lines, but, bless you !
he is a magnificent raw product, a nature’s
gentleman ! He may eat peas with a seoop,
and gnaw his chop with guttural noises, but
he has fought his way up, by God! and he
has never played it low down, by G —! and
(by G —!) he wouldn't shoot a chance
acquaintance in the back unnecessarily—
no. siree! Everybody in the little Western
town just adores him because he uses his
“gun” with such judgment, and gambles so
desperately, and only cheats when it is
highly advisable, and never in any circum-
stances uses correct English, or eats silent
soup, or puts on dog in the presence of the
meek and lowly comic pugilists and inno-
cent faro-bankers who are his body-
guard, Lord love him! In short, the
hero who is a nature’s gentleman is the
usual melodramatic villain regarded in a
wholly different light. Really in the aver-
age drama there is no good and bad. There
are only, prejudices and preferences.
“Ex-Nil” : If you want to forget the war
for a few minutes (if possible) go to a movie
show where they are featuring a child
actorrr. If it be properly advertised the
kiddies will roll up in hundreds and give you
quite a good time. When the Hero climbs
through the Burning Window which is fall-
ing into the River and snatches the Heroine
from the Jaws of Death, there is a dead
silence. Even deader than the Heroine
would have been if the fire were not on a
separate film and the smoke were not steam.
But when the child actorrr jumps on a
bicycle and rides 200 yards and telephones
for the police (American police of course)
there is a juvenile roar mixed with an in-
fantile (how do you spell skreem?) of tri-
umph and delight. I have never heard any-
thing so rousing at a fight or a football
match. I have not been to Gallipoli.
Sousa, the sprightly Yankee composer and
erratic conductor, is apparently to have
official honor. An “influential committee”
has memorialised Congress to the effect
that the U.S.A. are destitute of an official
march, and suggests a couple of Sousa’s for
choice—“ Dixie” and “The Stars and
Stripes for Ever.”
Sarah Bernhardt made her first appear-
ance after the loss of her leg at a Sunday
afternoon miscellaneous entertainment for
a French war charity. A vast audience
gave her a touching welcome. She did no
acting, but contented herself with three
patriotic recitations, one of them written
by her son Maurice and another by a grand-
daughter. She recited standing and “with
the fervor and beautiful golden voice of
former days.” Then came her struggle back
to the regular stage, and, as the cable said
last week, realisation at last that her acting
days are closed.
After joyfully detailing how the manage-
ment of Sydney Adelphi proposed to spend
£20.000 in completely. remodelling the big
auditorium Sydney Telegraph grew pessi-
mistic, and wound up a recent article by re-
marking that the Christmas attraction
would be “Dick Writtington.” Let’s hope
not!

Charles Doudney, killed in Flanders, was
an unusual mixture of Anglican vicar and
crack rifle-shot. He had parishes in various
parts of South Aus. from 1896 onwards,
always joined the local rifle club, once won
a “Kingship,” and shot at least once in the
State team. Ten years ago he returned to
England, and he was one of the first to
leave his country vicarage arid get to the
Front as chaplain.
When the call comes they just drop their
job and go. For instance, there’s A. E.
Wearne, who for years has been Reuter’s
Press Agency representative at Pekin. He
heard the call last week while on a visit to
his relatives in Sydney and crowded himself
into khaki. This isn’t his first helping of
war. He got that from brother Boer as one
of N. S. Wales’s first contingent, and re-
turned to his native land as adjutant to
Rupert Carington.
THE UNKINDEST CUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Few actors have the dignity
Of Arthur Sterne Macready Brown.
A man of many inches he,
And once of inter-State renown.
That to the bunglers of our time
He must give place he holds a crime.
Yet only fell the bitter curse
Upon his noble head that day
When he was called on to rehearse
In Bills and Phillet’s touching play.
The star was little Tottie Waite,
Child-actress she, and rising eight.
When Arthur strode across the stage
The fat producer sadly smiled.
“Another lead we must engage,”
He said. “You do not suit the child.
I know you do your best, but there,
My boy, you can’t act up to her!”

The Epistle of Billjim, by the hand of his
friend Ted Colies, formerly black-and-white
artist, now ambulance-carrier in Gallipoli:—
You want . the dinkum oil about thia bit uv
trouble? Well, here goes. First of all, Lawd
knows what th’ flamin’ Turks number, but we’ve
accounted fer thousands of ’em, anyway 1 Bein’
in their own country, an’ knowin’ the distances an’
elevation of all these gullies an’ hills, an’ bein’
acquainted with every twist an’ corner uv the blanky
place, they can put us at a disadvantage whenever
we move. Yet ’ere we are, a handful, not only
holdin’ our own, but gradually—if only a few
’undred yards every few days—beatin’ ’em back an’
winnin’. But, cripes! it’s tough work for a little
push. Consequently a lot of us gets “knocked.”
And a lot more gets fair done-up an’ in bad condi-
tion for th’ next scrap. Th’ strain, not only in
dealin’ out stoush, but in havin’ to keep awake after-
wards to hang on to what we got out uv it—well,
after a couple of months it’s Hell! If we had the

Ford, who makes the motor-ears that have
hitherto had a large sale in this country, has
something to answ T er. A month ago, when
the Allies were busy looking for money in
the U.S.A., the ’Frisco Examiner, among
other papers, published this, received through
the International News Service: —
New York, September 24. —“If I find any of the
banks in which my company or myself have deposits
taking part in the proposed loan to the Allies I will
immediately close our accounts. I won’t have any
part of my personal funds or the Ford company
funds, directly or indirectly, aiding the flotation of
this loan.”
This was the statement made to-day by Henry
Ford, the millionaire automobile manufacturer. He
continued:—
“If I had my way I would tie a tincan to this
joint Anglo-French Commission, and chase it back to
Europe.
“One of my strong objections to this loan is
that the foreign commissioners are trying to put
over a huge loan without putting up'real security.
“Why, you know who is back of this loan proposi-
tion, and making every effort to win its favor with
the American people? It is the militarist. He sees
big profits in the scheme.
“The British war loan is dangerous because it will
connect the American people with the war. It is
hardly to be expected that a nation with its pocket-
book at stake can remain peaceful.
“If England wants the money, why doesn't she
bring the American securities she holds over there
and sell them ?”
The Ford company is regarded as the largest bank
depositor in the country holding accounts subject to
cheque. It is estimated that the amounts held sub-
ject to cheque are as high as 530,000,000.
Presuming that the International News Ser-
vice isn’t a liar, this country at any rate
will know what to do with Henry Ford.
Fie has a perfect right to stand off any in-
vestment ; bat if he has used his power to
squeeze banks in which he is merely a
depositor into boycotting the loan, then Aus-
tralia at any rate is going to tell Mr. Ford
that it doesn’t propose to help him to acquire
any more money and power than he has at
piesent.

# * * #
Dissatisfaction has been sizzling over the
manner in which the Artists’ lied Cross Art
Union was conducted in Sydney by Nelson
Illingworth and E. J. Henry, treasurer and
secretary. The Attorney-General sanctioned
the scheme on the condition that not less
than half of the gross proceeds was paid to
the Red Cross. This was more than com-
plied with, a sum of £660 being handed over;
but the books are in such a dreadful muddle
that it is not easy to prove anything. The
Attorney-General stated in the Assembly the
other night that so far as he knew the com-
mittee had not published either a balance-
sheet or the names or numbers of the win-
ners of the prizes. Further, members of
the committee had been paid for their works
various amounts ranging from fl tl in the
case of Nelson Illingworth himself down to
a trifling guinea or two. Apparently no
suggestion is made that these people ran the
art union to “make a bit” for themselves;
undoubtedly their desire was to “do their
bit” with the rest of the community. But it
15 .,? Pity that the affair was not managed
with less of the notoriously haphazard
methods of the artist. Then the helpers
might have reaped credit instead of a har-
vest of suspicion.
UNDER SUSPICION.
Dr : E . Robertson, chairman, reported at the meeting of the Board of Health to-day that 124 cases
of meningitis had occurred for the fortnight ended yesterday in Melbourne and 29 outside municipalities.
It was a peculiar thing that no cases, he had been informed, had occurred among sufferers from
venereal diseases.
SEVERE RESPECTABLE P ARENT: “ Here, you young rascal, explain yourself—why have you
not had meningitis?”
“0.K.”: At the outbreak of the cerebro-
spinal meningitis epidemic in Melbourne
(which seems to have originated among the
troops who were hastily and damply herded
together on the Agricultural Showgrounds),
the local medical profession missed a great
financial opportunity: it weakly admitted
its inability to supply means of resistance
to the disease. Thus far the outbreak is
known to have killed more than 150 persons
outright, and of another 350 who caught the
disease in camp or from a “carrier,” the
majority will be permanently injured,
physically or mentally, or both. The policy
of the Public Health authorities was to
belittle the wickedness of the meningitis
demon, in order that the local troops might
remain at liberty to see life outside of camp.
A. few cases of “small-pox,” such as called
for emergency legislation two years ago,
would have necessitated a quarantining of
all the camps; yet the Health authorities,
who had insisted upon the isolation of con-
tacts when the non-deadly small-pox scare
was raging, were content to let the deadly
meningitis have a fair field and no disfavor.
They did nothing to prevent it being
carried everywhere. And the doctors! Was
it their business to make a song about the
hideous danger? Having no anti-meningitis
treatment to sell they just watched the
course of the contagion and casually men-
tioned that eucalyptus-oil is a valuable thing
as popular household prescriptions go.
* * * *
From “Vagabond,” who has now had 14
months of it in the trenches in France and
i landers
It is a bit difficult to answer “S.T.S.” (B.
17/6/’l5) as to just what makes defensive Are so
much less “unbearable” that, after one lot of
attackers have been irresistibly swept away, another
lot can make a rush and get home. I’ve seen the
thing happen again and again, and although I
can t explain the reason with certainty I can give a
personal opinion.
The human factor counts for a lot; perhaps for
more than ever in this killing-by-machinerv age.
All troops have a breaking strain, and it is the side
that reaches this point first that goes under. That
a line Can hold off one attack is no sound reason
why it should hold off the next. The defenders are
getting cruelly hammered before, during and after
an attack. They are gradually working up to that
point where they begin to crack up. They may
never actually reach that point simply because the
attack may come too soon and fail. On the other
hand, the attackers may stand the strain better, and
by persistent pushing may break through. The first
attack may meet a fire which is physically impossi-
ble. Tlie next regiment or even (though this sel-
dom) the remnants of the first regiment may make
another push, and the defence, badly mauled by the
first attack and by constant artillery fire, may meet
the new onslaught with a less rapid and accurate
fire, and so fail to hold off the new rush. A lot
depends, again, on the supports pushed up to main-
tain the wastage of the defence—whether there are
enough of them or of good enough stuff.
Another point is important. Up to the moment
of the actual attack the defenders of a position are
keeping as far as possible under cover, well down
behind parapets, or even for a large part in dug-outs
or the deeper ditch behind the firing step or plat-
form. When the first attack is launched the
defenders must crowd up on to the firing step and
to the loopholes, or actually fire over the parapet.
Naturally they get it in the neck to a much greater
extent from hostile shell, machine-gun and rifle fire.
They beat off the first attack, and it is even possible
that, being busy doing it, they individually don’t
notice what they are suffering. When they get time
to look round and see the pile of casualties and the
wreck the parapets and other defences are in, they
begin to wonder if they can stick out another rush.
When they arrive at that frame of mind, the difficulty
of the attackers’ job begins to slide down the scale.
It is the amazing slowness of the British fighter to
realise that he may be going to get licked, or to
arrive at that state of wondering whether he can
stick it out any longer, that provides the secret of
most of his success. It is going to win us this war.

“Hamer”: Britain can still have its glad
’rags; but it mustn’t ask for too much color
in them. _ A great effort was made to pre-
vent a crisis in the textile industries when
the country’s foolish dependence on a for-
eigner for dyes was discovered; and British
Dyes, Ltd., was created by a large subscrip-
tion of Government money. But it won’t
be able to catch up properly for two years.
Out of the £2,000,000 worth of dyes used
every year in the English industries.
£.1,800,000 were imported from Germany,
and that is too much leeway to make up in
a hurry. Meantime, the stupid Freetrade
person can’t even go into mourning for his
own past follies, since black dyes are speci-
ally affected.
*** ■ *
“Reuben Henry,” from a point of van-
tage :
How the pendulum swings 1 I remember that
“The Old Soldier” in The Bulletin about 18
months ago placed marching as the first accom-
plishment of the soldier, shooting second, and cere-
monial a bad third. Since then marching has
got down among the also-rans. The soldier does
not march. If he has a long way to go he goes by
ship, train or motor, otherwise it is not worth going,
as the other bloke will be there about a week before
him. If he has a short way to go he just walks.
He never troubles to march. To shoot and not to
shoot come second and third. To shoot like blazes
when there’s something to shoot at is important;
almost as important is to refrain from shooting'
when shooting would merely waste ammunition. “The
Old Soldier” quite missed the modern winner. The
one thing the soldier must be really good at is the
use of the pick and shovel, or, in trench terms,
“Mad Mick and Mrs. Lovell.” That and to win is
all there is in war.
THE RIGHT GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The rarest thing of all, my boy,”
Said old-man What’s-his-name,
“Is not in winning shallow joy
With piles of minted gold alloy,
Or working up to fame;
But it is this—to take to wife
The right girl. That’s success in life.”
Now that was many j-ears ago—
I’ve seen some life since then,
And in my age have come to know
A rarer thing than he could show.
’Tis giv’n to many men
To get the right girl now ; but, oh,
How hard it is to keep her so!
The right girl’s caught at twenty-two,
Again at twenty-five;
When thirty years have faded, you
Another right girl have in view;
At forty, man alive!
You know that light girls don’t exist
For any but the bigamist.

“Mujik": f It was at a recruiting meet-
ing and the orator was holding forth about
the duty of Young Australia :
Should you not keep the enemy away ? Is Aus-
tralia to suffer the inconvenience of war on her own
soil ?
I’ut that way, I can see that war may be a
bit inconvenient. Having the office tor-
pedoed and the shop punched inside out and
being compelled to get up in the dark and
rush across country with one boot and an
uncooked spud in order to escape being
butchered —yes, distinctly inconvenient
How uncomfortable, to say the least of it,
ii Mrs. Pure Merino, instead of going round
to the McChiffons for dinner after golf, had
to toast a rabbit on the end of a stick after
walking IT miles! Really most inconveni-
ent. It was nearly midnight, and wet and
cold. A boy felt himself lifted out of his
hunk, and in the darkness a 16-year-old
sister’s arms went around him and her
steady voice said in his ear—“ Hush.” He
was carried swiftly into the next room, and
in the half light could make out his father
standing by the window with a rifle, two
arpied settlers beside him. A woman kissed
him his mother. The back door was
silently opened and as the girl scurried into
the dark a rifle rang out, and then its
answer, and after that many another. With
the hoy in her grip she kept on for about
a quarter of a mile, and then collapsed into
the undergrowth. There the shivering pair
remained and were found next day by a
trooper. Of the four who had stayed behind
in the house waiting for death or the help
they had sent for, the boy and his sister
saw none again. You see the Maoris got in
before the help arrived. I happen to know',
because that girl was my sister, and it was
me she carried. Yes, w r ar is inconvenient
—-when it arrives on the doormat.

“The Pariah”: “Feng Shui’s” par (B.
21/10/’l5) about Chinese officers and archery
reminds me. In 1907, returning from a
walking trip to the Yangtse gorges, I saw a
big arsenal which contained hundreds of
thousands of pounds’ worth of modern guns
and ammunition, yet the guard of Manchu
bannermen was armed with spears and bows.
Cantlie, in his life of Sun Yat Sen, says he
saw the same thing in Northern China. Still,
it wouldn’t do for Australia to be misled by
a little thing like that.

knows a thing or two, after all.
* * * *
The South Coast (N.S.W.) miners con-
tinue to show their disrespect for wages
and the authority of the Arbitration laws.
The other morning, when 250 of them rolled
up to the Bulli mine prepared for work or
whatever else might happen, they discovered
that the deputies were annoyed because they
had been asked to do a job which they
thought somebody else should have done. It
was the sort of trouble that a little volun-
tary arbitration should have adjusted in five
minutes; but as that would be beneath the
dignity of a South Coast miner the 250 men
turned their backs and went without a
day’s pay. The loathing of these miners
for money will yet compel the owners to en-
gage special men to chase them with pay
envelopes and a lasso.
* * ■* * ,
“Oyez” : Anew habit which has grown up
with the Holman Government got a bump
the other day. For some time past Ministers
have been signing notices in the Gazette
with their bare, undecorated names—as H.
C. Hoyle, Arthur Griffith, J. H. Cann, in-
stead of adding Assistant-Treasurer, Secre-
tary for Public Instruction, Secretary for
Public Works, etc. Now, the Mines Act
requires that before any mining lease is can-
celled notice must. appear in the Gazette
purporting to be signed by the Minister in
charge. The Yerranderie leases were can-
celled in June last, under a notice signed by
John Estell, who didn’t claim to be Minister
for Mines or Minister for anything else.
On the matter being brought before Equitv
Justice Simpson he decided that this was
insufficient to cancel the leases and that the
Gazette notice aws bad in law. The
decision is likely to affect thousands
of Gazette notices, and there is much
lack of jubilation in the Public Service
Departments, which don’t know where they
are.

would be given on the newspaper contents-
bills to the inquiry in Melbourne into the
alleged ill-treatment of

Walter Perry, a re-

asserts that he was
roughly handled by a

and everybody who
ever came into contact
with Perry is being
called as a witness for

agitation by Billy
Maloney, M.H.R., who
seemed at first to have
an excellent case. The
chief point at issue is
whether Perry is a

cialdom. or a finished
and resourceful malin-

particular clerk in holy
orders testified that
Perry had tried to con-

a Perry at the gate of

and on the wrong side.
The same clergyman
swore that Perry be-
fore going to the Front
told him that he would
see the Defence De-
partment in Hell be-
fore he fired a shot.
“I looked upon him as
an ordinary Socialist,”
said this witness, and
the Court smiled.
Perry, whose physical
and mental condition is problematical, is in
a receiving house awaiting the result of the
inquiry.
* * * *
A man with the name of an ancient liti-
gant appeared in a Sydney Court last week.
When lawyers are arguing a supposititious
case to illustrate their meaning, they have a
habit of referring to Roe and Doe as the
mythical litigants, and the names figure
in dozens of legal text-books. This
time Doe was actually the plaintiff in a
claim for breach of warranty. He had hired
a house in the Glebe, which was in such
splendid repair that one day the leg of his
chair went through the floor and he received
injuries for which he wanted compensation.
The facts were not disputed, but the land-
lord, who happened to be a lady, pleaded
that there was no warranty that the floor
was sound, and no agreement to make it so,
and she got a verdict. According to this
a landlord is entitled to let a house though
the floor may open and pass the tenants to
the worms beneath. In Paddington a few
years ago a confiding tenant leaned a side-
board against a wall, which promptly fell
out. In that case also the Law w T as kind to
the landlord, and gave a dark hint that the
tenant who was foolish enough to think that
a wall was so strong that it could stand a
push might be liable for damage to property.
Many artless Melbourne moralists will be
surprised to hear that the bookmakers of
Victoria are a union for the purpose of
fighting the tote. A lot of nobbled parsons
and other anti-tote workers have known or
guessed about the union, but to the unin-
formed purists who view “the legalisation
of gambling” with righteous horror even
when they are having their own little flutter
with mining stock—to these poor snufflers it
came as a shock to read that the Federated
Bookmakers of Victoria gave their assist-
ance to the Police Charity Carnival at Mel-
bourne Exhibition in aid of Hospital Sun-
day. The legality of bookmaking is threat-
ened. The Tote Bill which Ikey Mo is
scheming in the first place to keep out, and
in the second place to block when it arrives,
would bring the police in collision with the
books. So, in an audacious bid for public
sympathy and the Force’s friendship, the
Federated Bookmakers have allied themselves
with, the police in charitable endeavor! But
nothing that Ikey Mo ever does for the hospi-
tals is worth considering in comparison with
what the tote would do. There are half a dozen
crying needs for the legalisation of a clean,
democratic tote in place of the unclean, mis-
chievous bookmaker. And the hospitals’
need is one of them.

“Nor’-nor’ West”: The light on Karon
Rock, Cook Strait (M.L.), is burning at
last. Many a shipmaster dropping in there-
abouts will feel more comfortable now for the
gleam that warns him of the foul ground
running from Terawhiti to Island. Bay. The
Maoriland skippers had a battle to get this
light, and many of them will see tiie long
arm of coincidence in the dead heat between
its installation and the death of Jack Millar,
formerly Minister for Marine. When the
Penguin, poking about in the wrong place
for the pocketed light on Pencarrow Head
at the entrance to Wellington, ripped her
bottom out off Terawhiti, the public de-
manded to know why such an obviouslv
dangerous place was not decorated with a
lantern. Millar, who in the long ago had
been mate of a coaster waddling along at
seven knots, said the idea was absurd,
whereupon the whole seagoing fraternity
rushed ashore to say that Millar didn’t know
what he was talking about; that in these
days of high-powered steamers making quirk
passages and rapid landfalls it was a
scandal to leave Terawhiti sicking out its
long, dark nose right in the track of traffic:

thing more terrible than the wreck of the
Penguin and other small vessels was certain
to occur. The riot raged with great fury for
a time, and at the end thereof Millar was
thrown out of his job and another Govern-

THE INTERRUPTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What does it matter if, in love with life,
In love with love, exultantly we sing?
What boots the risk? What boots the barren strife
Of day? The garden-god is king!
We fear no foe, nor dread the undertaker. ...
Oh, hang it! —what a nuisance !... . Here’s the baker,
Who says that Mrs. Jinks is very ill,
And will we kindly pay his little bill?
Why should we sigh who, in the meads of June,
Pluck lazy flowers to deck the blazing day?
Why should we weep, when all the world’s in tune
With youth, and pleasure thrills our raptured
clay ?
We praise smooth Venus. Gratefully we elutib
her....
The knocker.... Here’s a go! That rascal butcher,
Demanding coin, shouts hoarsely from his seat
His ultimatum vile—No pay, no meat!
Oh, little flies that spoil our honeymoon,
And mar the melodies our hearts adore!
Oh, vandals grim that, as we doze and croon,
Disturb domestic blisses evermore!
We have to work—to work!.... the Devil’s in it!
Joy’s summer must be paid for by the minute.
(Here’s that fish-hawker now who says we must
Pay cash, because he won’t sell fish on trust!)
X.S.W.
ELLERSLIE GREY.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
The recruiting campaign in X. 8. Wales
has dug out one orator —Sergeant P. 11.
Coates, who used to be a Deputy-Registrar-
General, but has promoted himself into
khaki. Coates takes the gloves off to the
crowd. “Can’t you see? Can’t you feel?
If the Germans come here —and that is one
of the possibilities of the present situation—-
what of your sisters? What of your
mothers?” _ As Coates was speaking the
other day in Sydney a press photographer
snapped the orator. He should have “got”
the faces of the crowd. It would have made
a far more interesting study.
“Eko” : From the little one-pub township
of Longwood (. Vic.) two men have gone
forth to get the Victoria Cross. One, Major
Maygar, now at the Front, won the decora-
tion in South Africa ; the other, Lieut. Fred
IT. Tubb, acquired his at Lonesome Pine, the
other day. He is one of the live sons of
a former schoolmaster at Longwood, and all
are doing their bit in the Big Row. The
youngster got a nice safe billet as a transport
officer in Egypt, but that was no good to
him. He exchanged into the trenches and
in the fulness of time got one in the arm
and another in the head, which sent him to a
hospital in England. He is as white a little
fellow as ever was born in Australia, and
you couldn’t say more if you included the
universe.
P. .T. Clara, who used to make things
lively in the N.S.W. Assembly over a decade
ago, passed out at Waverley last week. Clara
was a strong Laborite with a command of
violent language. Although he has been lost
sight of politically for years he had not lost
his interest in politics, and was in the list of
X.S.W. Labor candidates for the Senate.
TIME WORKS WONDERS.
DIDO (to ex-police constable and old enemy):
“ 'Struth, if a man ’ader told me a year
ago that you an’ me 'd be cobbers ruu-
nin’ blokes in I’d ha’ passed ’im one.”
When some troops were being embarked at
Wellington (M.L.) years ago for South
Africa, a young sub-lieutenant fainted. In
Gallipoli a few weeks ago, when a Maori-
land regiment was ordered to inter-
view the Turk with the bayonet, the
way up the fire-swept hillside to the
trenches was led by the Colonel —Bau-
chop, of Dunedin. The test of officers
and men was supreme. They responded
with a splendid gallantry and suffered hor-
ribly. "I saw him when he. was brought
down, desperately wounded,” says General
Godley in a despatch. “‘I hope we did what
what you wanted,’ he said to me.” Bau-
chop was the youngster who had fainted on
the Wellington wharf 15 years before.
An Adelaide schoolmaster, John L. Young,
u ho died in 1881, leaving no successor, vet
whose memory is so cherished by his old-
boys that on the 30th anniversary of his
death they founded an association to keep
his memory green, is to be commemorated
by two University scholarships. One, on a
bequest from J. 11. Finlayson, formerly edi-
tor and part owner of the Register, is for
political economy; the other, founded by
subscription, for general research work. It
was suggested at the last Old Boys’ meeting
that the really interesting way in which
Young used to teach them political economy,
and other practical matters which a school-
master commonly neglects, is responsible for
the big share they have since taken —for a
comparatively small body—in public affairs
in S.A. Among the best known of them
still living are Herbert Phillips, Peter Wood,
Scammell, Bullock, Harry Bickford and Dr.
•T. C. Verco. Of the dead the greatest was
C. 0. Kingston.
THE DANGER.
R ECRUIT: "Well, doctor, if you can't pass

think I was rejected' on account of a

Wade's followers in the N.S.W. Assembly
dul a. mean thing when, in pursuit of their
anti-German campaign, they alleged that
Conrad von Hagen had been interned as an
enemy subject, and made a fuss about the
enlistment of one of his sons under the name
of Hagen. Von Hagen’s father was a British
subject 70 years ago, before the German
States were Prussianised, and before his
native State of Hesse had been conquered by
the Prussians; and two elder brothers served
in the British army and navy. Jack von
Hagen was in the Light Horse before the
war started, and dropped the “von,” with the
approval of the military authorities when
lie enlisted. Old-man von Hagen lias been
in the N.S.W. Labor movement ever since
there was one, and at one time opposed
Wade; and the assault bears, therefore, a
nasty flavor.
S. Aus. ex-Judge James Penn Boueaut
has his S4th birthday on October 29. The
veteran, who was thrice Premier of S. Aus.
and nearly 27 years Judge, retired from the
Supreme Court Bench in February, 1905,
with faculties unimpaired and with a keen
interest in life’s affairs. But he is now very
feeble and lias almost passed out of the
general public’s life.
Brewarrina (N.S.W.) rectory, which is
under the control of the Bush Brotherhood
has supplied two recruities —Private Martin
Andrews and Private Wilfred Hartridge.
The former was vice-principal of tin-
Brotherhood, and hoped to obtain a nomina-
tion as chaplain. So did the then principal,
the Rev. E. C. Kempe. They were both good
sports and line men ; but their views were too
“high” to be acceptable to the nominators
m Sydney. Kempe therefore returned to
Emil and, where he expected to have better
luck. Andrews joined the A.M.C.; Hart-
ridge, who was a lay brother, reading for the
priesthood, went with the 3rd Battalion.
Lately he was killed; and now his friend
Douglas Jackson, also a Bush Brother from
Bom ke, has gone to see if he can find out
who did it.
One of Victoria’s earliest squatters joined
the big muster in Ireland the other day—
Edmund McNeill, of Craigdunn, County An-
trim, Ireland. Long Ned. as they called
him. came to the Port Phillip Settlement in
the first ’4o’s and took up stations —Glen-
mona, among others. Rolf Boldrewood has
embalmed him in his “Memories of Old Mel-
bourne,” where he describes how he rode his
bay horse, Thur’mpogue in the Woodlands
Steeplechase, near Melbourne, and fell.
McNeill was one of a party that explored the
new country north of the Pyrenees. Re-
turning to Ireland, he became Deputy-Lieu-
tenant of the county. His only son, Ronald,
M.P., is the assistant-editor of the Ency-
clopedia Britannica, and was formerly
assistant-editor of St. James’s Gazette.
Died, a victim to meningitis, at Hobart,
Major Hugh Hunt, another good example
of the dominie turned soldier. He was a
graduate of Sydney Uni., who, after a spell
at school-teaching in N. S. Wales, moved
West, and in 1906 joined the Defence forces,
lie was second in command in Perth for
some time, and was shifted last year to a
similar position in Tasmania, where he was
much more of a driving force than the State
Commandant, his nominal boss. He was
only 45.
Dead, Paddy Lyons, who was for many
years confidential detective to N. S. Wales
Chief Secretary’s department. One of his
jobs was to discreetly ascertain the boim
tides of inconvenient persons who sought to
“bite” the State Excellency’s ear. It used
to be fairly common for stranded dukes and
belted earls to “tap” the Governor for succor
on the ground that they were long-lost
cousins of his or his wife’s family, and it
was Lyons’s business to push them through
the window or keep them quiet with as little
outlay as possible, and without publicity.
Later, he was appointed an inspector and
protector of derelict children. When he was
sent for his time-sheet showed 84.
Alexander Deeper, warden of Trinity Col-
h'go, Melbourne, has just completed his
fortieth year in office. Deeper came to
Australia in 1875 as a newly-hedged gradu-
ate of Trinity College, Dublin, and before
receiving his present appointment put in a
few months as a master at Sydney Grammar
under A. B. Weigall, head there for half a
century. While in Sydney, Deeper mar-
ried the eldest daughter of George Wigram
Allen, an old-time solicitor and politician in
the Ma State. Though he is himself a lay-
man, under his rule Trinity has produced six
Anglican bishops—a record which leaves all
similar institutions in Australasia out of
sight.
Crossed over the other day, the widow of
the late Rev. W. B. Boyce, of Sydney, presi-
dent of the first Australian Methodist Con-
ference. Her father was George Allen,
M.L.C., a pillar of the church from his arri-
val at Port Jackson in 1816. In ’23 he
married the daughter of Thomas Bowden,
who was sent out by the English Govern-
ment as a schoolmaster in 1811. Bowden
was a Methody, and soon gathered the few
other Methodies there were then in the
colony, and sent off an application for a
Parson with that brand on his collar. This
resulted in the arrival of Samuel Leigh in
’l5.
THE SHIRKER'S HORRORS.
The shirker has drunk success to the boys at
the Front, for so long that he is begin-
ning to sec snakes!
Jim _ Ward ley. president of the Victorian
Operative Bakers' Society, who at Melbourne
the other day had what is alleged to have
been his 100th birthday, reckons he is the
oldest living trades unionist. He says he
was born in Devonshire a few months after
Waterloo. Anyway, he came to Australia in
1852, and has always been a good unionist.
A fine young Queenslander, Private Ray-
mond F. Shirley, of B company, 9th Bat-
talion, 3rd Brigade, Ist Division A.1.F., is
missing. The first news his relatives had of
him alter he landed at Gallipoli was that
he had been wounded; that was in May or
June. Since then he has been reported
wounded on April 25, but where he has got
to is a mystery. All attempts to locate him
have so far failed. Does any returned
soldier know anything of him, and, if so.
will he tell Dr. John Shirley, Bowen Hills.
Brisbane?

Leave of absence has been granted to two
more Ma State legislators in khaki —Colonel
Onslow and Major Nicholson, who are on
service. And some of the same thing will
be granted, shortly, to Fern and Chaffey.
who have both enlisted. There arc several
other legislators who arc likely-looking re-
cruits, but have yet made no respouse to the
call, and their position in the N.S.W. Par-
liament, where the recruiting enthusiasm is
strong, isn't happy. At a little send-off to
one of the recent political recruits a Parlia-
mentarian of florid speech urged the guest
to “Be pattern now to men of grosser blood.
And teach them how to war.” It made one
eligible stay-at-home M.L.A. wince. He re-
membered (and so did others) that his father
was a grocer.
Thomas Cawthron, who let go his moorings
at Nelson (M.F.) the other day, first struck
the sleepy place in ’4B, and later on started
a shipping business. He was agent for all
the shipping concerns trading to Nelson
when it was about the most important p rt
in Loan Land, and being also a shipping eon-
tractor he spent most of his time
going round to the bank with rash.
Having accumulated largely, he got
busy spending. lie handed the
Nelson citizens Cawthron Park, a
beautiful forest reserve of 2500
acres close to the town; built a
fine flight of stone steps up Church
Hill, at a cost of £1800; gave the
local School of Music £2500 to get
it out of the grip of Financial Diffi-
culty, Esq,, and followed this up
with an organ that made him £lBOO
poorer. But the gift that made
him really famous in lOUland was
a cheque for £15,000 that he casu-
ally handed the Hospital Board to
go towards building a new repair-
ing shop. Maoriland subsidises
free-will gifts of this kind to the
tune of 255. in the £ ; and the Min-
ister of Finance was very hard up
at the moment. Cawthron at the
time of his death was planning to
hand out more cash, and was talk-
ing of planting a solar physics ob-
servatory at Nelson at a cost of £30,000. The
will dealt with a matter of a quarter of
a million, much of which goes to education
and science. Croesus was 82.
Two of the patriarchs of the Sydney
agency business came to the final audit of
accounts within a week —A. W. S. Gregg and
Josiah Mullens, who had been chasing the
com. within a hundred yards or so of one
another for half a century. Aleck Gregg,
senior managing-director of Richardson and
Wrench, commenced as office lad with the
long-deceased R. P. Richardson, one of the
earliest of real-estate salesmen; and Josiah
Mullens was a stockbroker when there was
no Stock Exchange and the only other man
in the business in Sydney ivas the present
Justice Barton’s father. Gregg stuck to it
to the end : the other retired nearly 20 years
ago, and filled in time learning things about
ancient Egypt.
There has been a lot of trouble in the
Punjaub, India, but a man by the name of
Michael O’Dwyer has made things all right.
This Sir M. O’Dwyer, the Lieut.-Governor.
is brother of Father J. O’Dwyer, who is head
of the R.C. College at Ivew. near Melbourne.
Seventy years ago Henry Flardinge became
Gov.-General of India, and now another
Ilardinge, who is Viceroy, has been praising
Governor O’Dwyer for the way he has man-
aged the cleverly-engineered difficulty. An-
other O’Dwyer brother is a professor at St.
Patrick’s College, East Melbourne.
PLEASE DON’T FORGET
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T" post that letter to the lad you knew
la happier times along' the daily track;
lie's taken on a job that’s*stern and new,
From which there is no rest or turning back;
Orop him a line to say his country needs
Jlore of his sort, brimful of gallant deeds!
And don’t forget to spare his nibs a smoke —•
the chaps who’re back tell how a timely whiff
A\ ill turn the closest “shave” into a joke,
And stiffen a fighter’s pecker in a jiff.
It's up to us (that’s me and likewise you!)
To spare our fighting lads a puff or two!
And don’t forget that life is short, and where
Our boys arc settling things with Kaiser Bill
They're up against it, and, what’s more, that there,
When shells are shrieking, life is shorter still;
So don’t forget (don’t merely drop a tear!)
To make it Christmas for them all the year!

All things considered, the profits for the
past half-year have not fallen away as much
as might reasonably have been expected. Had
there been no war at all Victoria’s unusually
severe drought might well have pulled the
figures down from the recent average of
about £22,000 to £17,255. Drought not only
cut into the internal exchange business, but
also seriously affected foreign transactions:
a glimpse of that,is given in the item “Bills
and remittances in transit and payable in
London,” which has diminished from an
average of £425,000 or so to £290,974. The
drought being well over so far as Victoria
is concerned, there is good reason to expect
improved figures for the coming year. Not
that the figures of the few terms prior to
the war will necessarily be at once repeated.
For one thing, taxation will cut banks’
profits as well as other people’s, though the
bulk of the burden will be carried by indi-
vidual shareholders. Further, partly from
patriotic motives, the bank has put a good
deal of money into public securities. Twelve
months ago these assets stood at £69,955.
Since then no less than £321,700 has been
invested that way ; and though a good deal
of this money, when the Government has
spent it, may come back to the bank in
deposits, the investment, while giving extra
solidity, is likely to steady the earnings for
a while. Still even on last half-year’s figures
there was a good, wide margin between
profits made and profits divided, so the 8 p.c.
is not in danger. The Avar rather makes
this difference: in vieiv of income tax the
8 p.c. will not be worth to the shareholder
what it Avas a Avhile ago.
The position of the Royal at September
30 Avas unusually strong. This table shows
the principal features: —
Sept.,’l4. Sept.,’ls.

tion .. .. £1,014 £953 Dec. £6l

Liquid assets thus represent just upon 50
p.c. of all liabilities. There is £1,358,316
of debt not represented by liquid assets, and
against that £1,358,316 there are advances
£1,840,142 and bank premises £78,820 —a
margin of well over half a million to pro-
tect a debt of £1,358,316; and that of course
is exclusive of the margin required by the
bank itself when making an advance. The
security is excellent; and it isn’t astonishing
that, even in the past dry year, the Royal
has been favored by the public with in-
creased deposits to the extent of £217,368.

The profits for 1904-5 just beat those for
the past year; but since 1905 none have
been so good. Two things havm no doubt
been responsible for this co. doing ivell at
a time when so many others have done badly.
First, the co.’s fine new office buildings in
O’Connell-street were lately finished, and
the co. began to get revenue from a capital
expenditure which in the previous couple of
years had earned nothing at all. In the
second place the co., while it may have suf-
fered a little from shrunken incomes from
country properties, is likely to have bene-
fited much more from the higher interest
available for loans on mortgage and deposits.
A trustee co. gets its living from four
sources —(1) interest on its own funds; (2)
commission on the annual earnings of trust
estates; (3) agency commissions; (4) a
single commission on the capital value of
every trust estate. The bulk of the earn-
ings of the average trustee co. consists of
either interest on its own funds or commis-
sions on interest earned by trust funds; and
since interest is going to be higher instead
of lower in the stressful times ahead of us,
the earnings of these trustee cos. are not
likely to suffer. The capital value of trust
estates will no doubt generally shrink ; but
if anything is lost in corpus commission in
that way. it is likely to be more than made
up the other way.
Such times of great uncertainty as the
present are bound to suggest to a whole lot
of people that the only reasonably safe trus-
tee to appoint is one that can’t go away and
be killed. In times of peace you strike quite
enough reasons for pinning your faith only to
an executor who has put up a money guaran-
tee that he will be there when you want him.
Times of war merely add to those reasons.
The guarantee in the case of the Sydney
Permanent consists of a paid capital of
£50,000, reserves of £40,909 and callable
capital of £950,000; and, though the co.
does not guarantee either the beneficiary’s
principal or his interest, it does hold itself
responsible for any wrongful handling of
trust funds. Further, not only the right
but the most judicious handling of trust
funds is the life-job of trustee cos.’ officers.
They are experts in that, just as the pri-
vate trustee is liable to be an expert in
blaeksmithing or fellmongering or drawing
briefs.
The co.’s position is further shown in this
table:—
1905. 1910. 1914. 1915.
Capital ..£25,000 £25,000 £50.000 £50,000
Liabilities . . 23 42 6,720 16,679

Liabilities have run up lately. This no
doubt is owing to the big and now ended out-
lay on the city office property, and is there-
fore not of serious consequence. The trust
estates in hand (including agencies) amount
to £4,221,154, as against £2,533,515 ten
years and £3,029,970 five years ago—
figures which suggest that the co.’s business
is growing solidly. State competition may
compel one concession after another; but
the figures just quoted give shareholders
every reason to expect that, even so, they are
going to do very well.

powder. On what is referred"fo above as
“the great cleaning up,” there was a big
deficit; but that was turned into reserves
by the simple process of writing off capital.
One-half of it disappeared. For the purpose
of ascertaining how much capital was really
intact the assets were of course appraised.
The “profits on sales” seem to show that
they, were undervalued. But it isn’t safe to
say it and leave it at that. Any barrowman
can tell you that you mustn’t judge a bagful
of bananas by the first two you take out.
And, even if properties are valued on the
one basis, you can’t be sure that you will
get in 1916 what you were able to get in
1913; so that the fact that the 1913 sales
showed a profit mustn’t be regarded as proof
that the whole lot are going to show a pro-
fit. Anyway, profits from that source
suffered a violent sltlinp in the past year;
and as the next table shows there is no great
quantity of old written-down assets left from
which to make future profits even if the mar-
ket kept good :

Business had been growing very slowly up
to 1913; then it got brisker, but slackened
down again last year. The way of the building
society nowadays is not easy. For one thing
the State, through the Savings Bank, offers
terms which make the outlook pretty blue
for any concern that wants to make divi-
dends out of the same kind of business. Even
allowing that they own substantial reserves,
the shareholders are likely to have difficulty
in keeping their 8 p.c. dividend going unless
they can extend their borrowing connection ;
and that means taking more deposits to
finance, the loans; and that again means
more risks. But that of course is what you
get more than 34 or 4 p.c. for —for taking
risks. The whole art of money-lending is to
get paid for taking them when they are risks
only in name.

Table A provides for funeral benefits and
medical attendance and medicine. The
funeral benefits are £3O on the death of a
member. £l2 10s. to a member on the death
of his wife, and from £5 to £3 to pay the
undertaker when a child has to be buried.
The medical attendance and medicine is for
the whole family from one month to 16 years
old. And for the lot a member pays, accord-
ing to his age, from Is. 2d. to 2s. 3d. a week.
There are a number of other tables —heaps
of them; almost as many as the big A.M.P.
has: tables for widows and widowers; tables
for the benefits above-mentioned with sick
benefits added; even Ordinary insurance.
So far as the sick and funeral funds are
concerned the insurer could probably do bet-
ter with one of the best friendly societies.
The latter are more cheaply run and they
don’t have to pay dividends. There was a
time when they were so badly run that they
couldn’t pay the promised benefits either;
but nowadays they are under proper actu-
arial supervision. Yet, even so, this co. does
a good work. It certainly isn’t at all likely
to attract anybody away from the more de-
sirable friendly society; on the other hand,
it is likely to get hold of a good many people
who wouldn’t “keep good” with a lodge, be-
cause the lodge wouldn’t chase them, as this
society does, for their contributions. And
the people it thus gets hold of are the very
people who are in most urgent need of being
chased.
For the shareholders there are nice pick-
ings in it, though it is scarcely likely to he
permitted to ever become a big money-mak-
ing concern. As a general thing a Of p.c.
dividend is paid; and for 1914 there was an
extra 10 p.c., the consulting actuary having
reported that the reserves could spare that
much. As the rable shows, the present re-
serves are £43,877, and E. M. Moors, a capable
man, is to make a valuation this year
and say whether that is enough, or
more than enough, towards meeting
policy obligations. The capital is £lO,OOO.
The funds are in mortgages £35,719, Govern-
ment securities £8729, cash £4302. and loans
on policies £4671. From the very nature of
the bulk of the policies the expenses are
heavy. This table shows what the outgoings,
apart from claims, amount to in proportion
to the total income:—

All things considered, the business appears
to be run very economically.

The Western • Australian Insurance

"Subject to remarks hereunder.
You can’t tell the profit made by an insur-
ance office in the first year of its life by
deducting the actual outgoings from the
actual incomings, because a great part of
the revenue has been in. respect of policies
which have anything up to 12 months to run.
You could only ascertain the actual profits
at the end of the first year by finding out
what yon v* uld have to pay some other
concern lo taxe over your unexpired risks.
And in order to ascertain what your accu-
mulated profits are at the end of any subse-
quent 12 months you must similarly deduct
whatever the re-insurance of unexpired cur-
rent risks would cost. This reinsurance
reserve really consists of unearned pre-
miums.
As a co. increases its premium income
(and therefore its unexpired risks) it must,
in order to show its true position, increase
its reinsurance reserve. In the case of this
co.. for instance, there has been during the
past year a huge increase of business. The
net premiums have gone from £3519 to
£52,061. That is to say, in a general way,
new policies bringing in £45,542 of pre-
miums have come in during the year. Now
the chances are that one-half of those new
policies for a year have been taken during
the past six months, and, as an average,
the new policies have half a year to run.
Which means that thus far only one-half of
the net premium has yet been really earned.
The general idea among insurance men is
that 40 p.c. of the year’s premiums ought
to he put by to cover unexpired risks. On
that rule £19,416 should be put by to re-
insure last year’s new risks here; and on
that basis, instead of £20,512, as the table
shows, there would be an actual profit of
only £1096. Certainly one can’t say for
certain. Very much depends upon when the
bulk of the new insurance for last' year was
taken. If, for instance, it was taken in a
lump early in the year, then there would be
little risk now unexpired. But in a case of
this sort one can only assume average con-
ditions ; and that has been done in the fore-
going.
Further details (still subject to the
remarks as to unearned premiums) are
shown hereunder: —

who insure with a young office —or for that
matter, any office—are entitled to more pre-
cise information than that about the co.’s
means.

The Langridge is almost the only Melbourne
building society which escaped reconstruc-
tion after the bank smash of 21 years ago.
Of course it didn’t escape with a whole
skin, but it pulled through and paid every-
body 20s. in the £ by merely foregoing divs. :
for it was not till 1912 that shareholders
got anything beyond 5 p.e. For the past
year it pays 8 p.e. and seems to be able to
do it comfortably, thanks to the earn-
ings of the reserves which have been
steadily accumulated. So far as figures
tell, the position is exceptionally good:
for the 8 p.e. is made without risking any
outsiders’ money to speak of. This shows
various details: —
Deposits, Fore- Other
etc. Advances. closures. Assets.
1905 .. £12,229 £59,172 £54,245 £3678
1910 . . 21,351 96,143 24,997 Nil
1911 .. 27,944 107,052 9,675 Nil
1912 .. 33,234 119,905 4,158 Nil
1913 .. 24,370 111,239 4,016 3091
1914 .. 26,826 115,745 3,996 Nil
1915 . . 21,402 103,022 3,962 9139
The depositors and other creditors are there-
fore in this happy position: apart from the
liabilities not covered by cash, the co. owes
only £12,263, and it: has very nearly nine
times that much in mortgages, etc.
And the mortgages are all or nearly all
doubtless of the building-society pattern :
that is to say, the principal is gradually
reduced by repayments. From the sudden
collapse of a real property boom no concern
is likely to. suffer more seriously than a
building society. Where the ordinary mort-
gagee would not advance beyond 55 or GO p.e.
of a. valuation, a building society fre-
quently advances 80 p.e. on the theory that
if anything bad was going to happen inside
12 months or so one ought to be able to
foresee it, and that in 12 months’ time the
SO p.e. advance will be down to 70 p.e. or
so, and thereafter the risk will go on de-
creasing, whereas it doesn’t decrease at
all in the case of a fixed mortgage. Even
if property values should suffer very seri-
ously, as they are quite likely to, this and
other prudently-managed building societies
should lose little' or nothing. On the
advances made upon the excessive valua-
tions of two or three or more years ago
large repayments have now been made; and
the newest advances, which are always the
dangerous ones, have (unless the manage-
ment has suddenly gone mad) been made on
a most conservative basis, with a full know-
ledge of the uncertainties ahead. From
this point of view the shrinkage of
“advances,” as shown in the second table,
is very satisfactory.

Heaven and the directors only know why
they do it. The deposits have run up to
£104,490, and the office holds £59,480 in cash
—including, of course, fixed deposits with
banks that really are banks. Five years
ago the deposits were £47,303, and the cash
£7966. The co. has since then taken another
£57,187 of deposits and has had no better
use for the money than to put £51,514
on deposit. How it can be profitable for this
co. to take deposits and pass them on, pre-
sumably to banks, The Bulletin doesn’t
profess to know. Under ordinary circum-
stances one would guess that the regular
banks could borrow direct from the public at
a lower rate. Deducting the cash in hand
from the liabilities, the co. owes £48,606, and
against that there are assets, mostly loans
and balances owing (£89,370) and properties
(£12,323), totalling £104,974. The security
is therefore all right. Past history sug-
gests, however, that, so far as shareholders
are concerned, the business isn’t free from
peril. Present paid share capital is £27,SGS ;
20 years ago it was just on to £150,000. At
that time property alleged to be worth
£102,656 was in the co.’s hands and loans and
balances (mostly loans) amounted to another
£76,000. Terrific depreciation of property
values_ followed ; and corresponding amounts
of capital had to be written off as lost.
The question now is to what extent can
history repeat itself. Apart from some
national catastrophe which can be put out of
the question, there appear to be no circum-
stances at all likely to produce any great

is not reliable, no matter where it is made. But he
has not. to look to the outside world for a good

ALUMINOFKIIIIIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.*

ROYDS, LTD., Investors’Advisers, Challis House, Sydney
sudden slump. That removes the worst fea-
ture of the Australian position as it was in
the early ’9o’s, when the collapse was caused
by the rapid withdrawal of foreign deposit
money. But, if it is not likely that there
will be a sudden severe shrinkage, it is
likely that there will be a gradual severe
shrinkage. Values would tend downward
in any case; and the chances are that
the movement will be hurried on by
legislation, since there is a growing con-
viction that high land values are a
national curse. Such concerns as this
will back their own judgment; but it seems
to this paper that they are liable to make
pretty heavy losses in the next seven years
unless they are very conservatively managed.
* * * *
Can’t somebody who has some influence
with Hughes, the biggest man in Australian
politics, persuade him to leave off talking
about metals—-one of the few things he
knows less than nothing at all about? Speak-
ing in Sydney the other day he announced
that arrangements had been made in Britain
to treat all Australia’s zinc. Of course,
metal men knew it was nonsense and scoffed.
Hughes knows it now, too; his latest remark
on the subject is that “negotiations are pro-
ceeding which, it is hoped, will enable a
large proportion of the zinc concentrates of
the Commonwealth to be distilled in Great
Britain.” As a matter of fact, the negotia-
tions so far have been an utter failure, and
they will continue to be a failure until the
Government agrees to support the industry
with a heavy bonus or a guarantee of in-
terest. “After 14 months of war we are
able to say that the Australian metal indus-
try is completely free from the grip of Ger-
many,” Hughes says ; and he goes on talk-
ing of the virtues of the practically useless
Metals Exchange. To which The Bulletin
merely replies that, if the war stopped to-
morrow’, and war prices for zinc stopped with
it, Australia, even if it had a dozen Metal
Exchanges, would not be able to find a Brit-
ish market for one-twentieth of its output of
concentrates. That is the situation. Now
how is Hughes going to get Australia out of
it?
* * . * *
A rumor was current recently that the
surveyors on the railway-duplication works
near Sodwalls (N.S.W.) had discovered
something or other of great value —alleged
to be enough to pay for the cutting. Though
reported to be molybdenite, details seemed
to point rather to seheelite. As the works
are in granite country, not so far from the
much-advertised Yetholme, it should be quite
possible to stumble on a find in a railway cut-
ting. But as there has been no excitement
in the way of pegging out adjoining land, it
seems safe to conclude that posterity has as
good a chance of paying for that cutting as
any other.
“Jaques”: The Newcastle coal trade is
going to receive a bump if the action of a
nitrate co. on the West Coast of South
America is not misleading. This show
recently bought 1000 tons of Chilian coal and
reckons it is as good as any imported. The
price was 355. per ton, whereas the imported
costs about 465.

Chaffers (W.A.) still has a chance of
being raised from the dead. 'The directors
have cabled their willingness to take over
the debenture liability and pay preferential
claims amounting to £7OOO, provided the
unsecured creditors (£21,000) let the Board
have a first mortgage for their outlay and
accept a second charge in settlement of their
accounts. A meeting of those interested
has been convened.
* * * *
The Augusta (W.A.) has proved to be the
delusion The Bulletin predicted. After
years of fiddling and the absorption of a
large sum in plant, underground rooting and
directors’ visits, the co. now finds itself
weighted down with liabilities. Still the
Board has hopes ; and despite the fact that
everything has, according to the new man-
ager, “been run to a standstill,” the directors
look forward to “steady crushings with pay-
able results.” They might almost as well
look forward to an exchange of jobs by
Woodrow Wilson and the Kaiser.

Cbmmodore (W.A.) hopes to have its
slimes plant going in November, and after
that a div. will not be far off. There is
estimated to be a few thousands clear profit
in the dump, and development in progress
is expected to somewhat improve the posi-
tion. At 35., shares are alleged to be cheap;
but although the market has at times risen
a shilling or two above that, it has soon
fallen back exhausted. There is no depend-
ing on it.
