From Westonia: “Good stone,” “nice
stone,” “fair stone,” “payable stone,” and
other such vague expressions are creeping
into some official mining reports from this
field. This may do in Victoria, but it won’t
be tolerated for very long over here. The
Westralian practice is to express widths in
feet and inches, and values in fine gold or
shillings. Thus the exact size and value of
the stone or ore can be determined right
away. The local system of sampling, assay-
ing and mine working badly needs improve-
ment in the case of some shows. Frequently
only the best assays are published and made
to represent the value of the whole, and
more than one “trial” crushing has been
made up of only the richest ore. Why V
Well, the directors want to make things
look as bright as possible, and as the man-
ager gets only a few pounds a week and has
a wife and family to support he cannot
afford to fall out with them.
A graph of the Empress market resembles
that of the Lode Hill, hard though it may be
to say it. Lode Hill went sky-rocketing
because brother Jack Nortliey took it on.
Empress soared because Edna May Hartrick
and Fred Tricks approved of it. Somehow,
too, a generally wise lot in the West froze
on to the little stranger. A 9oz. prospect
was reported ; but when this prospect materi-
alised into a lOdwt. yield, there was a rush
for prompt wires. Whether Empress is
worth averaging depends on what Hartrick
has! in reserve —mostly on what his winzes
disclose. At the moment, the Imperial cos-
tume is far from being royal purple.
“Oof Bird”: I will try to enlighten
“Rigolo” <B. 7./10/T5) in a few words.
There is a State Mining Engineer in West-
ralia, and he and his staff are supposed to
look into the things “Rigolo” complains of,
but their hands are tied. There is not, and
never has been, any power under the Mines
Act to order an inspection with a view to
safeguarding the interests of mining inves-
tors in the State. The object of the Act is
to ensure the safety of persons employed.
The duty of the State geologists is, as
“Rigolo” says, to “get out in the field, sur-
vey the rocks, and make plenty of plans;”
and, as I said before, they are doing this
work uncommonly well. They endeavor to
protect the public against boomsters who
reckon they have found oil and other wealth
in country where geological conditions are
dead against the possibility of any such
discovery.
* * * *
Edna May Deep Levels (W.A.) persists
in its determination to have a mill, and has
acquired the old 10-head battery on the
grave of the Greenmount. Greenmount is
five miles south of Southern Cross, and was
once managed by Stuckey, who now runs the
Deeps. What the mill is going to do on
the Deeps until the Edna May shoot is
reached and opened up goodness only knows.
Stuckey reports he has located a couple of
quartz veins in the old workings, but with-
out reserves to work on the installation of
such a plant is unwarranted, and is bound
to lead to the suspicion that the State of the
market is weighing more with the controllers
than the state of the mine.
* *• * *
A Bulletin man writes: The water at
the No. 4 level of the Edna May is now very
heavy, and has to be seen to be realised.
Seven pipes witli valves attached have been
inserted in the face of the crosscut, and when
one of the valves is opened the water shoots
out as it does from a city street-hydrant, and
seems just about as inexhaustible. The new
pumping plant may, and most likely will,
cope with the flow, but there is no dead
certainty about it. What the Deeps and
Central will do if the Edna May stops shaft-
sinking and the flow comes their way is
something for shareholders to sit down and
have a hard think about.
* * * *
The Etheridge, an offshoot from the Chil-
lagoe, is thus far as complete a failure as
the parent. In fact, the whole family are
undesirables. Since the Chillagoe furnaces
went cold nothing had been done until the
other day, when work was started again at
the Queenslander, and the concentrates are
coming south. The small loss made last
year brings the total to £6344, or £12,027
if you include the money spent on develop-
ment, and mostly without result. Reserves
in the four mines run to about 17,000 tons,
worth from 15 to 21 dwt., all more or less
intractable. The co. has no money left, and
the profits from the Queenslander are relied
upon to see the co. through for the next
couple of years. After that ?
* * * *
In the first nine months of this year
Charters Towers produced gold and other
mineral worth £191,605. Dividends totalled
only £13,620. The stone averaged over £4
per ton, but costs are high, and so heavily
capitalised are some of the old mines that it
takes from £5OOO to £6OOO to hand out a
sixpenny. Such conditions deaden the market.
For instance, Clark’s has opened up a nar-
row streak of stone worth about £lO per ton,
but there are 180,000 shares to spread the
streak over.
* * *
Four sixpenny divs. came out of the
Duke and Main Leads Consols, Mary-
borough (Vie.), last term. These raised the
total to £66,250. or 265, 6d. per share, on a
capital of £61,458. It has taken gold and
slimes worth £365,000 to provide these
divs. Other outlay has been as follows: —
Plant and buildings.. . . £21,854
Wages 228,256
Fuel and stores . . . . 84,108
Head office costs . . . . 7,685
Royalty .. .. . . 7,016
Taxes and charges . . . . 5,106
If 50 p.e. were added to wages (and that’s
what the miners are now asking) it would
bring the total up to £342,000, so that only
£23,000 would be available out of the pro-
ceeds of the gold for other expenses. It is
impossible. The average gold value per
fathom of wash for the half-year was £3
3s Sd. Mining costs £2 ss. lOd.; and
as this is a comfortable mine to work, that
represents the dead-limit of cheapness for
Victorian deep-lead work. In the northern
end of the claim the drives have got out of
the scoured area, and a run of gold 250 ft.
wide is being opened out. The wash is
draining well, and as the co. is now 1300 ft.
ahead of the panelling line, the mine posi-
tion has improved a lot of late. And it
would improve a lot more if good ground
was opened in the western end. Here
Glover talks of “unseen possibilities.” as if
deep lead mining anywhere was ever any-
thing else.
Northern Hope, Beaufort (Vic.), sent out
a cheerless half-yearly report, but the declar-
ation of a dividend has since restored the
balance. In all, about £38,000 of gold has
been scratched out of the Hope gutter, and
as the wash was rich £6OOO has been avail-
able in divs. Of gold Avon about 15s. out of
every ounce has found its Avay into the
pocket of the shareholder, and there is £2OOO
cash in hand. The programme of the direc-
tors issued on the 12th inst. is “to conserve
profits” until the course of the run of rich
ground found in the north-west level off No. 2
rise is determined. As the Board iioav pro-
duces another div., does that mean that the
rich ground has been picked up? A second
string would be added to the co.’s bow by
hustling work towards the All Nations Ex-
tended tributary. This gave gold in its
upper section, but waits to be tapped in the
lower ground by Bryant and his Ballarat
friends.
* *■ * *
New Bendigo runs its neighbor New Chum
Goldfields close in the matter of cheap costs.
Most of its gold comes from about 1000 ft.,
and Avlien once the water was turned on
after the drought it soon swept the debit
balance into the sluice boxes. Houghed out,
5632 tons yielded a shade under 20s. per
ton. This gave a neat profit, as despite
trucking stone 600 ft. from the shaft, hauling
1000 ft. and double-handling a portion of the
quartz, costfe ‘ were kept at about 10s. per
ton. All goes to show that Bendigo has not
lost its deftness in treating stone cheaply.
The co. at 913 ft. has got to the Goldfields
boundary, but is bringing along a stope to
join up with the Nell Gwynne workings.
What is more to the point is that the vvest
leg is to be tried. Next door the stone on
this leg kept the pot boiling fairly Avell. If
nothing happens there, North Bendigo will
want the aurora borealis to brighten it up
after the present payable shoots are Avorked
out.
* * * *
From Pine Creek (N.T.) :
A new gold reef has been discovered near Brock’s
Creek, and close to the tin-show recently found. The
fact that the owners declined a Government subsidy
to Work it speaks well for its prospects. Surface
lots give from 12dwt. to 4oz. per ton, and the reef
can be traced for some hundreds of feet. Several
abandoned shows have recently been taken up, with
good prospects of success, and it looks as if mining
here is to take a new lease of life.
* * * *
They’ve dropped on to something promising
in the way of goldfields in the Nelson dis-
trict (M.L.). A number of men are on the
ground, which carries coarse gold, easily
saved. A few nuggets have been found, the
largest so far weighing Soz. The best yield
from a claim to date is £lOOO.
* * * *
Blythe River Iron Mines, Ltd., still hangs
on to the big Tas. deposit from which nearly
20 years ago William Jamieson had visions
of feeding works to be built on the Parra-
matta River. That scheme failed, and the
little co. has simply hung on since. Lately,
the B. 11. Prop., having put up works to use
ore from S. Aus., Willie Jamieson has gone
off the Blythe River Board, and W. S. Rob-
inson has taken his place. The co. has only
a few hundreds left and it has to keep the
labor conditions complied with ; so a recon-
struction is ahead. Of course it will be
easy; for the Blythe River is a Fat man’s
gamble.
* * * *
Ivalgurli (W.A.) has, in view of dwin-
dling ore reserves, cut its quarterly div. to
2s. 6d., or ,Is. 6d. less than it has regularly
paid since the beginning of 1911. The co.
doesn’t seem at all inclined to look out for a
neAV show, and will probably give up busi-
ness when the present one snuffs out.
* * # #
“Oof Bird”: Melbourne is taking on the
Fraser’s and Central group at Southern
Cross (W.A.), together Avith the machinery
erected on the Maori Lass, about a mile
west of Fraser’s. Frank Connor, M.L.C.
and cattle man, fathered the Maori Lass
about two years ago and was induced to
finance the erection of a mill when the
mine workings were only 15ft. deep.
Some well-intentioned friends tried to stop
him, but failed, and the price he paid for his
optimism was about £5OOO. The man who
mentions the Maori Lass to Frank Connor
these days runs the risk of injury. As for
Fraser’s and Central, the former Avas at one
time a good mine Avell managed by a man
Avho adopted a developmental policy rare in
those days, and unfortunately not too com-
mon noAv. 8., M. & Co. took an option over
and thoroughly sampled it about six years
ago, when the firm could have got the
capital for asking. Assays, particularly
those at depth, were so disappointing that
they turned the show down. Later a small
diamond drilling co. was formed to test the
country at depth. About six holes were
bored to a depth of 1000 ft., but with no
better results. The Central was a pocket
show at the best, and some of its pockets
were very acceptable to others besides share-
holders. Both mines have since been pig-
rooted by tributors and are now flooded.
Deeming’s cottage is not mentioned in the
prospectus, which is a wonder, for it has
possibilities almost equal to anything con-
tained in the alluring delusion. So much
for the “mines.” As for the Maori Lass
plant, it was second-hand 12 years ago. It
is now an out-of-date and weird contraption.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A GOOD REMEDY FOR
SALLOW COMPLEXIONS.
Everyone may have a clear and bright
complexion, free from furrows or wrinkles, if
they will only take a little daily trouble in
the following manner : Buy some Puritol wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply every
night, lap a little cold water on the face ten
times with the hands every morning', allow-
ing it to get thoroughly round the closed
eyes, and pinch the skin very gently across
all wrinkles. Puritol Wax Soap, which
leaves a fine film of wax on the skin, should
be used; this acts as a protective during the
day. This treatment arouses the dormant
nerves and blood vessels, and undoubtedly
accounts for many of the clear complexions
so much admired by those who do not yet
know the secret.* [3

Sydney, Octoiser _5. 1915.
My Dear .Muni, —
The Institute of Architects fathered the
ideal Home Exhibition which is open in the
Town Hall. The State Governor spoke the
usual preface, while the hammers suspended
action, amidst a smell of wet paint and
shavings. Monday is a bad day to start an
item of this kind. Though mayors and
aldermen may do a bit of carpentering in
their own back yards on the Sabbath, they
forbid the desecration of the Town ITali.
So the Ideal Homes were being hurriedly
dragged out of boxes when Monday morn
blew in.
The vestibule shrines the plans of the
dream-homes in which Mrs. Suburbs might
hold her first and fourth Thursdays. Near
the entrance to the main hall is a full-size
cot which has a large and handsome exterior
and rather scanty verandah accommodation.
It was having its turf lawns laid out while
the committee hung on the horizon to wait
for his Ex. The small models, however, are
much worse off in the matter of lungs. Here,
for instance, is an “ideal home” with only
a porch doormat for father to sleep-out on
in sultry weather. The Arts and Grafters
have a. good display of stuff—notably Mil-
dred Creed, Eirene Mort, Madeline King
and Mrs. Sansom. But tilings were so
unready on the opening day that housewives
were _ nol able to feast their eyes on the
electrical appliances that wash up without
sulking and do other unexplainable things.
The . organising secretary is Nicholas
Shields, with W. Aiming and Grace Bur-
rows helping him variously.
The Yaralla strawberry fete for a Bat-
talion Comforts Fund went off as briskly
as a damp cracker. The rain poured down
on the more or less draggled visitors as they
ran to catch the wherry at Fort Macquarie.
Then came clearing showers, a dead-low tide
and mudbanks. After the ferry had bobbed
and backed over this more or less solid
portion of Parramatta River and yawned
over the mangrove swamps, the voyage came
to an end in front of the Eaditii Walker
chalet.
Local society slopped over the "wet ground
to the house, or whizzed up by car. Then
it gathered in the usual dreary crowd to
wait for the official opening that never came.
Lady Helen walked round the grounds, with
Help Haskett-Smith in immaculate garb at
her elbow. Then his Ex., in khaki, with the
large Foxton at his heels, tramped up to the
portal. The lady of the house hurried out
of her bower to give him her hand and
toss a nod to the Help. Convalescent sol-
diers sat patiently with their feet on the
wet grass till the vice-regals passed on.
And still the crowd hung on to its bit of
gravel to wait for the opening. There was
nothing to open, as a matter of fact. They
realised this when hunger drove them, a
dejected herd, to the tea tents. Here pretty
waitresses fed the multitude on a modest
tariff. _ Tliea Stephen, a helping of the
Knox girls, young Macarthur Onslows and
other buds passed round the food atten-
tively to business. They bumped each other
in the usual amateur style, and I didn't hear
what one beauty said to another when she
got some cream on her neck.
The G.-G.’s staff is to be reinforced by
Aide Kerr-Pearse almost immediately. He
lias been in and out of the Help business
for years, and leaves the vice-regal Macart-
neys for the chieftain's bigger paysheet.
The last Strickland tour Outback gave
Lady Edeline the chance to hold a few Red
Cross gatherings amongst western sheep. She
formed a centre at Barren Junction. It
starts 30 strong, and hopes to bring all the
adjacent lady fleece-farmers into the pen
before Christmas.
The State Governor’s eldest treasure- had
the joy of digging out some opal stone at
Lightning Ridge on the recent tour. Besides
doing this bit of gougin’ (that’s the right
word for this sort of fossicking. I’m told),
some local lease-holders gave her a gem
they’d found before she came.
Lady Helen watched the Rose Hall con-
valescent home for soldiers blossom last week.
The morning was fine and warm, and the
air was thick with cameras. Voluntary-aid
nurses are allowed to wear the Red Cross
uniform, and they bustled to the fore to
have their appearance recorded in the pic-
turesque garb. With the Red Cross on a
dead white ground, the effect is striking.
The sun made faces wrinkle under the small
tight caps that are becoming only to young-
sters ; but the camera is sometimes kind.
New arrivals whose humble shoe-leather
crunched the gravel track were sharply
ordered to move on as the vice-regal car
snorted in the distance. Society was in
charge, and the herd got to heel forthwith.
Her Ex. was followed by vhe Sphinx-like
expression that Aide Haskett Smith brings
to these functions. Committee dames panted
up and tore her Ex. off to see the premises.
This ex-school for boys should make an ideal
rfest-kouse for nerve-shattered warriors.
Standing high above the city, its bright and
airy wards are the last word in cheery com-
fort. Mrs. Tony Hordern and Mrs. Laidley
kept an eye on the paperhanger and the
decorator; and Hanbury Davies bared his
brow to the morning sun to tell Lady Helen
all about it. Mayor Richards and Mrs. Jim
Ashton threw in a few side-lines of oratory,
and the thing was done.
Lady Doris Blackwood, who goes every-
where with Auntie Helen, is a very unassum-
ing Miss compared with a few of our own
belles, and steps out in substantial shoes
with the tread of a good golfer. When the
British aristocracy comes this way one
notices that the feminine part of it reserves
its splendor for evening wear, and gets about
o’ days in a coat and skirt and thick leather.
The male sprig, however, turns out wonder-
fully spatted about the feet, and is a strik-
ing example of the Right Way to wear a
silk hat. Since the war tore the dressy
young aides away the Sydney girl hasn’t
seen such millinery as Haskett-Smith’s
superb silk gear.
Major de Burgh Griffith, an Indian officer
who came ashore the other day in charge of
some of our wounded, is a brother of Arthur,
Minister of Education. The traveller has
been under his relative’s roof during his
stay.
Pretty Mrs. George Vi vers is blooming
again after a bout of ill-health. She is
emphasising the charm of the wide hat, and
appeared at a function the other day under
the drooping brim of burnt-straw millinery
of the rustic type.
Still another military change. This time
it is the appointment of Col. Ramaciotti,
o*’ the citizen forces, as Acting-Commandant
of N. S. Wales. The present man, Col.
Wallaek, takes a health trip to Egypt in
charge of a troopship. Ramaciotti, a soli-
citor in days of peace, used to be a partner
in J. C. Williamson’s ; and when he sold out
to Hugh Ward four years ago he was rich
enough to cultivate his military hobby dur-
ing a long stay in Europe.
News filters through that Miss Knatch-
bull-Hugessen, who was out here with Lady
Denman, is engaged to a brother of her
former hostess. She was a girl with cream-
and-roses complexion, you’ll remember, who
had a good time in the glad days when
Young Australia had not much to do but
enjoy itself in the sun.
Killed in action, somewhere in Flanders
recently, Captain R. W. Braithwaite, of the
Durham Light infantry. He was once a
helpful Aide to Governor Ranfurly, of M.L.
The swarthy London actress, Lilian Braith-
waite, who usually plays tragedy, is a sister.
Dick Braithwaite was much liked in M.L.,
specially in the sporting spaces.
The death of young Leslie Beauchamp,
only son of Harold Beauchamp, chairman of
the Bank of N.Z., will affect a large circle of
M.L. people, as well as the Redleaf Lassetters
of Sydney. Mrs. Lassetter, sen., was his
aunt. Mrs. “King Dick” Seddon was an
aunt, too- —a sister of Mrs. Beauchamp. The
young man was accidentally killed in France
while holding a commission in the Bth South
Lancashire Regiment—St. Helens, Lanca-
shire, is the place where Richard Seddon
first blinked at the sunlight. The Beau-
champ girl who wrote so charmingly about
a German Garden was one of this clan.
Dressed in their best clothes the Red
Cross dames waited impatiently for the arri-
val of Lady Helen the other afternoon.
Someone had given the nod too soon, and
the early arrivals at the George-street depot
passed the time thus: "Do you think she’ll
speak?” “Oh, surely they’d never get us
here if she wasn’t going to speak.” After
it had mastered its fears that a suddenly-dumb
Excellency might wave her hands in Dreadful
Silence over the Good Works, the company
readjusted its bangles and proceeded: “Do
you think they’ll give us tea?” “Oh, I don’t
think any but the executive and Lady Helen
will have refreshments.” Subdued wails:
"Oli. zurely they wouldn’t bring us here and
give us nothing! I hear them coming—
hus-s-sh !” Later, Mrs. .Tim Ashton gave the
word that there was plenty of fodder for
everybody, so peace flew its pennons.
Mrs. Johnstone, of Til pal station, on the
Queensland side, is in town. She’s doing
her bit for Allies’ Day by presenting a
locked casket trimmed with gems. In it
lurks a lucky charm. Tickets are selling-
fast for the raffle, and the result will be
mentioned on New Year’s Eve.
Government architect MeCrae is designing
the big stalls that will turn Martin Place
into an avenue of pavilions representing
every country on our side of the fight. Vene-
tian masts with flapping streamers and
pointers to index the way will be features
of the day. And illuminated tramcars are
going to procesh to the Cricket Ground
Now that the cobbler lias half-soled and
heeled old Government House, the State
Governor and his kindergarten move out of
Granbrook. The big meeting of the lied
Cross in November occurs in the Macquarie-
street residence. The Strickland lady comes
down from Moss Vale for the event, which
threatens to be full of ginger. A new execu-
tive is to be chosen, and the fight will be to

Melba, writing from Pasadena, in Cali-
fornia, after her final concert there, says
she had her photo taken in the Universal
World movie-city. As an extra thrill she
was “photographed with a tiger, and put
my hand on him—most gingerly. He did
growl!” Pavlova had just been there, and
had relieved the treasury of 100,000dol. as
a film star.
Artist B. B. Minns, home after 20 years
in Europe, has retained the typical Aus-
tralian exterior of the long lean man of
Outback. Under the brim of a shady
Panama, Minus looks as if he had just
blown in to the city from the tasseled fringe
of the Sunset. He chats of artist George
Coates as having “arrived” after the usual
struggle in England. Longstaff has moved
into a smaller studio while the war makes
the outlook blue for even the top-notchers.
Americans, who are great customers of por-
trait-painters, have mostly flitted off to sit
under Woodrow Wilson’s gamp till the
storm blows over, and duchesses are not
“sitting” just now. Minns confirms the
story of other Australian artists about Hol-
land. “The Dutch are clean in their per-
sons, but cling to their own household
arrangements. Everything from the baby to
the cow is huddled under one roof. By the
light of a table lamp you see the straw
sticking out of the barn above your head.
And if you find that the drainage soaks into
the well from which the water is pumped
for domestic use, you mustn't mind.”
From an artist friend in England :
These things should come under the head of Eng-
lish atrocities. They are committed by the numer-
ous swagger, sometimes alpinely exalted, dames who
visit the poor dear wounded, and talk to them, and
patronise them through the long, drawling hours, till
the poor dear wounded almost yell aloud in the
agony of boredom. I am not exaggerating. I have
the confidences of scores to the above effect; a dozen
friends at ambulance work corroborate the tale of
woe. “If the blighters only wouldn’t talk war!’’
wailed one Cornstalk in my ear the other day.
“But they will! They’ll talk nothing else. And
they talk as if they knew and I didn’t. And, God
o’ pity, I’ve had my heel shot off, and can’t run!”
Tommy Atkins stands it better; he’s more used to
the appalling superiority of the uppish classes.
One of our Australian artists, a rouseabout with the
Red Cross here, has a yarn of a fat dowager, who
had talked to a morc-or-less-riddled Victorian for
an hour, rising and saying with great satisfaction:
“Ah, poor follow, lie is better now.” So he was —he
had fainted.
The brilliant Elliot Smith, M.D., who is
attracting the world’s notice with his treat-
ment of mental patients, is a Cornstalk.
When the Science Congress came this way in
1914 he was with it and had a happy time
at Killara with father—a school-inspector
—mother and a couple of sisters. A brother.
Dr. S. A. Smith, lectures on anatomy in
Sydney Uni.
A former Manly school marm, Mildred
Fry, M.A., made a recent pilgrimage to the
altar with Prof. Muscio, of Cambridge. For
the last two or three years the Australian
’Varsity-woman has been teaching in Eng-
land. The groom is also an Australian, and,
as his name warns you, of Italian parents.
He is a professor of philosophy. Sydney
Uni. has borrowed him for 10 months, so the
Muscio duet come this way in January.
Another member of the clever Fry family,
Edith, 8.A., is in Paris. She took up art
decorative work in that city with such suc-
cess that at present she is doing the work
of a. man who is fighting the Hun. A bril-
liant young brother died here a few weeks
ago.
Bill and Co. generously lend a whole third-
floor back—nine rooms in all —-in their new
Bridge-street buildings for Red Cross cen-
tral offices; and the. ladies moved in on
Saturday. The offices are worth £550 per
year, and the big meat-shipping co. hands
them over for six months at least. It isn’t
the first Birt benefaction either; the co. has
been lending a shipping clerk for manly
moons.
Leonardo de Lorenzo, the great U.S.
Italian flautist, has written a concert waltz
anu dedicated it to his “cherished colleague”
John Lemmone. The Australian hasn’t even
a bowing acquaintance—except in Art—
with Lorenzo. The latter followed John’s
tracks in S’Africa about 20 years ago, and
has been throwing bouquets at him ever
since.
There is great interest among the girls
to know just what Julius Superbus Knight’s
next move may be. Once he carved wood :
now he knits socks for soldiers. I’m told
he lias finished 20 pairs—some of them
made of heather wool with a green stripe.
He plies his needles whilst living in a new
hostel in Castlereagh-street.
The Woolwich Pierrots, with Topsy Kely-
nack as hon. sec., had a bumper house a
Hunter’s Hill on October 22. The 12th
Light Horse Comfort Fund was the objec-
tive. In blissful ignorance of the majesty
of the Law, some of the girls and boys in-
dustriously plastered the scenery with pos-
ters. By the light o’ the moon they even
gummed notices on the telegraph posts. Then
some mean person “laid information” at
headquarters, and a deputation of trembling
maidens, with the fear of six months’ hard
in their hearts, tottered up to town to in-
terview a postal tetrarch. He rvas nor
adamant, and darkly hinted at a way out.
Now anyone who meets a Woolwich maiden
brandishing a knife on her native crags can
remain calm. She is only looking for proofs
of her crime, and clasps the means to scrape
it off.
The Violet Dandies* outdoor show in Bays-
vvater-road has a clever Dunedin girl, Doro-
thy Gardner, as stage-boss and producer. This
streak of energy has a pair of sparkling
black eyes and a ton of vim in a willow-
wand frame. She has done a bit of writing
as a side-line to dancing, acting and produc-
ing. Cuthbert Rose, the comic man, and
baritone George Brooke are the meat of a
lively programme.
A brisk and brainy old lady, Airs. Evans,
passed out at Killara the other afternoon.
She was the widow of the late Markham
Evans, one of the founders of the Sunday
Times. Heart failure was responsible for
a swift call. The only daughter. Airs. Aid-
worth, was an organiser of the big patriotic
show that was to have occurred the same
evening in Killara; so the committee post-
poned the party and packed up cakes and
sweets for return to t lie donors.
That fine, upstanding lass, Jean Brunton.
has accepted an engagement ring from Cap-
tain Adrian Aloreing, an Englishman whose
homelet is in the hop county, Kent. The
Sydney girl is a daughter of Air. John S.
Brunton, of the flour-mill firm. Jean and
mamma, after a stay in N’York, went on to
England, where the crisis occurred.
Fred J. Mortleman, of the other side, and
Mary Doutty, of Marina. Mosman, were re-
sponsible for the few bits of pretty wedding
finery this month. Choristers proceshed to
the altar ahead of the bride and her maidens,
and the “I will” was said under a white
anchor hung from a decorated arch. The
bridal plumage was a shimmer of white
satin and Brussels lace, with pink tulle flush-
ing through the white foam. The Alortle-
man pair go off in the Medina on October 30.
The Tom Hughes lady took the best chair
at last week’s meet for the Passionist
Fathers’ entertainment. The big charity
fete is an Easter fixture.
The Babies’ Kit Society, with the busi-
ness-like Airs. Tom Raine in charge, is mak-
ing a dash for warm garments. Infant
refugees in England, who arrive in swaddling
garb of newspaper, are clothed and com-
forted from the Sydney hive. The depot is
at George and Jamieson streets.
Alaisie Scott has favorably considered a
question put by Jock Allan, eldest son of
Captain Alex. Allan, of Gosford. Great-
grandpa was the founder of the Allan line.
The girl’s parent was the late Gavan Scott,
of Neutral Bay.
A cool snap brought back the tartlet mil-
linery. It is oval, with two little tails
dangling down behind. A wreath of liny
blooms is apparently gummed round the
microscopic crown.
Last week I told you that Aliss Pudney.
who leaves for India and a wedding next
week, had been a “sympathetic honorary-
secretary” to Aladame Calve on her lasi
visit to Australia. I now learn that Aliss
Pudney was merely a fellow-passenger to
Australia with Calve, and for a few days
her guest; and the report that she had any
other association with the singer is an error.
One of the few younger concert singers
who have kept the pot boiling in spite of the
war is the Westralian soprano, Lucille Ben-
stead. When the last news came she was
in Dublin.
Ladies who plunged into scalloped flounces
are not making many converts. Even the
very slim look too much like the draped leg
of a chair in the billowy waves of glace. As
compensation for the new fashions women
can now run when they see a motor heaving
up to them. For quite three years we have
only hopped, but the chauffeur’s honk now
finds us unafraid.
Still they come! This time it’s a word
from the 2nd Battalion Comforts Com-
mittee. They remark, in italics, that they
are having plum puddings made at the
nominal price of threepence each. Send
your cash to the depot, 39 Pitt-street. Ail
parcels for Christmas gifts must oe rushed
in to this address not later than October 20.
Yours affectionately.
VAN DORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, October 25. 1915.
My Dear Yariue, —
Unto the Stanleys a son is born. It hap-
pened last Friday, and is the only event of
the kind to occur in Victorian vice-regal
circles since Charles Melbourne Hope, second
son of the late Hopetoun, arrived at the Lin-
lithgow’s country-place near Ararat. Before
baby Hope there had been nothing doing in
that line, for very many years—back as far
as the ’6o’s, anyhow, when widower Sir
Henry Barkly picked a young Australian
bride to act as relieving bazaar-opener.
The Soldiers’ Rest Room at Wirths’ is
under most distinguished patronage. But
though Distinguished Pat is a nice fellow
to have about the house, he doesn’t contri-
bute much to the larder. This by the way.
The State Governor and John Madden and
some others spoke at the opening. Allawa
Buchanan, the social organiser, gave a
library and an organ ; Byron Moore and the
V.R.C. were responsible for a grand piano ;
and Alcock’s donated a billiard table. A
brigade of Sassiety girls are attending in de-
tachments to scrub and dust and tidy up,
and honoraries will see to the cooking and
serving. This help idea is being extended
to all the rest rooms; it is in operation at
the Quamby as well as at Wirths’.
The Western District has a fine reputa-
tion for looking after its doctors once they
have overcome the stiff and uncomfortable
feeling of their first 10 years’ practice among
the grassocracy. Brown, of Colac, is one of
these tried and trusted medicine men; and
the fact partly accounts for young Geoffrey
Brown having been at Cambridge, doing-
medical studies, when the first German
bomb burst in Belgium. The lad, who pre-
sently became a lieutenant in a British
regiment, was wounded in ’France early this
month.
Pianist Goll and violinist Verbrugghen
(the Belgian at the wheel of Sydney. Con-
servatorium) played together twice last
week. The second concert had a packed
house and a madly enthusiastic musical
audience of the all-wool variety. Ver-
brugghen plays well —and knows it. When
he saws you off a length of sonata he does
it languidly, as if he doesn’t feel
quite sure that you really deserve the
treat. He made me feel so humble
that I longed to build a pyramid
of Vienna chairs and crawl under it. When
he raised his eyebrows at me in that super-
cilious way geniuses have I almost howled
for mercy. And you mustn’t run away with
the idea that his violin is cheap. It is a
genuine Something-or-other, and he would
certainly refuse to take 15s. for it. He
made some beautiful sounds with it —
sounds that made me think of dew and
honey and roast beef. It was a very mellow
machine.
. Goll wore a child-like smile and showed
his very white teeth all the time he was
tickling the piano to death. He had student
Peggy Sutherland to turn his music for
him. Peggy, in bright greeny-blue, was a
picturesque improvement on the
saic crow-like anonymous male
does the job. Mrs. Goll, in black, sat in
the front row and beamed. Mrs. Janson,
in shining red. set me thinking of chemists’
lamps. The University people were there
thick as bees and drinking hard with their
ears. Professor Masson made a speech and
formally thanked Verbrugghen on behalf of
the Lady Northcote Permanent Orchestra
Trust Fund Committee (deep breath here!),
which was mixed up in some way with the
recital.
Dr. Hamilton Russell brought his lovely
silver hair and Alan Newton, a clever young
local surgeon who is causing an immense
fuss in medical circles here. Mrs. Sander-
son (shipping interests was wrapped in
silk Paisley and looked her handsomest.
Pianist Gunning Sat in the crowd and be-
came more and more demonstrative as the
evening wore on. Verbrugghen’s playing
seemed to go to his hair. All the musical
folk wore that characteristic look of yearn-
ing for unutterable things when the fiddler
got off something by Franck —-his favorite
composer.
For his stay in Melbourne Verbrugghen
has been the guest of pianist Edward Goll,
down the Hobson’s Bay shore. Verbrugg-
hen has a personality apart from his music
achievements; he’s a born talker and
thinks quickly enough to give art-words in
a torrent. He finds the Australian has
“wonderful natural talent,” and believes
the prospects of the Conservatorium are
marvellous. Edward Goll’s fine work at
Verbrugghen’s three violin and piano
recitals may be the last of his Melbourne
appearances for a time, as he is understood
to be booked for the N. S. Wales Conserva-
torium also.
For quite a while the Overseas Club has
been looking around for somebody to help it
with the rent, and the quaint little Ancient
Mariners’ Club has just taken over part of
the basement, fitting it out to represent a
ship’s deck. In times past the Mariners
sometimes met at the houseboat-restaurant
which is to be found afloat on the Yarra
alongside Queen’s-bridge, or dined with
entire respectability at some rather more
central and expensive city eatery; but with
a persistent increase of membership the club
found it necessary to have a permanent
anchorage. The affable Charles Atkins is
the Mariners’ president-navigator, and it was
Mrs. Atkins who named the new “ship” at
its “launching” the other evening.
Belle White and Percy Alderton were
married with the blessing of the Best People
last Thursday. The youthful bride was
steered to the altar rails by Uncle Bowes
Kelly, and moans of admiration went up as
she rustled past the congregation obscured
by yards of lace, and so forth. Somebody
whispered in my ear that the lace was worn
by William the Conqueror when he landed
in 1066. Bridesmaid Violet Kelly (cousin)
blossomed in palest pink chiffon, and Arthur
Cooke, who shortly proceeds to the wars,
lent her one of his arms. About a hundred
of the local peerage sat down to refresh-
ments, and after George Fairbairn, Herbert
Power, Charlie Bright and some others had
seen the bridegroom’s hair ruffled and rose
leaves forced down his back, the motor
whirred away with lucky footwear and one
kerosene tin trailing behind.
Hilda Morrison, sister of the famous
George, the late Norman (of Geelong Col-
lege) and of our Dr. Reggie, has gone
off to Rangoon to marry Lance Gaunt, son
of the late Judge and brother of
writer Mary. Lance is one of the two
brothers who pursue law. All the rest are
terrible fire-eaters.
Vera Madden, the pretty daughter of
Frank, is blushfully accepting congratula-
tions. Lieutenant Johnson, of H.M.A.S.
Confetti, is the happy sailor. You don’t
know the Confetti? Neither do I. But we
must be very careful not to give the enemy
any information. So Confetti let it be.
Last week Clarence Smart, only son of cx-
Collector of Customs Archibald Smart,
paused long enough in his war-preparations
to get married to a dan ah ter of the George
Laverses of Kew. Clarence is a sergeant in
the A.I.F. The Smarts had another lad, an
amateur entomologist, who was killed some
years ago through a fall in the dark, while
pursuing a specimen for his collection. He
was engaged to Nurse Yeaman, once of Mel-
bourne Hospital, but now abroad on military
service.
Merrie Boyd (Merrick is the full-dress
style) was married last week to Miss Gough,
daughter of Mrs. Evelyn Gough. They
“celebrated” later at the Grand Hotel. Mer-
rick is the artistic young man who has made
some noticeably good art pottery from Aus-
tralian clays. Brother Penleigh is the
landscape artist (now soldiering) who car-
ried off the prize offered for the best picture
of the Federal capital, and they are both
related to the clan of Boyds and a’Becketts
hereabouts. The bride is artistic also, and
modelled gum-leaf designs on her own wed-
ding cake.
The Maoriland Cowpers (she is bachelor
Dr. Nyulasy’s sister) have returned after
honeymooning for a year and a half. Quite
accidentally, of course, they happen to strike
Melbourne during the Cup season, on their
way home, and (quite accidentally again!)
Mrs. Cowper has some fascinating clothes
with her. Catch a poorly-dressed woman
coining near Melbourne at this time of year !
Adelaide will now say a few words; —
Lieut. H. V. Throssell, the Westralian who has
earned a V.C., sheds a reflected glory on this State,
as he and his brother were at Prince Alfred College
from 1896 till 1902. The hall-marked hero is at
present in hospital in London.
“Billy” Wood, who went to England in the Mon-
golia, has a commission in the Scottish Horse. He
used to be a well-known follower of the local
hounds ; his sport is now pig-sticking.
E. Stokes, who has just come to the fag-end of
six months’ leave, left for Bombay by the Khyber;
his wife and family remain here for a month or so
longer. One of the Pewsey Vale Gilberts, who has
also been here on leave, was a passenger by the
same boat.
The Government House f6te in aid of Belgian and
Red Cross funds, which is billed for November 11,
12 and 13, threatens to lay over anything this
village has yet witnessed.
Dr. Pitcher, of the 2nd Light Horse Ambulance,
is returning on sick-leavq. Lieut. Neil Campbell
also is coming in from England, where he went
for repairs, after being severely wounded: at Galli-
poli.
The Rose Show, at the Town Hall, on Thursday,
provided conclusive proof that the drought in the
suburban gardens has finally broken. The Governor
“declared” in the usual way,- at the instigation of
T. E. Barr Smith, who is the society’s • president.
There were other things than roses—sweet-pea,
poppies, columbines, orchids, pansies, carnations and
stocks in a welter of color and a riot of scent that
made even the dingy Town Hall a place to dream in.
Hilda Felstead, who has accepted an engagement
with the Brennan-Fuller eo., and who has used her
talent generously for patriotic entertainments ever
since the war began, was farewelled at the Unley
Town Hall the other night, with complimentary
music, an address and a dressing-case.
Mrs. Crawford Vaughan is recovering from her
influenza, and expects soon to be making sandbags
again. This should improve the situation in Galli-
poli. Ponder, M.P., has undergone an appendicitis
operation in Melbourne; but his little show has not
so much bearing on the war.
The Navy League celebrated Trafalgar Day with
a special public meeting, which was sanctified by
the Galways and a party (God bless it!) from
Government House. A sufficient quantity of the
general public attended. The League’s president,
E. B. Grundy, gave an address (you know the sort
of thing!), and the secretary, Mr. Tyler, gave a
short lantern lecture with special slides. (Hooray!)
Mrs. Symon and her Girl Guides have presented the
League with a banner bearing the names of the
British battleships and submarines that have been
lost in the war, and this sad reminder was on view
on Thursday night. On the same evening the foun-
dations of a Returned Soldiers’ Association were
laid in the Cheer-Up Club rooms.
The land that Peter Waite gave to the Govern-
ment for the purposes of agricultural education is
going to be utilised. The deeds of about 120 acres
between the Fullarton and Unley roads have already
been handed over, and the University authorities
have been given the deeds for part of the Netherby
estate. The Urrbrae estate, which comes to the
Government later, will be used for residential
quarters for the University and as a recreation
reserve.
The Governor is making a round of the manufac-
turing industries of the State, and has already looked
in at a good many factories, including a harness
mill where big military contracts are in hand.
Engagement.—Miss M. Law Smith to IT. S. lludd,
the ex-politician -who is now in camp.
John Verran is the chief dispenser of texts in the
South Aus. Assembly, but other members know a few
too, and Peake last week “capped” a hymn-book
quotation for him. Verran was chaffing Peake and
Co. with having been thrown out of office:
How blest the hours 1 once enjoyed!
How sweet their memory still!
quoted Verran; then Peake took up the running:
Now there is left an aching void
Your crowd can never fill!
The Higginses and other seasiders who
were engineering the Beaumaris Carnival
on Saturday have been moaning ever since
the week-end rain partially burst their show.
A Governor had been engaged to do the
opening, and some of the motored classes
had shown signs of following his dust; but
when the day arrived there was no dust —
only mud and a razor-edged wind. Still the
indoor part of the revel went on, and Gov-
ernor Stanley left his new boy at home
and manfully sat out the Kirby concert and
the Rose Tea. The Lady Stanley Fund gets
about £2OO from it.
Mrs. Dick Casey is smiling again. The
big V.R.C. husband is out of hospital, and
is being tended devotedly by herself and a
trained nurse or two. No visitors are
allowed yet, but the regulations will be less
strict presently.
The Frank Austin widow has gone back
to Egypt with golden-haired daughter
Avalon. They're going to devote their spare
time and money to the wounded soldiers.
One of our most precious bachelors.
Lieutenant Steel, is off to the beastly old
war with Colonel Grimwade’s explosive
ironmongery. He is as like as a twin tc
brother Freddie Steel, who was killed is
France just this time last year while lead
ing a charge.
Carrie Haase, who used to do platform
reciting here before she went to England
and won eminence in the “legitimate,” is
back in Melbourne. The war cancelled her
plans, but she will make some fresh ones
as soon as the atmosphere clears.
Another returned traveller is Talworth
Burder, who was looked upon as a musical
prodigy in these parts 10 or 11 years ago.
After getting European experience as com-
poser and conductor, he returns as Regin-
ald de Talworth and talks of producing a
couple of his musical plays locally. I don’t
like the new hiccup in his name.
Actor George Titheradge, who has pitched
camp in Sydney, is putting in a week or
two here in the hope that the change will
help his nerves to behave better. For the
last three months he has been troubled with
neuritis.
Dorothea, the youngest Mark Moss
daughter, has had to exist without her sol-
dier fiance ever since the first boom of the
wars. However, she is cheered somewhat to
find that he is now Major Dare —which is
a fine, brave name and title for a young man
of 26.
The Savoy dinner in aid of the Red Cross
is still in anticipation. Some exalted Appe-
tite had not enough edge on, or something,
so the food has been put back to catch the
Melbourne Cuppers.
After all, Leslie Stuart is not going to
Egypt. Another man who toiled on local
Red Crossings takes Stuart’s place.
On Friday night the most fanatical music-
drinkers rolled up to the Conservatorium for
a deep draught of Fritz Hart’s best brew.
The whole programme was Fritz, yet nobody
came away with a headache. Evidently the
curly-haired director can make the right
stuff.
In “The Dancing Mistress” young Maggie
Dickinson, now trying her wings as leading
lady, gets the iceberg stare from most of the
walking duchesses. As most curates are
aware, jealousy is one of the failings of the
mumming profession, and the budding Bern-
hardts cannot get over Maggie’s well-earned
promotion. All through her turn the looks
of the chorus are sidelong and gloomy.
Maggie now wears her own brown hair,
which is an improvement on the heavy red
wig she used to support. As the D.M. (not
the D.C.M. !) she weighs herself down with
tons of Paris-green toggery.
Chubby Edward Sugden, the Methodist
parson who is Master of Queen’s, is now
Captain Sugden, in a tightly-fitting uniform
and a broad smile.
Mrs. R. G. Baldock is in Melbourne to say
good-bye to her people and see the Cup run
before making off to India with hubby, who
is giving what spare time remains to him
to the Clifton Downs sheep-run. The Ban-
analand drought has cleared the place almost
to a sheep, and I think he is busy furnishing
it with drought-resisting lambs. The Bal-
dock pet racing horse, Scotch Artillery, is
being told to win the Brisbane Cup.
Postmaster Linton, of the Northern Terri-
tory, who has passed out so tragically, was
a brother of Colonel Linton. One daughter
(an extremely pretty girl) comes to Mel-
bourne occasionally, and another is at school
here. The Lintons are related to wealthy
Victorian families —the Hentys and Mac-
phersons and McLeans, for instance.
Dr. C«cil McAdarn, son of a Brighton
medico, is at work in France. He has a
breathlessly interesting job at the chief clear-
ing hospital, alongside some of cleverest
French surgeons.
The "Dick” Hawkers are over from Adel-
aide, holding on to the tail of their Carbine
horse, Cyklon. There would have been jun-
ketings if the noble extravagance had won
the Caulfield Cup, but it saved everybody’s
digestion by not doing so.
Kathleen Graham, one of the rich damsels
of Cliveden Mansions, has given up the
gentle sport of programme-selling for charity,
and gone as a voluntary helper to a con-
valescent hospital for soldiers. Truly the
Empire is Waking Up.
The Innes-Noads (she is a sister of the
Spruce Myth) have taken a gorgeous country
place in Berks, England. Every now and
then—or oftener —Mrs. Innes-Noad runs a
motor-load of wounded down to her lean-to
for a rest cure. Some of the Melbourne
brides whose husbands are away at the war
have been entertained and comforted at the
Berks house by the big-hearted matron.
These lone ones include May Grice-Wilson,
sister Mrs. Collins, and Molly-Atherton-
Erskine-White. (That runs me out of hy-
phens!) Daughter Mrs. Geoffrey Stuart is
another refugee on Mother’s hearth.
Now that theatrical-manager George Ed-
wardes is dead, Melbourne is wondering if
the nephew it met has stepped into the mana-
gerial boots he used to talk about. The
nephew struck Melbourne in company with
a sunburnt captain from the Balkans.
While they lingered here the supper cafes
never went to sleep. It is said that the
irrepressible pair started the town on the
outburst of night life that ended .in the sen-
sational cafe raid. Nephew passed as a
millionaire—or a millionheir, I forget which.
The committee of the Women’s Hospital
is delighted with itself. The institution is
now spreading a new wing, and the money
to feather it flowed in from friends without
any public drum-beating—£lloo of it.
A most beautiful bachelor, who stands
6ft. 4in. in his socks when his boots are
being mended, and has gone with the Field
Artillery, spent his last leave in ringing-up
favorite girls and working off a poor
little joke. As this : “Oh !Is that you ?... .
Ye-s, I’m off this time, old girl. Au rev-
war/” Then the wires would carry a fatuous
chuckle and the sly inquiry, “See the point?”
The girls thought it “most awfully funny,”
and have been maddening their men-friends
by repeating it. Which shows that when a
man is beautiful and looks like a skyscraper
in his hosiery he can work off any old thing.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“J.M.”: I support “Pick” (B. ,30/9/’l5)
in disputing the lyre-bird’s mimicry of bulloekies,
sawyers, splitters, etc. I lived for years where these
national assets were unknown, and have heard the
lyres utter their various alleged imitations of sounds
never produced in the locality. 1 quote a paragraph,
from the “New Australian Heading Book l\.” in
use in the public schools, which is responsible for
much of the belief regarding the alleged mimicry
aforesaid: “The lyre-bird is a great mimic. It
mocks the cries of other birds, such as magpies and
laughing-jackasses, the barking of dogs, and even
such camp sounds as the sawing of timber. Often 1
have mistaken it for the thrush.” 1 dunno why
these immigrants are allowed to write our Public
Instruction books.
“McNorth” : Let me buy iuto the auto-
matic-pistol versus revolver argument. The auto,
might suit the requirements of city householders
during burglar scares, or suburban explorers who
are always handy to a gunsmith’s, but for the man
who knocks around in regions where Black Brother
has a habit of suddenly becoming nasty, it is not
a weapon to be relied on. Under my pillow is
an old-style .450 Colt revolver which I have humped
for years through parts where a squirt is apt to be
wanted in a hurry. Through the capsizing of boats
and canoes, it has been under water for days at a
stretch. It has been fired so rapidly as to become
almost red-hot. Hornets have built nests in its in-
nards. It has been wet with rain and caked with
mud scores of times, and it: once fell over a precipice.
In short, it has suffered all those things which it
should not have suffered. Yet the old gun is still
serviceable, and a bit more rough usage wouldn’t
ruin it. What automatic would stand such hand-
ling and remain reliable? Even when new the auto,
is liable to go wrung. Werner had one of the latest
pattern when he was killed by niggers in
Papua a year or two ago. It was found near the
spot where the tragedy occurred —with a cartridge
stuck in it. A few empty shells were lying about.
'The theory is that Werner kept the cannibals off'
till the automatic, on which his life depended,
jambed, and then
“Panhandle Pete": The soldier here—
“here” being- Gallipoli—is stringing together some
bright new expressions. For instance, “blightv,”
otherwise “old blightv,” means England. If we have
a blighty it is a wound assuring a visit to John
Bull’s island.. “Cushy” is soft, pleasant. \ o t
merely yielding, like a cushion, but—well, they talk
about “a cushy trench,” “a cushy wound.” A
“jammy” wound entitles the holder merely to a trip
to the base or some such abhorrent spot. Next we
have one of Germanic origin, and of very varied
usage—“straf.” Used as a noun “straf” refers to
a “scrap” of small proportions, in which neither
Party attempts any advance. The verb “straf” has
a parallel noun, and is often applied to sniping-.
One “poops” at the enemy’s trench or at his trans-
ports. But rifles do not poop, only guns. “Pip-
squeak.” is a shell thrown by a trench gun. The
name is appropriate. The 77m.m. shell is “Whiz-
bang,” also very appropriate. One’s acquaintance
with it begins and ends with exactly that
whiz-bang-, and the thing is over. However, this
shell is not recognised in the best trenches. Next
is the “lu-ump”—some spell it “crump,” but lirupps
make it, so krump she is. You hear krump coming,
and you can locate the spot where it is going to
land. Owing, however, to atmospheric conditions,
wind, drift, etc., it generally falls elsewhere, and
makes a noise like “krump” ; just like that
“krump.” Its chest measurement is Oin., and it
isn t at all popular. The “coal-box” and the “Jack
Johnson” are one and the same, and answer to Biu.
odd. The “portmanteau” and the “black Maria”
hover round Uiu. and 12in., and the notorious 17in-
sheli is the “Brighton Express.” If you stand up
to the rails as the inter-State tears through Sun-
shine, say, you will get a vivid idea of what the
shell sounds like as it passes overhead. “Archi-
balds” are anti-air craft guns of either party. They
are all “Archies,” for short.

“Blauky”: After Australia it is weird to
SC’ these Egyptians conducting their farming opera-
tions. The plough is an awkward-looking* turnout
generally made of wood, and is drawn by a cow
with unexpected lumps distributed over it, or some-
times a camel, or a donkey, or any old thing; in
fact, wallabies and 'roos ought to consider them-
selves lucky they’re Australians. Several times I
have seen a camel and a bent cow yoked together
to a plough. It looks ridiculous, but looks don’t
worry the Egyptian, any more than dirt and flics do.

“Sarto": This ought to be a record for
lady bush-laborers. A Riverina (N.S.W.) district
has two fairies, about 12 feet all told, mother and
daughter, who earn a crust by navigating a plough
when the time is ripe. When it isn’t they revert to
grubbing, fencing, wood-carting, etc. They recog-
nise no union but their own, work as long- as it's
light, and pick up plenty jobs. They help to supply
Albury with the fuel to fry its morning sausage.
Last summer they were in great demand as water-
carters. Seated on a red lOOgal. tank, with slouch
felt hat, man's coat and —no, a skirt, they look
just the thing-. One day I saw them nearing home
with a good-sized leak in the tank about a foot
from the bottom, of which they were unaware. People
say the tank was blue the next trip, but I don't
believe it.

erected over 40 years ago. The posts are about 4in.

“Billy Bunker” : It is usually arranged
that two shearers catch their sheep from the one
pen. As the pen gets cut out there is keen rivalry
amongst these pen-mates, each trying to force tin-
other to take the roughs. The hist sheep left in the
pen is always a very rough one, and is termed the
“cobbler” or the “snob.” This is an allusion, by
a devious way. to the article with which tin
cobbler is reputed to have killed his mU-us with
the last. The part of the woolshed where the har-
boring is done is the hoard,” atid the party who
supervises the work is the “boss of the hoard,” “the
boss,” “pitch-en-toss,” “the bloke,” or “
Most often . A shed-hand is a “rousic,”
“loppy.” “blue-tongue” or “wop-wop.” Ii was left
to the manage]- of a contract shearing co. to earn
undying fame (and blasphemy) by explaining in the
Arbitration Court that a rouseabout was a “dere-
lict.” The expert who attends all machinery is in-
variably “the squirt.” The boss, expert, classer and
bookkeeper don’t dine in the same lmt as the rest:
of the camp, and are in consequence "Silvertails.”
The cook is “the babbler'’ or “dough-puncher,” the
former an allusion to “the babbling brook”; or he
may be the “rusty hook” or “the poisoner.” The
cook’s offside)- is “the slushy.” The yarder-up,
picker-up and penner-up need no explanation. The
“broomie” is the “blue tongue” who sweeps the
board free of locks, etc. Wool-rollers and pieee-
pickers are recognisable terms. A “pony” is a rouse-
about who carries the fleeces from the classer to the
wool-bins. A “barrower” is one who tries his
'prentice hand at shearing during; the smoke-o’s.
Bay-rowing has been stopped by the last Arbitration
award. The expression “tar” is still used, although
sheep-dip .has now replaced it for dressing cuts,
etc. A jumbuck-barber always refers to himself as
a “shearer man.” If a shearer man is spoken to for
rough work, he is “chipped” or “gets a wire.” By
the way, he never gets “sacked.” He always “pelts
it in” or “snatches iiis bit.”

THE COLLIER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A dirt patch on the mornin’ sea,
Smudgin’ grey wi’ black ;
A rusty ocean parasite,
A broken-kneed old hack ;
A wastrel o’ the water ways,
Lumberin’ on the track.
See her chopped-off end a-liftin’
To the surgin' o’ the seas ;
M atch her stumpy masts a-rakin’
O’ the blue sky an’ the breeze !
Oh, just listen to her engines—-
Clank an’ rattle, bang an’ wheeze !
She s an awesome thing to look at,
Hither light or deep wi’ coal —-
A balloon o’ rusty side-plates,
Or submerged too deep to roll.
She s a swashin’, bashin’ horror
M i’ the iron in ’er soul.
She's an architect’ral marvel
MT an unsurprisin’ pace:
She’s a bleary, weary wonder,
Lackin every sign o’ grace :
An’ slic’d break an angel's temper,
if she didn’t break his face.
She s a rattlin’, shakin" scrapheap,
M i’ her screw a-ehurnin’ air ;
Just a snub-nosed, dirty coal-boat,
Bound for any bloomin’ where;
An offence upon the ocean,
Yet she doesn’t seem to care.
1‘ or she’s faithful as a grafter
In 'er dour-faced sort o’ way;
In the tempest an’ in sunshine
She’s a-rakin’ in the hay,
An she’s built some dandy liner
in ’er rough-an’-tumble day.
I’itchin’, rollin’, grindin', sliakin’,
She’s a demon in a fit,
Makin’ horrid stinks an' noises
That’d make a pole-cat spit:
She goes bruisin’ air an’ water,
An’ just fancyin' she's It.
M hen the Lord from out o’ chaos
liaised the smilin’ earth an’ sea,
Found His handiwork was pleasin',
I'm a wonderin’ if He
Hver reckoned on the coil-boat.
Ever guessed what she could be.
Lurchin' through the mornin’ grey.
Nosin’ to the gale,
Masts an’ funnel steerin' south.
Scrap o’ coal-black sail,
Heelin’ like a drunken thing
Down the coastwise trail.
VICTORIA.
ROBERT M. HEDDLE.

HOW I DARKENED MY
GREY HAIR.
Lady gives Simple Home Recipe That
She Used to Darken Her Grey Hair.
For years I tried to restore my grey hair
to its natural color with the prepared dyes
and stains, but none of them gave satisfac-
tion, and they were all expensive. I finally
came across a simple recipe, which I mixed
at home, that gives wonderful results. I
gave the recipe, which is as follows, to a
number of my friends, and they are all de-
lighted with it. To 7oz. of water add a small
box of Orlex Compound, loz. of bay rum and
i o z. of glycerine. These ingredients can be
bought at any chemist’s at very little cost.
Use every other day until the hair becomes
the required shade, then every two weeks.
It will not only darken the grey hair, but
removes dandruff and scalp humors, and acts
as a tonic to the hair. It is not sticky or
greasy, does not rub off, and does not color
the scalp.* [5

“Ringbolt”: The Papuan boatman (B.
16/9/T5) isn’t alone in his fancy for a belayed
sheet. There’s no better boat-hand anywhere than
an Orang-Laut (sea-Malay), but if you leave him
to tend sheets in a blow he’s pretty sure to earn
you a capsize. I was caught once in a Sumatra-
squall off Bintang Island. This specialty has a
nasty trick of slacking-off almost to nothing, and
then springing at you again in an instant like a
young typhoon. We had luffed and let fly a
couple of times, when, having to go for’ard for
something, I left the mainsheet to S’li Mahlat to
hold, with a single turn round the cleat. Ten
seconds later we were over, and, since a shark-
haunted seaway doesn’t lend itself to dignified
expostulation, I vigorously cursed S’li as we
swam. By all his gods he swore that he was blame-
less, but when we had been picked up by a prahu
and had righted our craft I found that the sheet
had been knotted as if for all eternity.

THE PEACEMAKER: “Now, Mick, he’s had enough—two black eyes!”
MICK: “ Yis! An’ fer two pins I’d give him two more.”
“Barcoo” : A number of the leading char-
acteristics of the Gulf and Territory are upside down
or wrong way about. For instance, the tide flows
only once in 24 hours, but is a very high one. On
most stations the smartest “stockmen” are gins.
The highest and driest ground during the “wet” season
is right along each bank of the streams and rivers,
and the back-country cattle have to be collared as
soon as possible after the deluge begins to dry up,
or else good-bye till next wet season drives them
from the low-lying land out from the water-courses.
In the McDonnell Ranges, S.W. of Burketown, huge
gorges, which were the beds of ancient rivers
flowing south, pierce and cleave the hills at inter-
vals, while the present water-courses run north.
“Boona Blue”: Anent “Sarto” and his
bower birds’ palaces (B. 2/9/'l5). There is one in
Condobolin (N.S.W.) district now which is seven
years old. It is about Bft. x 2ft., and is built of
grass, about 14in. high, and as thickly studded and
as solidly set as an ordinary millet broom. The
ornaments include various colored glasses, screws,
nails, every nut and berry growing roundabouts, and
even pins. A piece of mirror thrown to the birds
recently has not yet been seized. At each entrance
to their majestic hall the owners have built a portico
about 3ft. long, composed of nuts only.
* * * *
“Elgar” : As to this argument about the
warping of Australian timbers. Some years ago I
built a workshop and wash-house of Victorian hard-
wood —alleged to be messmate. The floor was of Tas-
manian blue-gum. Although the studs were 2ft. apart
there is no visible warp about any of the boards,
; while the floor, though subject to hard usage, is as
sound as the day it was laid. Some of the stuff
had been lying in the timber-yard a long while and
' was well seasoned, but the weatherboards were beauti-
fully green and juicy; yet there is still no more
weatherproof building on earth.
* * * *
“Kuku-Kuku”: Samarai, the island town
at the tail of New Guinea, is the Pearl of the
Pacific, so far as my experience goes. Tahiti, with
the splendid spire of Orohena rising 7000
feel above your little steamer, and weirdly wonder-
fed Moorea looking at you across the bay, is very,
very lovely. Manahilci’s windy blue lagoon, sur-
rounded by an irregular wreath of cocoanut forest
tossed upon the sea (for that is what an atoll is),
fascinates everyone who has ever seen it. There is
something irresistible about every bit of Samoa —
an attraction hard to describe, but felt by many
before and after Robert Louis Stevenson. For a
perfect little “high island,” Raratonga’s outfit of
snowy beach, fringing cocoanut, and central peaks
veiled in falling creepers would be hard to beat.
Suva and Ovalau are famous. The green lawns and
orange woods of Yavau, overlooking a harbor that
is better even than —(sh!) —body out in reality all
men’s boyish dreams of a South Sea Island. But
Samarai ! It is a real island town, in the first
place—a sparkling village of red roofs and deep
verandahs, set upon a green-velvet island that fits
it as closely as if it were a toy on a stand. It
has been gardened and planted, and is full of
flowers, and its palms are, or seem, taller and whiter-
stemmed than any others in New Guinea. It is in
the midst of a group of lovely islands,
and the coloring of the straits and inlets is the sort
of blue and green that you see in opal matrix. All
day long launches, schooners, cutters, dinghies, come
sailing or chug-chugging up to the various jetties,
and people from the goldfields, from plantations,
from Government expeditions, get out and come ashore
with their bags and boxes, as at a railway station.
In Samarai you rank partly by your moral char-
acter, no doubt, but very largely by the tonnage
and nature of your boat. If you have none you are
nobodj r . If you have a dinghy it is well. If you
have a good-sized cutter it is better. Do you pos-
sess either a small launch or a schooner you are
a person worthy of all respect. And if you are one
of the few who own a large launch or a sailing
vessel fitted with auxiliary you take your place at
once among the aristocracy. These are, as Mrs.
Gamp might put it, the consequences of living on a
Pearl. But the Pearl itself... .When you come up
by the steamer you immediately go for a walk round
the island. This takes you 20 minutes, if you
hurry, which of course you do not; and every half
minute of the walk shows you some new and won-
derful combination of island, wood and water. It
is all views and view. You go back to the steamer,
exclaiming that here is Paradise upon earth, and
someone off the island tells you that it is Purgatory
to live in, and that people are always fighting. You
needn’t believe him. Samarai has reformed of late
years to an extent that puts all ancient descriptions
of the island out of date. It is astonishingly sober,
and stiff with respectability. It has got to that
point where it absolutely loves to be shocked; 10
years ago you couldn’t have shocked it with a herd
of wild Huns. It has calls, and cards, and teas;
it has tongues with a razor edge, but hands that
overflow with kindness. It is civilised —O Lord,
yes ; church three times on Sunday, library, Chamber
of Commerce. Yet the other afternoon I met a
Ferguson Island pigmy cannibal swaggering down
the main street, dressed in a boar’s tusk, some teeth
and a couple of leaves. He was about four feet ten
perpendicularly, and something not much less later-
ally. He was full of bounce and impudence, and his
canoe, carved and hung with shells, lay just below
the two storeyed, acetylene-lighted Cosmopolitan
Hotel. The Pearl of the Pacific is interesting yet.

“S. S. Severn” : When Br’er Mosquito in
the Gulf country becomes “too obsessive,” local
binghis of the Koko-bundi tribe build an image of
wood and grass-straw interwoven with a dead man’s
hair and kidney fat. This they call N’guru. They
set up the idol in their midst and dance and bow
before it, after which the old man of the crowd takes
it, and, holding it at arm’s length aloft, and fol-
lowed by the tribe howling and banging drums and
making all the noise it is capable of, capers into
the scrub until the moon goes behind a cloud. Then
a funeral pyre is raised, and N’guru is cast upon it
and cremated. It may be magic, or maybe noise,
hue whatever it is (Binghi says it is the spirit of
the man whose hair and fat is used) the mosquito
savees it and absents himself for some considerable
time.

“A. 8.” : Some history of the Darling, as
per “Ess” (B. 30/9/’l5), is wrong in parts. W. G.
Spence, in “The History of the A.W.U.,” gives a
true account of the Rodney matter. When she was
burnt she was moored to a gum-tree on an island
off Moorara. A dozen men waded through the mud
during the night of August 20, 1894 (not 1890 as
THREATENING THE IMPOSSIBLE.
SHE: "He said he’d knock your brains out.”
HE: " Oh kid stakes! ’E couldn’t knock
no brains outer ME.”
■“Ess” wrote), and captured 45 “scabs” who were
going to Tolarno. The boat was then burned and
cut adrift. In the case of nine men who were
arrested, 30 witnesses successfully proved an alibi.
A second lot were run in, and they, too, got clear.
Thomas Bonner was eventually given seven years
on a charge of firing the barge, although he was not
within 100 miles of the flare-up. However, that
made no difference in those days, when it was a
crime to be a shearer.
THE TRAIL OF PRAWNS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The gloom of beers that no one shares
Lurked blackly in my mind ;
I wandered with a heart that longed
For converse with my kind.
I wished to march in festive song
With some kin spirit, who
Would help me wake the sleeping earth,
And make it join us two;
To face the morn when it should break
And sing of other dawns.
Then, in my hour of loneliness,
I found a trail of prawns!
I felt my heart in rapture start;
No more I uttered groans.
“Some festive soul this track has left,”
1 said—“most likely Jones !”
Then, blvthe and gay, that festive way
I followed, prawn by prawn,
As through a maze of dizzy streets
A fretwork path was sawn.
In circles wide from side to side
Of many streets it passed,
Till, holding up a falling lamp,
I came on Jones at last!
Two quarts of stout from pockets peeped,
As if they’d like to spill,
And from a parcel in his arm
The prawns were dropping still.
With joy, and prawn, and stout, the morn
Brought up the cheerful day :
We greeted it with jest and song
And laughter full and gay.
And with the break of dawn the thought
Flooded my happy mind:
The genial soul, where’er it goes,
Must leave its trail behind!
And it may lead the hearts whose need
Is such, to newer dawns,
Tf they, within their hours of gloom,
Should find the trail of prawns.
N.S.W.

AUSTRALIA’S FUTURE!
ITS TRADE, ITS INDUSTRIES.
ONE lesson the war has taught is that Australians must make this country as self contained as possible.
They must build up industries that will employ Australian workmen, in lieu of importing goods and sending
money overseas to build up the prosperity of other countries to the detriment of their own National expansion
and prosperity do not follow along those lines. This country calls for more people, but not more imported goods.
Take the Rubber Goods Trade in the Commonwealth. The imports into this country, during the twelve months
previous to the war, totalled over £350,000 and yet there was
No need for one penny of this huge sum to have gone
out of the Commonwealth, except for raw materials.
The sum mentioned meant the employment of thousands of hands, but unfortunately not Australian hands. The
work could and should have been done here, and Australia would have benefited, and users of Rubber Goods would
have benefited, for in addition to keeping the money circulated in the country, they would have had satisfaction,
for despite the influx of foreign-made Rubber Goods, the Australian-made article has gained ground and beaten
the imported goods for Quality, Workmanship and Price.
There’s not one sound reason why Australians
should prefer imported Rubber Goods!
Quality for quality—and price for price—we, as the largest Rubber Manufacturers in the Southern Hemisphere,
can supply your requirements in this direction, and with a better article and at no greater cost—in fact in most
instances at less than the imported goods. Furthermore, we are on the spot to stand by our reputation for
Quality, Service and Satisfaction. The name “ Dunlop” is a household word in Australia to-day, and it ■is
synonymous with the highest standard in the Rubber World. We manufacture:—
MOTOR TYRES
(Pneumatic and Solid)
CYCLE TYRES
VEHICLE TYRES
TENNIS BALLS
RUBBER HEELS
MINING GOODS
SPORTING GOODS
PLUMBERS’ GOODS
ENGINEERING REQUISITES
WATERPROOF GARMENTS and RUGS
RUBBER TILES—MATTING
HOSES-GARDEN, ENGINEERING, &c.
MILKING MACHINE REQUISITES
Etc. Etc.
These goods are worthy of this country and the workmen that make them. They at present command
owing to their reputation for Quality and Service—a big trade throughout the length and breadth of the
Commonwealth—a reputation they have justly earned, and one we are determined to uphold. This trade can
be doubled if unjustifiable preference for imported wares is overcome. Our Mill workers can be raised
from 1,400 to 2,800 and our annual wage sheet increased from £150,000 to £300,000. It’s a result that you can
help achieve, if you will always specify “ DUNLOP ” branded Rubber Goods, and see that you get them. You
thereby not only help yourself by obtaining a superior article every time, but you help to materially assist in
the expansion of the industries of our Country
ARE
YOU
