“Billy T.'s" dale of a Bad Man :
I was walking up Bourke-street with my Jong
friend, Captain Iline, of the tramp ship Helen. Iline
was never a demonstrative seaman, hut to-day he Had
taken his drink in the shadows, and oppression sat
upon his brow like an undertaker’s horse. Suddenly
he stopped in his long- stride, and stood gazing- at
a lean Asiatic who dawdled on the kerb humbly offer-
ing fragile silk handkerchiefs for sale. Captain
Hine took two strides, and wound his large, mari-
time hand in the ear of the pedlar. The coolie
burst into a splutter of unintelligible invective.
“Be damned!” was all ; Captain Iline said, and bo
restored his pipe and went smoking- down Bourke-
street again, towing his captive, whose language, if
comprehensible, would have corrupted the town.
Captain [line found a policeman and explained.
“Nigger sailor off my ship,” he said. “Infernal
scamp ran away. I sail in the morning. Must take
the blackguard. My company is up against a £2OO
fine.” “Blear liar!” squealed the coolie. “Nev"
see. Not know. No come out ship me. Blear
liar.” “Will you tow him, constable, or will I?”
said Captain Hine. The discolored brother was
borne off,' reviling the “blear liar” in odds and
ends of 11 languages. That night I met Captain
Iline, and lie was his old, happy self. “If a captain
loses a nigger in an Australian port his company has
it into him a yard,” he said. “The fine’s
a corker. I simply had to have that coolie
back.” “Well, your luck was good,” said I.
“Why, you ran right into him!” He shook his head
and winked. I am told that this is how it's done
ill many eases.
Commenting on George Reid’s attempt to
boom Australian meat at the. Front in
France, the Age suggests that, after all, the
High Commissioner’s destiny may be to act
as sandwich man for Australia. It won't pay
-—far too much meat in the sandwich.
“Skeeter”: Allegations about the preva-
lence of child-slavery in the cow districts
have my hacking as a school-teacher. One
hoy told me, when I remonstrated with him
for sleeping during lesson, that before com-
ing to school he had gathered between 30
and 40 rabbit traps spread along a line of
nearly a mile, collected and made his catch
ready for the carrier to take to town, milked
live cows, drawn a load of wood and a cask
of water, and walked to the post-office, 11
miles away (father wanted the horse). On
the way lo school he dropped his traps and,
after school, on the way back, -set them.
Later lie penned the calves. Early tea. and
then to the traps again for the* sundown
catch, the most important of all. When lie
go! hack he did home-work if lie had a.iy,
and if not tin' lazy young animal went to
bed for a few hours.
From “Rosna,” in London :—-
The infant London A.V.A. lnid a party the other
night for prominent Maorilanders on this side, the
chief guests being, as far as I could see, Rosina
Buckman, G. B. Lancaster and Private Wellington,
Corporal Auckland, and Lieut. Dunedin, the last three
all war-scarred. It was a jolly little revel
held at the Palace Hotel, Bloomsbury, with supper,
and a speech and song or two. The Buckman star,
with her new grand-opera honors thick upon her,
contributed, and High Commissioner Mackenzie said
a word or two—which was mighty sporting- of him,
since his son had just reached here from the Darda-
nelles, hopelessly blind. The plucky youngster, an
unassuming trooper in a Maoriland battalion, had
to walk five miles to a hospital ship, and when he
reached Egypt his eves were so injured by concus-
sion that they had to be removed. He didn’t men-
tion he had a High Commissioner in the family, and
it was only when a cable of inquiry reached' Cairo
that his identity was discovered.
“Compulsory service is contrary to the
genius of the British race,” says the party
or muddle and drift portentously. But lis-
ten to William Elliott (the best living-
authority on the history of certain English
counties) —-
A thousand years ago, and for centuries after
that, every able-bodied man between the ages of 16
and 60 was subject to compulsory military service.
Landholders had to appear properly armed, but the
poorer sort made such provisions as they could.
The Bulletin doesn't know for certain
what “the genius of the British race" is ; it
merely knows, on the highest authority,
that, when the British race was making it-
self great it not only had to come and carve
—it had also to provide the knife and fork.
“Mark C.”: What is the most numerous
Australian family? There’s one in Youa-
rang (Vic.) which a week or so ago cele-
brated the christening of the twenty-second
child. I’m solemnly assured, too, that there
is a family at Euroa (Vic.) 33 strong, and
that nineteen of the hoys are footballers.
Andrew Fisher lias further explained his
doctrine that a Tory Government should
appoint none but a Tory to be High Com-
missioner. Listen
Positions in Great Britain are filled from the pre-
dominant party, and to say that this was spoils to
the victors was absurd. It was proposed to follow
the same system as under the British Constitution.
What in the name of the sacred gumtree has.
the British Cons’toosliun got to do with the
appointment of G. Reid or ? Andrew
was a miner. Now what would Andrew
have said if a Eat mine-owner, wanting a
mulloeker, had asked Andrew what his poli-
tics were, and on finding that they were
Labor had said that that fact debarred him
from the job under the Br’ish Cons’toosliun?
“Galvin”: Surely the official description
of Peter Lalor, quoted by "Kettledrum” (B.
30/9/T5), must have been wrong. I was in
Ballarat during the great deep-alluvial
boom. Peter was a director of one of the
big Creswick mines, and I, who saw him
frequently with his friend Tnomas Drum-
mond Wanliss, proprietor of the Ballarat
Star, would reckon him as about oft. Oin.,
and not the sft. Min. of the official descrip-
tion. I frequently saw him, too, in the
Chair of the Assembly, and lie always ap-
peared to be a big man in every way,
whether sitting or standing. But what I
remember most about him was the tremen-
dous strength of his jaw. Had lie gone to
South America instead of to Australia that
jaw would have made him the dictator of
some republic of his own creation.
From London :—-
The diary of the Rev. Bobby Kuopwood lias just
been dug up out of the dust. Fragments of it,
copied long ago in llobart, were printed by the Vic-
torian Government in 1879, in a collection of early
records edited hv the late .T. J. Shilling-law. The
man who did the transcript in Tlohart was a rela-
tive of Colonel Collins, the Governor, and that tnav
explain why lie turned a blind eye to the pages
in which Parson Bobby with brutal candor recorded
the relations between Collins and the best-looking-
of the female convicts. In the hitherto-missing pages
Bobby describes a violent scene between Collins
and Lieutenant Lord over an attractive lady-convict
named Mrs. Powers, who was the Governor’s favorite
at the time, a member of his establishment and a
regular diner at his table. Lord threatened to
knock Collins down and then “kick him for falling”;
lie also accused Collins of “keeping Powers as a
pimp,” and used a variety of other language cal-
culated to lead to riot and confusion. Bobby also
mentions that Collins sentenced a soldier to 200
lashes for drinking with a convict on the very dav
that he himself was entertaining- Mrs. Powers at his
own table, lie adds that the soldier and the convict
received 100 lashes each- —a division of the awarded
punishment. The streets in Melbourne and Hobart-
named after Collins seem to be. in urgent need of
rechristening.
Colonel Moore, a Melbourne surgeon, has
succeeded iu supplying an Australian sol-
dier with a new face. Most of the old one
was lost in the Dardanelles.
After all, the mostly-Tory Victorian shire
councils are not entirely hopeless. The other
day the Kara Kara council, consisting
chiefly of hardshell Mallee fringers, had be-
fore it a proposal to go to the Government
and cadge £4OO for some job or other. When
this was urged one councillor upended him-
self and remarked that as they had previ-
ously carried a motion asking the Govern-
ment to economise, it was tough hide to
pass the hat for money. The other coun-
cillors said that that seemed reasonable, and
threw the cadging resolution in the tire.
From Jas. Stewart, of Windsor (N.S.W.) :
Re “Itan’s” par on Queensland’s water-diviners.
The real Mackay can be found, hired or inter-
viewed any day in this district. Young- Charlie
Nicholls is 12 years old, and when looking for water
he extends his arms and walks about the country.
As soon as he comes across, or travels with, under-
ground moving- water, his arms are drawn down to
his sides. He has located 20 wells without a
.failure. True, h.e hasn't been tested on ground that
covers streams more than 40ft. down, but then 30ft.
is the limit for a common or garden pump. If
anyone doubts this he need only carry his doubts
as far as the Freeman’s Reach district, Windsor,
and the inhabitants to a man will relieve him of
them. I am prepared to hand over £lO to any
fund The Bulletin likes if this boy fails to locate
running- underground water, first shot, as far down
as, sav, 35ft., in any part of Australia. No ex-
penses wanted. Now, come on.
Buttons made in France from “galalitli”
are now being sold in Australia. A factory
for the material from which these buttons
are machined was established in the Western
District of Victoria before the war started,
but so far no cards of buttons marked "Made
iu Australia” are on the local market. Gala-
litli (milk-stone) is celluloid. The Vic.
factory makes or made its product out of the
skim-milk which would otherwise go for pigs'
food, and it seems absurd that Australia
should import more pigs’ food when there is
a plentiful supply at hand, ready for treat-
ment by this country’s own wage-earners.
From an anti-conscription pamphlet on
“The Military Menace in Australia” :
An Australian editor, travelling recently on the
Continent, was struck by the absence of men from
the harvest fields of Germany. Women everywhere
were reaping the crops, The men wore busy at
drill. Could one have a better illustration of what
conscription means to a nation ?
Well, the women might rather harvest a
crop of wheat than of widows’ weeds; and
the latter is what Englishwomen hare har-
vested as the result of England's foolish
system of drift and muddle and general
unpreparedness.
THE FAT FLIRT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O fat, flirtatious lady,
You must be forty-two!
’Neath lashes long and shady
I glimpse your eyes of blue!
But, though you flash them boldly,
They don't set me on lire —•
I eye your gambols coldly ,
• For soon, I know, you’ll tire.
Yes, middle-age is horrid
To those in love with love I
Though your desires are torrid,
You’ve aged, old turtle-dove.
So, when awhile you’ve flirted,
You’ll silent sit and stare,
Till Demon Sleep has spurted
And drugged you in your chair.
Staid gentlemen of forty
(Polite, safe gallants all )
Re-peet your instincts sporty.
And, when you challenge, fall;
Your ancient fascinations,
They one and all pretend,
Bear modern innovations—
Till you’re asleep, my friend!
When you are safely snoring',
And look your age and more,
These cavaliers adoring
Converse across Ihc floor.
No jealous, youthful patter
Do these old veterans pass ;
They make a friendly clatter,
And drain the whisky glass.
O fat, flirtatious lady,
' Why don’t you give it best?
You're far from Youth’s A ready l
Your beaux deserve a rest!
E'en as you're smiling sweetly
Sleep flits across the floor
And chloroforms you neatly.
Sleep, flirt! Sleep on and snore!
WESTRAI.IA.
JOHN COMPANY.
if only half a head survive
Beneath the soldier’s bonnet,
Some cheerful doctor will contrive
To put a good face on it!

The “Universal” Drafting; Machine
is adapted to all forms of drafting—architectural, mechanical, structural,
man work and surveys. Thousands are in profitable use in every civilised
country in the world. In Australia we have the. Victorian Railways and
the leading Mining and Engineering Companies using them successfully.
You do better work with a Drafting Machine, because you are not repeatedly
distracted by handling and changing tools and watching their alignment. You
are thus able to concentrate your mind—put in your lines just as fast as
you can think them out.
Besides the speed gained by concentration, the Drafting Machine saves 25 to
50 per cent, in time through mechanical convenience. Summed up, the Uni-
versal Drafting Machine takes the drudgery out of drafting, makes it quick
lasy and—by giving you a chance to give more and closer attention to what
you are drawing—it improves the work.

During the half-year a new City Office was opened in Equitable Buildings, George Street, Sydney, and
the Branches at Cressy and Goroke, in Victoria, were closed. Agencies were opened at Big Brook and
Corigin, in Western Australia, and that at Glenrowan, in Victoria, was closed.
In terms of the Chairman's intimation in the course of his speech at the Half-yearly Meeting in May
last, shareholders are asked to concur in the Directors’ proposal that the Bank’s future contributions to
the Officers’ Provident Fund shall bear a fixed relation to the salaries of the staff from time to time, and
be included in the current expenditure of each Half-year—a desirable reform recommended by Mr. E. J.
Stock, the actuary of the fund.
The number of Officers who have enlisted in the Commonwealth Expeditionary Forces is 178. Of
these 9 have unfortunately been killed in action, 13 wounded, and 18 invalided, viz.:—
KILLED. —Major E. F. D. Fethers, Sergeant H. Bick, and Privates D. M. Anderson, K. R. Brown,
11. R. C. Currie, C. V. Down, K. Harrington, V. B. Reid, and J. G. Turnock.
WOUNDED. —Compy-Sgt.-Major R. Hemingway, Sergt. J. E. 11. Swain, Corporals IT. R. Donnelly,
H. J. Johnston, D. A. McLean, L. T. Paroissien, and Privates F. M. Blackwell, D. H. Brooke,
D. C. Johnston, J. A. Lennie, P. Loxton, J. F. Shawe, and H. W. Trethewey.
INVALIDED. —Capt. John Walstal, Lieut. D. Duff, Sergt. W. A. Luke, Corporals D. Carmichael
and J. W. Kellow, L.-Corpl. R. W. Fincham, and Privates E. R. Etherton, E. 11. ICernot, H. N.
' King, W. D. King, J. E. McPhee, W. Thompson, and B. G. Walter.
In addition to the above there are 7 officers on leave of absence for the purpose of service with the
Commonwealth Military Forces in Australia.
These numbers are equal to about 22 per cent, ofthe Bank’s Staff at the outbreak of the War.
During the half-year the Bank’s contributions to the Red Cross and other War Funds have amounted
to £2200.
The Ordinary General Meeting will be held at the Head Office of the Bank, Collins Street, Melbourne,
on Wednesday, 3rd November, at noon.
By order of the Board of Directors,
E. H. WREFORD, Chief Manager.
The National Bank of Australasia Limited.

BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXIONS
WITHOUT ROUGE.
Ladies who dislike rouge and “make-up"
—and what lady of refinement does not —
often ask what they can do to restore. the
natural velvety bloom of childhood to faded,
sallow, wrinkled cheeks. All such ladies, as
well as those who now have good com-
plexions and wish to preserve them, can
realise their hearts’ desire by getting their
chemist to prepare a lotion composed of
2 07. rose water, 1 dram tincture of benzoin,
and 2oz. flowers of oxzoin. Before using
shake thoroughly, and then apply with a
soft cloth or sponge. When dry brush
lightly with a soft cloth or piece of chamois
leather. If you will do this whenever you
go out you will be astonished at the abso-
lutely natural and refined improvement in
your appearance. This lotion is also excep-
tionally beneficial in relieving and prevent-
ing chapped faces and hands as well as red-
ness due to exposure to wmather.*
POLITICAL
POINTS
PER CONTRA.
HOLMAN: "Here, I’m thinking of giving
you a new alarm dock—to go off at
ten!”
RUNG: " 'Arf a mo'! 'Ow about making a
reduction in me account?”
A Sydney paper last week solemnly
demanded that
If there are any irresponsible outsiders who know
better than the* responsible insiders how to carry on
the campaign. . . .they should be given command of
the naval and military forces.
Now who is this string being pulled for?
Joe Cook or George Reid?
South Aus. Liberal Unionists don’t know
where they are. Ever since they lost the
State elections last March the poor general
secretary, a decent fellow named Freeman,
has been blamed because the organisation
showed its defects alongside the superior
solidarity of the Labor side. Freeman’s
unhappy lot has been worse than the police-
man’s, and now he is told to quit. The
Liberal Union has had three secretaries in
about half a dozen years. The frequent
official changes make the Labor people glee-
fully rub their hands.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Hall: —-
I belong to a Government which, rightly or
wrongly, believes in extending the State’s functions.
Hence naturally the Government hires the
Norton Griffiths syndicate and hands out
£50,000 to the London speculators who are
interested in Great Cobar.
The Tasmanian Parliament has put up
another record. The other night a town-
planning expert brought a magic-lantern into
the sacred Chamber, hung a sheet on the wall
and gave lion, members an illustrated lec-
ture on laying out garden cities. But really
the Commonwealth is much more interested
in laying out the country. What about
that ?
Nielsen’s little business (£-1 4s. a day and
exes.) in ’Frisco has extended itself. A
similar office has been opened in New York,
and P. E. Quinn is in charge. A cabled
report of his remarks on the occasion of the
opening seems to hint that the new agency
was inspired by N.S.W.’s hunger for im-
ports. It hardly seems necessary to assume
that the Yanks will ignore a promising
market, even if that market doesn’t keep an
expensive official round the corner to remind
them of it. What this country really needs
is an inexpensive agent at every corner to
remind it that it ought to be doing a hundred
times more to supply its own wants.
Thomas, the English railway men’s organ-
ising secretary, babbling the other day
against conscription, spoke a bigger truth
than he thought:—
If everybody gave all, the result would be the
breaking down of the curse of militarism.
But it is not the curse of their own mili-
tarism that the Allies are concerned about
breaking down. Their present job is to
break down the German. If they don’t do
it, then the other fellow’s curse of militarism
will get its punch in, and Thomas and other
Englishmen will find themselves called upon
to keep the curse in full blast.
E. S. Keep, president of A r ic. Employers’
Federation, bitterly attacks Labor-leader
Elmslie’s proposal that production should be
increased by adopting the labor conditions
of mining for agricultural and pastoral
property. Says Mr. Keep :—-
Labor’s idea is that you can increase production
and provide employment by increasing taxation on
the land owner.
Labor’s idea is nothing of the sort. Labor’s
idea is that you can increase production and
provide employment by penalising the land-
owner if he doesn’t increase production.
Eimslie’s plan was so absurdly modest, how-
ever, that he didn’t really ask the land-
owner to increase production. He merely
asked him to begin it; the payment of a
paltry £125 a year in wages for every £5OOO
worth of land held would, under his scheme,
save the owner from punitive taxation.
The lily hands of Alban Morley, the long,
pallid, Melbourne barrister, are to take the
Liberal wheel during the next contest for
the Cork) (Vie.) seat in the Reps. The
Women’s National League has finally ap-
proved of Alban Morley; the rickety Lib-
era! machine lias been overhauled for
another burst of speed, and it is hoped that
with adequate lubricating the chariot will
complete the course without breaking down.
That is all that Liberalism may reasonably
expect, however. For his own part, Morley
apparently depends on his record of past
failures in State politics to carry him
through.
•Toe Cook encouraging Young Australia to
acquire a gun :
I sometimes think it would have a tightening-up
effect, if some day a few shells were dropped into
Sydney.
But why wait for the enemy to do this
thing? Why not make it part of the recruit-
ing campaign? Of course a roof would be
blown off and the citizen’s wife and children
sprinkled over the lawn. But then Brother
Cook would be able to call around and rake
his friend off the telephone wire and remark
that, things having been tightened up at
last, the time was ripe to do something be-
sides blow off hot air.
Sydney Telegraph ascended to the top-
most peak of its new skyscraper last week
and wildly yelled that “if anything so alien
to British character as political opportunism
is being allowed to split the Cabinet,” great
would be the disaster. Of course. Oppor-
tunism is the one thing .T. Bull will not
stand, and that is whv Peel (who bolted to
Cobdenism). Gladstone (who suddenly
embraced Home Rule), Disraeli (who
“dished the Whigs” over the franchise),
Salisbury (the gentleman who traded Heli-
goland to the Kaiser and gave him Samoa),
Chamberlain, Balfour and Lloyd-George are
never spoken of. Heaven only knows what
the agitated Telegraph would say if an
Australian newspaper, after virulently de-
nouncing the idea of an Australian navy
and compulsory military training, had later
on the cast-iron cheek to pose as the friend
of these things.
Speaking in the Vic. Assembly on the
McLeod no-confidence motion, Railways-
Minister Mackinnon offered some particulars
of economies in his deDartment. “In De-
cember, 1914,” he said, “we knocked off
360,000 miles per annum. In September
last we knocked off another 760.000 miles.
In the beginning of October 400,000 miles
were knocked oft around Melbourne. While
we are trying to put the finances square we
can’t be particular about a little inconveni-
ence.” On the morning following these Minis-
terial remarks, a daily paper reported that
“while the Railways Commissioners’ special
train was on its way from Ballarat to Mel-
bourne an empty champagne bottle was
thrown from one of the care, near Warren-
heip. It struck a line-repairer named
Michael Quirk and knocked him insensible.”
It will no doubt mean a little inconvenience
to Quirk ; but, dash it all, this is no time
for airing petty grievances—not while the
Commissioners are so earnestly engaged in
attempting to squeeze the price of a few
more cases of wine out of the railway
revenue.
The Vic. Anglican Synod has considered
J,, . totalisator. Dr. Deeper, warden of
Trinity College, moved a vote against
th< machine, objecting “legal approbation
ot the vice of gambling,” and the tally of
the dearly beloved brethren went six to one
in. favor of Deeper and the bookmaker, who
is a, parasite. Randal Alcock, who helps
the firm of James Service and Co. to its divi-
deiuls, was the most emphatic supporter of

the machine, and invited the clergy to say
how they liked their new allies, the para-
sires before mentioned. Archbishop Clarke
and the Dean of Melbourne both brought
their guns to bear on Alcock, but neither of
them even attempted to explain why it is
better for Parliament to permit gambling
through bookmakers, who are active cor-

teachings, the bookmaker has a soul to

point of view, then, the tote ought to have
the gaitered support which it somehow
doesn’t get.
FIRST THINGS FIRST.
COUSIN JACK (well primed with war news):
“Things he lookin' up in the West,

THE S ECOND: “ 'Bout time they did; Coheir

At a time like this it is well to have a
calm, strong man at the head of affairs.
Such a one is Victoria’s Health Minister,
J. Dry Brown. The State is afflicted with
an epidemic of meningitis, and the tale of
deaths, averaging 34 per cent, of those
attacked, is now well over 150 ; but Brown
remains calm. The Health Board, which
has been threatened with reform by three
successive Premiers, avers it has no power
to do anything; and the military authorities
are dealing with their own cases with their
well-known incompetence. In these circum-
stances Dry Brown opines that it would be
better if the press was muzzled, so that it
could not tell the public about the preval-
ence of the disease. Then the public would
make no fuss and no wearied Minister or
official would be harried into doing anything.
play with. Anyhow, if when investigating
the explosive character of certain gases a
student had his nose blown off, thai would
be his funeral. But when a politician mon-
keys with commercial transactions and
blows a gaping hole in the national eashbox,
that is the public’s little trouble. Suppose
little David had fractured his own pocket
with these experiments, would he still be
keen about going on?
The S. M. Herald lays down the law: —
If there are to be combinations these combinations
should be combinations of producers for self-protec-
tion. If prices are to bei controlled they should bo
controlled in the interests of those who mine the
metals, and not in the interests of those who use
them.
It’s a fool of a rule that can't be applied
all round. Employers are the users of
labor. In controlling wages the users’ in-
terests should not be considered. Therefore
in future “arbitration” matters affecting
wages The Bulletin invites the Herald
to he consistent and say that wages should
be fixed in the interests only of the laborers.
If it isn’t prepared to apply the rule to the
miner, the Herald had better throw it over-
board so far as the mine-owner is con-
cerned. It is a rotten rule, in any case, and
overboard is the best place for it.
“Copabella” from the little ohice at the
back :—•
We storekeepers are now buying toys fov Christ-
mas. A year ago the local patriots said exultantly:
“No more German toys after this year!” and we
began to look round for something to take their
place—Australian for choice; British, anyhow.
This year we’ve got them —from Japan. And
what ? The same gaudiness; the same flimsi-
ness; the same spring that winds once (when
the salesman does it) ; the same variety;
and cheaper than ever. Travellers show their sam-
ples in pieces ; and if a careful salesman can't keep
them whole even while he is selling them, what will
the children do? They are economic crimes, these
toys, every one of them; unsatisfactory to the
seller, Ttrfeatisfactory to the purchaser, and a taunt
and a delusion to the child who receives the
dubious gift. But they’re right up to the moment
—lead A.I.F. expeditionaries ; tin motor-ambulances ;
wooden Australia battleships; cardboard hospitals—
and they’re cheap! What does anything matter as
long as things are imported—and cheap?
William Brooks, president of the
Employers’ Federation of N. S. Wales,
repeated at the annual meeting of that body
an ancient, futile protest against December’s
referendum-poll:
When we consider that on the day the referendum
vote is taken some drama may be enacted on the
battlefield, involving the sacrifice of thousands of
our manhood, the prosecution of this political and
party strategem becomes a crime.
Now will W. Brooks or any of his pals be
at the Melbourne Cup?

observed that bis price-fixing experiments

was it contended that merely because there
was an element of danger in an experiment,
such experiment should be abandoned.” It
all depends on the experimenter. School

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The State Budget in South Australia shows that
the income for the coming year will probably amount
to £4,328,000. Expenditure is set down at
£4,838,000, leaving an expected deficit of £510,000.
The population of South Australia is estimated at
100,000.
The S.A. State debt is about £35,000,000.
—Plain fcwt-s.
To us the acme of sublimity
Is just to paint the State vermilion ;
And so we face with equanimity
A deficit of half a million.
Though war and drought may cause disparity
Between incomings and outgoings,
The murky Future takes on clarity
When we arrange for further owings.
This war, that Time’s deep stream is bloodying,
Brings us not doubt nor hesitation;
Our simple methods are worth studying
By all who would “finance” a nation.
Wo first take years of great prosperity,
And count their revenues as normal- -
And then put down, with much celerity,
“Expenditure”—but this is formal,
Because—by some most strange perversity,
Or outside factors left unheeded—
1 n days of affluence, or adversity,
We find that estimate exceeded !
Whereas the revenue, so essential
To Treasurers, power and place enjoying,
Is often sadly differential
And unexpectedly annoying!
This Ancient Problem was confronted,
And, after anxious meditation,
We solved it in a way unwonted
Which, ive think, meets the situation.
This is our modus operand*,
Which outworn methods made a sport of
And knocked the old-time bankers bandy:
We always borrow what we're short of !
N.S.W.
O.C.C.

of your pamphlets I decided on giving Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills a trial, and so
much benefit from their use have I derived
that they are now our family medicine. I
might say, that since undergoing a course

bilious symptoms, and I am never even
troubled with headache now, as I find a pill
or two taken regularly wards off any repeti-
tion of my old complaint. You have my
consent to publish this testimony. I have
been a resident here eight years, and highly
recommend your medicine personally to my
friends, as is well known.”*

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The first day of the V.R.C. meeting should
be full of valuable information for Cup punters if
the Derby is a fast-run race, and the most fancied
of the entrants have a go "for either the Melbourne
Stakes or Hotharn Handicap, the latter being a test
race in which more than one subsequent Cup winner
lias been sent to earn a penalty. But the Derby,
however it may eventuate, is not likely to bring' the
winner into first favoritism for the Cup on Tuesday.
This is not a year when any three-year-old weighted
at 7st. 61b. will be preferred to the older “ehucked-
in” horses, provided that they are fit and fancied on
the day. Besides, Cetigne is already placed on the
7st. 91b. mark, and if the son of Grafton beats "Wal-
lace Isinglass again, with Westcourt, Panacre, Pat-
robas and others in the rear, it will be at least 12
to 1 against a three-year-old capturing the Cup.
This writer, however, expects Wallace Isinglass to
do the trick on Saturday, and start at less than 10
to 1 for the Cup. “Baron” Brown’s colt was not
quite ripe at Randwick three or four weeks ago.
Probably he will do better at Flemington, and prove
himself a stayer when he is asked to go two miles.
At time of writing, the six nags that had most to
say at the finish of the Caulfield Cup look likely
to include the winner of the rich Melbourne ditto
—Lavendo, William the Silent, Cyklon, Garlin, Repu-
tation and Patrobas. Three of them were in the
first four at the finish of the Metropolitan, but one
of these—Garlin—is apparently a non-stayer, so he
may be counted out. Also Reputation (Bst. 101 b.)
is open to the objection that he has to carry a
rather heavy burden for his size, and is quite as
unfavorably situated as Mountain Knight (9st.) and
St. Carwyne (Bst. 131 b.). The top-weights will be
up against some very hot stuff this journey.
The only thing to be said against Carlita (Bst.
41b.) is that she has run her best in long-distance
races when allowed to lead all the way, and even
Bst. 41b. is a trying weight for a pacemaker in
a Melbourne Cup. The time for the two miles last
year was 3min. 26sec. Of the likely other ones
that didn’t run in the Caulfield Oup the writer
prefers Naxberry, Marculfus and Lempriere (a very
likely one) to the mucli-talked-of Christian Brother.
Marculfus (7st. 41b.) has been backed for as much
money as Christian Brother. On his best perform-
ances he looks a very dangerous candidate. But he
is undoubtedly a difficult, sluggish animal for a 7st.
41b. jockey to ride.
As for the well-proved chances —Lavendo (7st.
131 b.), Cyklon (Bst.) and William the Silent (7st.
21b.) —William promises thus far to be just as
sound on Cup day as the other two, or not more
unsound. lie ran like a plucky little horse at
Randwick and Caulfield, and if he should happen
to be in better nick next Tuesday nothing but bad
luck can stop him. William the Silent is a reason-
able tip for a win outright. Wallace Isinglass is
worth backing for a place, on the assumption that
the colt’s A.J.C. Derby running was wrong.
“R.L.F.” : Another little lot to add to the
list of coincidences. On Saturday, October 16, at
Caulfield, the first and second horses in the second
race. Belgian Consort and Irish Comedy, figured as
five and six on the card. In the Cup, Lavendo
(Ist) and William the Silent (2nd) were 14 and
15. In the Steeple, Highland Clan (Ist) and Expia-
tion (2nd) carried the numbers six and seven on
their respective saddlecloths; while in the last race
Fidelio (Ist) and Linass (2nd) were together on the
card as 12 and 13. And yet we can’t pick winners.
A.J.C. (Sydney) notifies tlie cutting down
of prize-money for Villiers Stakes from £lOOO to
£BOO and for the Summer Cup from £l5OO to
£l2OO. These are the principal events at the
Band wick Summer Meeting. Also, the Westralian
Turf Club reduces the value of the Perth Cup
from £2OOO to £l6OO. There’s more to follow
before the war-beast is hamstrung'.
* * * *
Capital has sustained a nasty blow in
Adelaide. A man with a few pounds ready cash can
buy winning- totalisator tickets on the racecourse for
a shade under their value, winners often liking to
avoid the crush and the waiting at a pay-out win-
dow. The 3d. or 6d. difference is generally regarded
as a commission for collecting, but in fact and in
law it is not so, but profit on a purchase. A
bought B’s Devron ticket—announced as winning
235. 6d.—for 235. 3d. Before he could collect a
warning flag went up, pay-out clerks said “Wait a
bit,’’ and presently Devron was disqualified. A
claimed return of his 235. 3d., but B said “No,"
and a Court has said “No,’’ too. Reckoning on a
small profit, it said, but one that seemed to him
sufficient, A had bought a piece of paper, with all
rights attaching to it —if any. It was a straight-out
sale and purchase, and that ends the matter.
“Moana” : Tlie tote has a good chance of
collapsing under the weight of the cash it will be
asked to carry on Warstep in the M.L. Cup early in
November. The faith in the mare for the big event
is almost pathetic, seeing that she will be asked to
hump 9st. 51b. over the two miles. What most
impresses me is that Warstep’s half-brother, Snub,
from the same stable, can sprint and can stay, and
that he got into the race at 6st. 7/6/ He has since
been handed a penalty of 71b. for winning the Dune-
din Guineas, but even with that will go to the post
carrying nearly 21st. less than his brilliant half-
sister, and he will find that allowance mighty handv
at the business end of two miles. Snub is worth
risking money on. If you back Warstep and she
wins, you might get 19s. 6d. back for your £1; but
if Snub lands the bacon for you there will be some-
thing waiting at the tote worth doing a songandance
about.

‘'Gosh": So stupid in liis work and un-
couth in his habits was Denis O’Murphy, recruit,
that the camp authorities, in desperation, fired him
out. Directly his mother heard of the ejection she
put on her bonnet and shawl and caught the first
’bus for Victoria Barracks. “What’s this I ’ear
about me bhoy Dinis?” she angrily demanded as she
stormed the inquiry citadel. The alarmed attendant
summoned the boss, who attempted pacification.
“Yes, yes, madam,” lie said in a soothing sort of
fashion. “Er—Private O’Murphy—ah—the truth is,
Mrs. O’Murphy, your son is—well—er—-rather dull
and—er—illiterate, so to speak.” Relief glowed
over her face like a December sunrise when she saw-
how simple the misunderstanding was. “Illiterate,
yer say!” she exclaimed amiably. “No, no!
Patrick ’is father (rist ’is sowl!) an’ me was married
11 years before Dinis come. Ye’re thinkin’ uv
M icky.”
Passes tlie famous old cricketer, Dr. W. G.'
Grace, at Eltham (Eng.), aged 67. His death pulls up
a root of the game, for there was a time when W.G.
was cricket personified—“the matchless Grace,”
as George Giffen said of him. His first appearance
in Australia was in 1873, as captain of the third
English NI. Great was the excitement concerning-
him, and at Melbourne, where Victoria was repre-
sented by 18 men against the visitors, 40,000 specta-
tors paid 2s. 6d. a head at the gates during the
three days of the match. At Sydney, after 18 N. S.
Wales players had followed the lead of Victoria and
defeated the Englishmen, seven of the latter played
12 of the home men at single wicket. The 12
made 29 ; Grace then went to the crease, made 28,
not out, and, plus two byes, won the game on his
own. He finished the tour with an average of 39.17
runs. In 1882 the great batsman played in his first
“test” match against Australia (Murdoch’s team)
in England, scoring 152 in the first innings and
nine, not out, in the second. Ten years later he
again visited Australia as captain of Sheffield’s team.
But ’ neither, then nor at any other time was he up
to his best form in this country. During the 36
years, from 1865 up to the end of the 1900 season,
he made 51,000 runs, with an average of 43, and
with the ball took more than 2800 wickets at an
average cost of 20 runs each. He was not a graceful
batsman, but as to his soundness and effectiveness
there, have" never been two opinions. On two occa-
sions national testimonials were given him, one in
1879 (£1500) and another in 1896 (£10,000).
Some items from the career of W. G. Grace: 344
runs was his highest score in first-class cricket
(M.C.C. v. Kent), made in 1876... .In the same year,
for , the South of England against 22 of Grimsby, he
made 400 not out, the highest recorded individual
score against 0dd5....1n 1886 he bagged 10 wickets
for 49 in one innings for M.C.C. against Oxford.. . .In
1876 for Gloucester his batting aggregate was 2622
and his bowling total 124 wickets.... Playing for
England against Australia at Kennington Oval, in
1886, he had knocked up 170 runs when the total
score stood at 216....0n three occasions he scored
over a century in both innings of a match....He
made every figure from 0 to 100. .. .In 1895 he
scored 1000 runs in the month of May alone (he
was 47 years old then) . . . .In 26 different seasons he
piled up over 1000 runs....He played in first-class
cricket when he was 15 years old, and scored 32
against an All-England XI. ; and when he was 53
years old he made nearly 1300 in a season (aver-
aging 42) amongst the top-notchers.
When the 1884 Australian Eleven was in England
it met a team of Lord Sheffield’s. Grace was
one of the eleven, and created some feel-
ing by demanding that the gauge be put over Alec
Bannerman’s and Percy McDonnell’s bats,- despite the
fact that they were branded with the names of high-
class English makers. McDonnell’s bat, spread by
hard knocks, failed in the test, and Grace beamed ;
but that beam came off when the gauge was put on
to his bat, and showed it to be broader even than
the Australian’s.

From the annual report of tlie Yic.
Cricket Association: “The season 1914-15 proved
harmonious, progressive and eminently successful. . . .
It was, in fact, a glorious season. .. .and but for
the gloom that has been cast,” etc., etc. Special
praise in the general congratulations over winning
the Sheffield Shield is given to the left-handed
bowler Ironmonger. “Experience,” says the report,
“cannot fail to make him the most deadly bowler
in Australia, and when the war is ended and the
exchanging of trips with the Old Country is resumed,
the probability is he will prove Australia’s best
trump card.” Maybe; but it’s a cruel long way
to Lord’s!
And this tribute: “It is deeply and universally
regretted that death removed the most gifted bats-
man and the most highly esteemed cricketer Aus-
tralia or the world has produced. Victor Trumper
during his brilliant career commanded the admira-
tion of cricketers and cricket patrons in every part
of the dominions.”
T.F. : Central Cumberland cricket club,
which has dropped out of Sydney grade cricket, has
excellent reasons for doing so. Out of 41 active
members the 31st folded away the flannels for the
puttees last week. Better average recruiting, and
claimed as a record percentage, comes from Kelly-
ville, in the orange district outside Parramatta,
where 21 of the 23 members of a local football club
have joined in the rush for the Turk’s scalp.
* * * *
Saturday’s dust-up at Sydney Stadium
between Les Darcy and Jimmy Clabby was almost
wholly one-sided. Except for one round the Mait-
lander held the upper hand all the way and had
he not been too eager the combat would have ended
at the eighth, when Clabby reeled back to the ropes,
his defences down, completely at the other’s mercy.
Of Darcy’s metal there can now be no possible doubt,
for Clabby is a boxer of rare skill, a ring strategist
of exceptional craftiness and holder of a long record
of successful performances among the first flight of
his class in the States. lie brought into the ring
his whole box of tricks, and while he certainly
showed a varied assortment, backed by a fine cour-
age, this writer at any rate is convinced that
Clabby’s tactics over the greater part of the journey
DRIVER TOMMY ADRIAN, swimmer,
note with Field Artillery, 5th Brigade,
shortly going to the Front.
were the worst he could possibly have followed. He
went in wholly for in-fighting from the first round,
and at this the other fellow’s strength and quickness
left his opponent no sort of chance. Thus it went
on for 14 weary rounds, with Clabby mostly on the
run or hanging on in clinches, getting severely
punched and handing back scarcely anything in
return. Then in the fifteenth he uppercut Darcy in
a breakaway, and encouraged by this success livened
up considerably, at least holding his own in two or
three of the remaining rounds. It was rather a
haymaking sort of effort by a man who had been
severely battered, but it afforded sufficient evidence
to show that Clabby’s methods in the earlier stages
had been all wrong. Considerable admiration has
been expressed for Clabby’s skill in weathering the
storm without meeting the disaster of unconscious-
ness, but in a case like this, where the aforesaid
skill didn’t help towards winning, and guns which
might have done so were kept masked, praise of
Clabby’s adroitness can only be regarded as testi-
mony to Darcy’s superiority. After all* Clabby
didn’t pull his gloves on to show that .he could
remain in the ring although beaten.
Arthur Scott, who had not been seen in
the Stadium arena since that decision of his in the
McCoorty-Jeff Smith affair early last year, was back
in his old place on Saturday, and the crowd roared
him a great welcome. Harald Baker, who has been
in the referee’s angle lately, is off to hold a hand
in the Big Game, and hopes to be soon taking a
more one-sided part in the proceedings than he has
been used to. In fact, Lieut. Baker will disqualify
the Turk without the slightest provocation, and fling
him over the ropes afterwards.
Bert Spargo, who inside six months has
battered his way to the top of the feather division,
put all the signs of the Zodiac ov'er Jimmy. Hill at
Melbourne Stadium on Saturday, in spite of the
latter’s advantage of 4-Jlb. and a shrewd head.
Spargo bored in from the start, sending in every
variety of wallop in his catalogue. Hill’s frequent
retaliation was to bang with the left and clinch, in
which position Spargo gleefully tattooed on his
learned friend’s ribs and ear, occasionally varying
the menu of bones by uppercutting half a dozen
times in as many seconds. From the ninth round
Hill’s stock began to slump, and at the end of the
nineteenth his backers were sufficiently satisfied to
pay out. One of Hill’s friends credited him with
superiority in six of the 20 rounds, but Jimmy him-
self reckoned that half a dozen less would have been
nearer the mark. Which proves him to be an honest
lad inculcated with the righteous spirit of truth.
-* * * *
“Bad Australian”: A small, one-sided
squabble over what is termed the boxing champion-
ship of South America occurred in this pueblo last
night, and the following rough translation of the
Los Andes’ report of the proceedings will give you
a good idea of the way in which “el box’’ is run
and reported in this country :
BOX.
El Match, Murray—Priano.
Triunfo ue Murray.
According to announcement, the match de box
between the boxeadors Jak Murray (Norteamericano)
and Ferdinando Priano (Italiano), disputing the
championship of South America, was realised at the
Casino last night. From an early moment the house
was full, so that not a single place remained. Ter-
minated the preparations which the case required,
the pugilists appeared and were introduced to the
public.
Round 1. —After some brief moments Murray,
with a straight from the left, touched the mandi-
bula of his opponent, producing “un clinch.” Sepa-
rated, Murray entered again with a new straight
of the left, also on the mandibula (jaw).
Round 2.—Murray directed a straight of the left
and an invitation of the right (feint), and a for-
midable straight at the mandibula. Priano at-
tempted an attack on the estomago, which was side-
stepped with disdain by Murray, who then entered
with a straight of the right, which touched Priano
very seriously. The clinch being again produced
the men were separated by the referee. Priano
attacked and was again side-stepped with much
asperity, and it was noted at this stage that Murray
had a marked advantage over Priano.
Round 3.—Priano is attacked of left and right
simultaneously many times, which he answered with
very weak blows that gave no result. In this
round. Murray showed his grand superiority, and on
two occasions refrained from hitting nis opponent.
Round 4.—Priano received various straights, and
fell twice, lifting himself with much difficulty. He
replied weakly and Murray managed to secure an
uppercut in clinch.
sth, and last, round.—Priano tried to repel Mur-
ray with one arm, as the other bad been injured in
a fall, and he received several straights and crosses
until he fell nokout. The regulation 10 seconds
were counted, and thereafter Murray was proclaimed
champion of South America.
The encounter in general was devoid of the in-
terest which it merited, as from the beginning the
superiority of Murray’s style was apparent to all.
The conqueror during the whole time demonstrated
the most gentlemanly feeling, even at times to the
extent of refusing to strike liis opponent. The con-
queror was applauded with great heat by the public.
That’s the scrap for the championship of a con-
tinent, and I as one of the public who didn’t
applaud, have to say that the promoters and the
gentlemanly Jack Murray should be handed six
months’ calaboose for the tired feeling they engen-
dered.
* * * *
“La Blanche” : Re Frank Slavin and the
statement that he has joined the ranks in France
(“.John P.,” R. 21/10/’l5), Eugene Corri recently
said of his (Slavin’s) fight with Peter Jackson at
London National Sporting Club, in May, 1892, when
Jackson won in 10 rounds: “I doubt very much
whether any man living would give that fight second
place to any other heavyweight fight.” Corri, who
was in Slavin’s corner, has given a telling descrip-
tion of the terrible blow which nearly settled Jack-
son in the sixth round:—
“With explosive suddenness Slavin seemed to com-
pletely overwhelm Jackson as he forced him to the
ropes. This done, the white man stepped back a
pace and summoned the whole of the resources for
a blow which seemed to spring from the calves of
his legs and upwards to the muscles of his right
shoulder and right arm. I have never seen such an
expression of consummate deadliness upon a human
face as that which spread across the features of
Slavin at this crucial stage. ITe threw himself at
the black man like a battering ram, landing a
terrific punch under the heart which made Jackson
gasp and groan audibly. The blow echoed through
the building. It literally doubled Jackson up like
a shut knife. . . .With characteristic ingenuousness
Jackson let his seconds and all within earshot know
how terribly Slavin’s blow had stricken him....
‘lf he hits me like that again I am done,’ he
gasped.”
If that wallop had been delivered earlier in the
round Corri reckons nothing could have saved Jack-
son ; but coming near the gong Peter had time to
recover, and he never gave his opponent another
chance.
* * * *
“Micko”: Some bloke wants to know if
the sports of Britain are getting war-busy enough.
Well, I dunno, but, strewth! look at this
written by John Murray, editor of Boxing. He was
discussing Victor McLaglen, the bloke who stood
up to Johnson (I dunno whether he got John’s per-
mission or not) for six rounds, at Vancouver, when
the big coon was at his best. McLaglen holds a
commission in an English regiment, which knocks
Murray apparently. Says he: “In some respects it
is a pity that Victor should have felt he had to
go, or, rather, to do him justice, that he should
have been so keen on joining the army. For it '-'ls
impossible to say how the tear may affect his pro-
spects.” I leave it to j T ous—isn’t that the blanky
limit!
* * * »
The Bowling Association of N. 8. Wales
has decided upon its season’s programme, which has
been framed with a desire to do the impossibler—
please everybody. It may work in this instance,
as the portly sports do not worry much about
details, although a terrible lot of froth is blown off
in preliminaries. The difficulty in this State seems
to be that the grass won’t act contrary to the laws
of nature. Hence, instead of commencing play in
a sensible month, such as September would be,
players have to wait until December; and
when other States have half finished their compe-
titions the mother State makes a commencement.
Can’t somebody tell how to make couch grass spring
earlier in the season?
* * * *
Two East Maitland (N.S.W.) lads shone
conspicuously at the combined High School cham-
pionships at Sydney last week. W. Hutton won the
100yds. and 220yds. sprints and K. Harbison the
broad jump (19ft.), high jump (sft. 4iin.), and
pole vault (9ft. lOlin.). Sydney Technical won
the championship with 33J points; East Maitland
second, 21 points. The Maitlanders took the honors
in the junior championship.
THE STRAIGHT GRIFFIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bertha, at the private bar,
Took my order with a smile,
Saying to the boj-s, “Ta-ta!”
As they left her for a while.
Bertha at the private bar
Deems herself a bit of style.
Touts and jockeys, small and young,
List enchanted to her quips ;
Bertha has a nimble tongue,
Dimpled cheeks and pouting lips,
And the jockeys, small and young,
Give her arms audacious nips.
Bertha learns a lot of things
Touching gee-gees on the job ;
When the dead-bird spreads its wings
Bertha bids me risk ten bob.
Oh, she learns a lot of things
From the rowdy racing mob !
She gives me her latest news.
I, too critical and cold,
State my pessimistic views
Re the tale she doth unfold ;
Still I find that Bertha’s news
Oft is worth a bit of gold.
Talking of the Cup to-day
With an air of mystery,
In her confidential way
Bertha whispered this to me:
“Wallace Isinglass, they say—-
Back it—win and 1,2, 3 !”
I replied in accents low,
Accents crafty and subdued:
“Why not tell me all you know,
Give me further mental food ?”
Bertha answered, soft and slow:
“Wallace Isinglass is good.”
Many other tips I’ve heard
Coming straight from “stable” source,
Each of them the deadest bird
Ever raced on any course.
(Most of them had names absurd,
Quite unsuited to a horse.)
Wallace Isinglass ! The name
Lacks the sound of sense or wit ;
Yet upon the scroll of fame
This will probably be writ.
Foolish name! —But all the same,
Like a fool, I’m backing it.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

NO STROPPING , NO HONING
“II.O.” : The statement of the Melbourne
Royal programme that the comedy spirit of
"Nobody's Widow” is “really more 'Frenchy'
than American” leaves my boiled shirt limp
with astonishment. The blatanc-y of the
Yankee and the delicacy of the Gaul are as
unlike as Berlin and Paris communiques,
and this musical comedy without music or
legs does nothing to bridge the gulf. Where
French sentiment is champagne that of the
U.S.A. is ginger-beer; French humor lias
the subtlety of phul-nana and Amurkan the
obviousness of a boiling-down works ; where
one treads lightly the path of naughtiness,
the other stumbles clumsily; and whereas
Alphonse tells a story and finishes it at the
end, or a little before, Cyrus B. Hoggen-
heimer shouts the yarn, and having told it
adds a couple more acts. After the first
half-hour “Nobody’s Widow” scintillates like
a piece of blotting-paper.
“Moana” : During the past theatrical sea-
son iu Noo Yark five plays reached the •"00-
night mark. They were “Twin Beds,” “It
Pays to Advertise,” “Potash and Perl-
mutter,” “On Trial,” and “The Law of the
Land.” “Potash and Perlmutter” headed
the list with over 600 nights; but when the
last mail left it was about to be replaced by
some more of the same stuff with the same
characters under the extended title of “Pot-
ash and Perlmutter Incorporated.”
“Monkbarns”: “Whittington” has been
performed more often in Melbourne than any
other pantomime theme. Two theatres
had it in one season 34 years ago,
the Royal and the old wooden Prin-
cess’s, where the brothers Marshall were
in the caste with Whyte, and Annie
Taylor took Dick. I passed little Ted
Marshall in Swanston-street just a
minute before he was killed by the new-
fangled tram, rounding the Hospital corner.
Ted was one of the three Alehouse cronies in
the original Melbourne- “Silver King,” with
Sefton and Daniels. Freddy Marshall, Ted’s
brother, was a genius, as Titheradge rightly
says, naming him as the only conspicuous
member of Garner’s Comedy Company, which
opened with “Friends.” Fred’s mind went,
lie tried to rehearse Touchstone in London,
with Mrs. Langtry, for America, but had to
give up. His Quilp was astonishing. The
Whittington pantomime was first given in
Melbourne at the Iloyal, during Christmas,
1857. The Leopold Family made their first
appearance in the harlequinade. Fanny
Morgan, said to be Lambert’s niece, amazed
the public as Dick, with an imitation of
Joey Gougenheim (Josephine Geoghegan),
then in the acme of favor at the Princess’s,
with her sister Adelaide, supported by
Rogers. 11. N. Warner, Fawcett and Jimmy
Milne.
After a long spell in Australia following
upon the death of brother Jim, Charles Mac-
Mahon takes the Maoriland road again with
the cino. This time it is an exclusive set
illustrating, as far as the Censor will allow
(and in this case he has given a good deal
of latitude), the work of the Navy. Australia
will see the films after Maoriland.
London Sporting Times, arguing that
financing musical-comedy is a sheer gamble,
gives what it claims to be the correct figures
for one week of the enormously successful
“Watch Your Step” at the Empire. Takings.
£2BOO. Running expenses, £2000: author’s
fees, £430; written off cost of production
(£8000), £2OO. Profit. £l7o—which -a
merely trifling drop in the attendances would
turn into a loss. And 40 weeks to go before
the initial cost of producing is written off!
“Stargazer” : For a time I figured as press
representative with a variety eo., and just
before dead weariness assailed me there came
an infant prodigy, a small girl with a gift
of mimicry. It was this sweet child who
said to me: “Pa wants watchin’. That’s why
nia’s here. Pa is always giving shillings to
actors. He’s so kind to animals.” I asked
her if any of her relations had histrionic
gifts. “Oh, yes,” she said. “It was pa bred
the cow with seven legs. We’re a very
artistic family.”
“Mid-Channel”: If Australian managers
are to be excused for “glutting the market
with Yankee dramatic goods” (B. 23/0/T5l
they are not to be excused for inundating
Australia with Yankee trash. The modern
plays of Pinero (“Letty,” “The Thunder-
bolt,” “Mid-Channel,” “Preserving Mr. Pan-
mure” and “The Mind the Paint Girl”) have
all been big successes in the U.S.A., yet
one performance of “Mid-Channel” and a
season by an indifferent company of “His
House in Order” is all Australia has seen of
the modern Pinero. The reason for the some-
what tepid reception in England of the plays
mentioned is that Pinero has drawn his pic-
ture of the various types of English life with
too deadly an accuracy. The idle rich of
London who live in flats and have no time fc v
“brats of children” are not fond of being
caught at the looking glass unawares. I heard
a theatrical manager say: “Oh, so-and-so is
a beautiful play, but there’s isn’t enough
muck in it for Australia.”
“J.F.H.” recalls the pleasures of the last
great wartime : —-
In 1870 George Leybourne was singing “Up in a
Balloon, Boys,” and “If Ever I Cease to Love.” Tile
latter was distributed in many unauthorised versions.
No song ever written lent itself so readily to local
allusions. The “Great Vance” was warbling “He's
a Pal of Mine” and “Walking in the Zoo.” Harry
Rickards, who had not then discovered Australia and
its Tivoli gold-mines, was giving “Costermonger .Toe ’
and “I Wish I Was a Fish.” “Tommy Dodd” was
having its boom mainly by reason of its rollicking
chorus. The Alhambra that year lost its license for
permitting the naughty Parisian “Can Can,” which
would now be voted almost too dull for a Young
Ladies’ College “speech-day.” Negro minstrelsy was
in its zenith. No fewer than five big burnt-cork com
panies were drawing crowded houses in London, not
counting a. troupe of “Scotch Niggers” . who were
doing a turn in the halls. Salaries were very small.
No more than £lO a week was drawn by men like
Leybourne and Vance. It was not until 187 S that
this figure was raised to £2O, the “great McDer-
mott” securing that sum on account of the pro-
digious popularity of his anti-Russian ditty of
defiance: “We don’t want to tight, but, by Jingo,
if we do.”

A Remarkable Pen Picture
of Australian Bush Life
and an Absorbingly Interesting Story.
A full-size novel of 348 pages, printed on a special, light-weight paper, and hand-
somely bound in full cloth, with picture wrapper in three colors, specially painted bv
Mr. I -.ouis McCubbin.
Read what the Age says:—
“An intimate acquaintance with Australian conditions, both ‘out back’ and in the city, is
revealed in ‘The Lure of the Land,’ and it has been turned to good account in the production "of a
genuinely interesting- novel. The picture of the drought-stricken station, with which the book opens,
is impressive in its realism, and the tragic sequel to little Mary Brown’s night ride to bring the
doctor is in melancholy keeping with the sombre prelude. But brighter days are in store for the
self-reliant young heroine, whose unhappy experience in childhood has but strengthened her courage and
resourcefulness: and, bringing her broken-down father with her, she faces the task of earning- a
livelihood in Melbourne. After making a start as a typiste, Mary shrewdly sees the possibilities that
exist in the purchase of seaside and suburban allotments. Her first investment is a modest one or
flO, hut it is the beginning of a steady march to success. Not that the fair student in land valiu-s
is engrossed in the pursuit to the exclusion of other interests, for sentiment has its claims, and heart-
aches have to be suffered before the haven of contentment is reached. The story moves brisklv
throughout, and the numerous people in its pages are all clearly drawn.”
“The Lure of the Land” i.s an attractive book that will please you. We think it
one of the best Australian stories we have read for some time, and can recommend it
to you both for your own reading or to send to a friend outside Australia.

BEFORE THE MIRROR.
By “JEANNETTE.”
‘‘Jeannette,” the well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of “The Book of Beauty,”
will be pleased to help and advise any reader on matters of the toilet. Queries should be accom-
panied by a stamped addressed envelope, and directed tc “Jeannette,” c/o Before the Mirror,
43 Gray’s Inn Road, London, W.C., England.
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and all of
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow over the
lives of many of us women, and while, perhaps, a few the more stoical amongst
us —can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, we may all, with a little
care and forethought, succeed in preventing the results of worry from being notice-
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat will, however, be
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real need of help in toilet
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown
method of retaining, or regaining, that beauty of face and figure which is the birth-
right of every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present,
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Some Hints about the Hair.—An Excellent
Shampoo. —Few women realise how all-im-
portant a really good shampoo is if the hair
is to be kept in perfect condition. To shampoo
the hair properly, shake it down, comb it out,
then dip the lingers into a little pure olive or
almond oil, and thoroughly massage the scalp.
Then, mix a teaspoonful of stallax granules
in a cup of warm water. Fill a basin with
warm water, into which the ends of the hair
can fall, and shampoo the stallax mixture into
the roots in the usual way. Rinse, and dry
by fanning, or in the open air. if the weather
permits. Should the hair be exceptionally
greasy, the oil massage of the scalp should be
omitted. Stallax can be bought at any chem-
ist’s in quarter-pound sealed packages; it keeps
indefinitely, and one package is sufficient, for
twenty-five or thirty shampoos.
A Tonic for Thin or Falling Hair. —When the
hair falls unduly, or has become thin and im-
poverished. a tonic should be applied every
night for six or eight weeks. To make a
simple and inexpensive but most . efficacious
lotion, at home, take a quarter of a pint, of
bay rum and add to it one ounce of boranium.
Shake thoroughly, and leave for thirty
minutes, then strain, and add sufficient water
to make half a pint. Every night, massage
the scalp for ten minutes, going over the
entire head, then dab the mixture into the roots
with a soft sponge. Regular brushing for a
few minutes every morning will also help to
improve the condition of the hair.
The Question of Grey Hair.—The arrival of the
first grey hair is always a worry to a woman,
for, while silver hair is undoubtedly beauti-
ful. iron grey or streaked tresses are far
from being so, and they are not only unbe-
coming. but a sure tell-tale of age in these
days of eternal youth. It is not generally
known that one ounce of tammalite. procur-
able at the chemist’s, mixed with four ounces
of bav rum, makes a most satisfactory lotion
for restoring grey hair to its original color.
All that is necessary is to apply the lotion
night and morning with a soft tooth-brush.
In a few days the hair will be found to be
gradually regaining its proper shade.
To Wave the Hair.— Never wave the hair with
hot irons—if you value the beauty of your
tresses. The heat makes the hair harsh and
brittle, and in the case of fair or auburn hair
entirely spoils the color, while it undoubtedly
hastens the advent of grey hairs. To give a
wave to straight hair it is only necessary to
damp it with silmerine, and either plait it
tightly, tying the ends, or arrange it in wav-
ing pins for a short time. A couple of ounces
will last quite a long time.
A Greasy Skin anti Blackheads. —A greasy,
oily skin with blackheads and enlarged pores
is a sore trial, and many a girl goes through
life with her appearance entirely spoiled with
these unsightly blemishes, which may now be
so quickly and effectively removed with but
little trouble. To remove blackheads, and
the dull, dirty look of the skin which acconi
panies them, dissolve one stymol tablet in a
glass of hot water. Allow the effervesceucc-
to subside, then dip a small, soft sponge into
the liquid and bathe the face, heave on the
skin for a few minutes, then dry with a towel,
and the blackheads will come right off. For
a greasy skin, bathe the face three times a
week with stymol, and spray with cold water
every morning, using a vulcanite throat spray.
The Secret of a Good Complexion. —The secret
of a good complexion is so simple, that were
it only more widely known there would be
no such thing as a rough, blotchy skin.
Nature has decreed that the outer cuticle, when
it shrivels and dies, shall be removed, but so
manv women fail to realise this. To remove,
by absorption, the dead outer skin, and pre-
serve the complexion in all its pristine beauty,
mercolised wax is recommended; ordinary-
creams and lotions being quite ineffectual for
this purpose. Smear the wax over the face
and neck, rub it gently into the skin, and
leave for the night. In the morning wash il
off using a good soap, such as Pilenta, and
all the dead skin will bo removed with the
wax. Then apply a lotion to remove any
trace of greasiness and brace up the skin,
making it firm and white. The lotion can be
made at home quite easily, by mixing one
ounce of cleminite in four tablespoonfuls ot
hot water. Bottle, and, when cool, apply to
the face with a soft sponge or the tips of the
fingers, allowing it to dry on the skin.
To Remove Superfluous Hair. —Superfluous
hair is the bane of many an otherwise pretty
woman’s life. Sometimes it is due to hereditary
tendencies; with others, it is the result of had
soap or greasy face creams; but be the cause
what it mav. 'the result is always disfiguring
To remove the growth, apply a little powdered
plieminol. First dust the skin with powdered
alum, then mix the pheminol with cold water
to a paste, which should be spread on the
skin with a bone knife. Leave on for two
minutes, then wash ofl. About one ounce
should be sufficient for the most stubborn case.
Powdered pheminol can be obtained from any
reliable chemist, and its use not only re-
moves the growth but eventually kills the
roots permanently. U

Few Australians have pursued the dogs
of war so far in recent years as Lieut-
Commander Bracegirdle, reported wounded
in the Dardanelles. He got a start in ISIKS
when he went to China. Then he attended
the Boer war. Each time he was with N. S.
Wales forces. He volunteered immediately
the Hun war broke out, and was ordered to
Rabaul last year. A few months ago he
started from Australia for France in com-
mand of a naval unit known as a bridging
train, but the little force was diverted to
Gallipoli. Bracegirdle’s father is a survivor
of the old Marine Board, and one of Syd-
ney's most ancient mariners.
A SQUARE-DINKUM LOVE LYRIC.
Sing me a nice spooney song, darling,
Sing me a nice spooney song,
A song of sighs and genuine passion to tear my soul
asunder,
Not merely a soupy, sloppy song
Like Shelley wrote when he felt all wrong;
Not a song of milky sentiment
To dazzle my eyes like meat in Lent;
But a fierv, eloquent, passionate song,
To lift me up and cart me along,
And place me amid the deities’ throng •
In fact, a nice spooney song, darling,

Poetic Diary Verses from ■ childhood To manhood.
THE LOST SCHOOL PRIZE
(Recollections of childhood)
’Twas on the verge of Christmas,
And flowers in grand array
Did decorate the schoolhouse
To celebrate the Day ;
I’d proved a slowful pupil
And to my great surprise
I was presented by the master
Witn a gilt edged little prize
Which for years 1 treasured
When by mistake was cast
Into a pile of rubbish
My token of the past;
I endeavored for to find it
Rut my effort was in vain;
It had vanished from the refuse
Of the dust bin in the lane.

HEALTH INSURANCE.
Some people are naturally thin. There is
also a natural pallor, but most people who
are both thin and pale are far from well, and
they need a tonic.
Many people neglect to take a tonic until
they get so sick that a tonic is not sufficient,
just because the demand of the debilitated
body is not insistent enough, x'he pale face,
weak nerves, enfeebled digestion, are
neglected until the point where pain or
actual breakdown requires medical treat-
ment. A tonic taken in time is the best
health insurance. It supports the overtaxed
system, the worried nerves until nature can
make repairs.
Build up the blood and you are sending
renewed health and strength to every part
of the body. The appetite is improved, the
digestion is toned up, there is new color in
the cheeks and lips, you worry less, become
good-natured where before you were irri-
table, and you find new joys in living.
Tonic _ treatment is useful in dyspepsia,
rheumatism, anaemia and nervous disorders.
In many cases it is all the medical treat-
ment that is required.
Free booklets on the blood, nerves and
diet will be sent on request by the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney. Your own
chemist sells Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.*
Caused by Carelessness.
If you are constipated it is your own
fault. It is caused by carelessness, and can
be corrected by cultivating careful habits.
Diet is of the first importance, then exer-
cise and the use of a laxative until healthful
habits are established. Harsh purgatives
are harmful and make matters worse, but
Pinkettes, the little pink ovals prepared by
the Dr. Williams’ Meuicine Co., Sydney, give
nature the gentle assistance required and
they really do correct constipation. If the
laxative you are using is unsatisfactory try
Pinkettes. •*

IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Teeth
There are no teeth like those furnished hi
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve .their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/-tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted.

IN
BANANA LAND
“Itan”: The Chamber of Fossils has
emasculated the Compensation Bill by de-
stroying the clauses which declared the in-
surance business to be a State monopoly.
With only three Labor men in the Council
the Government has a poor show of forcing
its radical legislation through, and, accord-
ing to a Ividston Act, Bills must be rejected-,
twice before the question at issue can go to
the people by referendum. That means a
short session early next year to complete
the details of an appeal. Ryan is deter-
mined that at least two questions shall be
submitted to the electors, and one of them’
will decide the fate of the second House.
It’s about time.
Archie Human, teacher of the Coen’
State school, up near Cape York, has put
down chalk and duster to go and help Bill
and Jim. Hurman filled the place that the
pedagogue of old was expected to fill —was
physician, lawyer, dentist, chemist and
schoolmaster to all the Coen country. His
shelf of bottles of medicine was hardly less
a part of his stock-in-trade than his
geography and grammar. He will do good
work where lie’s going, but that won’t be
much of a change, for he has been doing a
lot'of it in a place where it was a matter
of a six days’ journey to get a doctor out
and back. .
“W.G.8.”: Just when the A.W.U. was
kicking up a row and demanding a K hite
Australia in the sugar industry, I picked up
the paper to see an announcement to the
effect that Ah Yam was applying for the
transfer of his hotel license to C. Ah Tie
for the premises known as the Canton Hotel
in the mining district of Thornborough.
Frankly, isn’t, it about time that the North-
ern and Western unionist either repudiated
the White Australia doctrine altogether or
accepted it consistently? At present he
merely insists that nobody shall have tire
doubtful benefit of cheap yellow labor out
himself.
“Politicus” : Passage money for the year’s
assisted immigration to Queensland has just
been reduced from £22 500 to £12,500.
Apropos of this reduction Premier Ryan
remarked that the Government did not
want any able-oodied immigrants who were
capable of serving at the Front. He
added: “Immigration would not cease abso-
lutely, but no one would be allowed to
come as an assisted immigrant who was
