capable of serving.” But this was no-
reason for slaughtering the vote. Tne
£22,500 (a trivial sum for so vast a country
to spend on immigration) could easily have
been spent in bringing out middle-aged men
with families, domestic, servants, and so
forth. The fact of the matter .is the Queens-
land Labor party is vat heart opposed to
immigration, and just cut out of
its platform the 'insane plank which declared
for the stoppage of State aid. Australia is
sending away the pick of her young men by
tens of thousands, and her semi-demented
State Governments are preventing the resto-
ration-of the balance by discouraging the
influx of the human factor that can alone
make good the losses. Meantime the empty
spaces gape an invitation to less desirable
immigrants as much as ever they did. The
attitude is worthy of Bedlam —or of a State
Parliament.
Treasurer Theodore anticipates a rise in
ordinary expenditure for the coming finan-
cial year of nearly half a million pounds—
or nearly one pound per head for every soul
in Bananaland ! It’s no use preaching thrift
•“to Queensland on Monday and inviting Hol-
man to lecture there on Tuesday.
Minister Adamson, in justification of a
rise of SJ per cent, in the wages of railway
men: —
I found there was a serious difference in favor of
the men emoloyed outside the Department 1 have
faced it squarely, and the men have given me credit
for having faced it as no other man has faced it.
We must increase their wages to the rates lulmg
outside.
This is precisely the brand of “courage”
with which Jack Sc-addan has been filled tor
some years, and which has resulted in an
accumulated 1\ estralian deficit of lv mil-
lions. The truth is that Queensland rail-
way men are the aristocrats of Northern
Labor, and for every vacant billet you could
get 50 applicants in 24 hours. There could
be no better proof that even in the past the
railway servant has been much better off
than the general taxpayer; yet the general
taxpayer is now to be called upon to make
the Labor aristocrat's position better still.
And the Minister calls it “courage” !
The recent State generosity to railway
employees in. Queensland is, of course,
counterbalanced by the new Budget propo-
sals to raise fares and freights. The town
worker, however, will not suffer, for the
increased charge will not fall on toilers who
live adjacent to the cities and take season
tickets, but on the country population en-
gaged in primary production. Go on the
land, young man —go on the land!
A proud man is Charles Lewis, of Too-
woomba. He , has a family of seven sons,
of whom four are in camp, training, while
two more have applied to be enlisted, the
seventh intends getting into the ranks as
soon as they will take him, and the old man
is"under strong suspicion of wanting to push
in, himself.
“Weemurra” : The Estimates provide £470
for the water-diviners whom Bananaland is
going to put on their trial. The salary is
poor, but £2 a day as travelling expenses
and a free, railway pass ought to console
them. I didn’t see any provision for a
Christian Scientist in the Health Depart-
ment, but I expect it’s somewhere in the
Occult and Witchcraft division.
The Tragedy of Lou.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We waited long an’ eagerly, as young folks allers do,
To see wot sort o’ luck we’d ’ave w’en all wuz
worried through;
O’ course a boy wuz all ’is thought (the men is all
the same) —•
A boy ’oo’d foller in ’is steps an’ bear ’is daddy’s
name;
I’d nothin’ ’gin a boy meself, but, if it wuz a girl,
1 think a smile would find the way around my lips
to curl.
A month ago the stranger came, orl right from off
the cruise;
The bonus landed Friday, last, an’ Bill is on the
booze!
An’ Willyum is his little name, as we agreed it
should,
In compliment ter Willyum’s dad (o’ course that s
understood) ;
’E’s got the look o’ brazen-bold a-lurkin’ in ’is eye,
The sort o’ boy that’s got the grit ter ’old ’is own
or die ;
I wuz so filled with ’appiness I couldn’t wear a
frown
No matter ’oo wuz lookin’ black or wot wuz lookin’
down.
But now me cup’s wiv bitter dashed, a cloud is on
me views,
For, since the night o’ Friday last, my Bill is on
the booze.
The neighbors all wuz good an’ kind, an’ called me
lucky Lou,
An’ ev’ryone ’oo gave a call would ask wot she
could do;
An’ Missis Jones, of Ginger’s Lane, she sez a finer
boy
(’E turned the scale at nearly nine) wuz never
mother's joy;
But now I know they whisper low: “ ’Er man is on
the licker :
A burnin’ shame to leave her so an’ spend the note
on shicker.”
J ’ides me tears in bubby’s neck — ’e ’asn’t ’eard the .
news
That since the night o’ Friday last ’is Dad is on the
booze.
An’ Bill is not the worst o’ men. ’E's mostly good
ter me ;
’ls weakness is ’is fellow-man ’n’ soshability;
’E simply met his mates an’ sed: “I m shoutin,
coves, to-day ;
We christened bub this mornin’, an’ I’m feelin’ sort
o’ gay.
Just nominate yer pizen, boys, an’ drink ter )\ ill-
yum’s luck —
’E’s orl yer fancy painted ’im, an’ quite ’is mother’s
duck.
I wouldn’t call ther Queen me aunt, or wear the
Kaiser's shoes;
So fill yer pewters up again!” an’ Bill is on the
booze.
’Ow is it somethin’ alius comes yer cup. o joy to
spile?
W’y can’t I drink the comfort in I find in bubby’s
smile?
’Ow bent I wuz on dressin’ ’im just like a little lord
In orl the giddy vanities my pocket could afford ;
An’ Bill wuz just as keen as me —but now es clean
fergotten,
An’ took up with that soakin’ crowd, a low-down
lot an’ rotten.
O Gawd! Yer rod wuz never meant a mother's
. heart ter bruise,
But at the core she’s bleedin’ sore —since Bill is
on the booze!
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP
Brisbane, October 23. 1915.
My Dear Tii argomin dah,—
The Governor, chaperoned by her Ex.,
flirted with a flower fete this afternoon at
Claytield, and on Tuesday , night the pan
escorted each other to the Orchestra
Society’s concert. What with a few otlie
strenuous jobs, and dressing for dinner anti
so on, I don’t know when he gets any time
for himself. I haven’t seen him at a single
picture-show since he arrived. Coming bar
to the concert, Betty Cockburn was the lacii
soloist, and her rich contralto resounded m
“Softly Awakes My Heart.” I don't know
the name of the carrying firm that remover
the flowers heaped upon her. The other fea-
ture of the show was the orchestra s playing
of Elgar’s “Carillon.”
Lena Hammond’s pupils give a concert
on November 20 for the wounded soldiers
fund. The same fund will benefit by a sale
of work that the Arts and Crafts Society
announces for October 27 and following day -.
Members of the Art Society and pupil.-
of the art classes at the Tech, tarewellec
Godfrey Rivers in the studio he lias worked
in for so many years. W. H. Campbell
president of the Art Society, handed over a
leather suit-case and other traps. Beton
Mr. Rivers, who is a very shy artist, could
escape, Mr. Morris, director of the Tech., in
sisted upon him taking an address and a
book filled with the names of the art pupils.
A wristlet watch for Mrs. Rivers was in-
cluded in the booty. .Winifred Burston,
Gwen Stanley. Mary Grant and Air. Temple-
ton gradually chased the hunted look from
the guest’s countenance with songs and in-
strumental music, and refreshments brought
about a complete reconciliation.
Miss Kate Caiman arranged a juvenile
dance on Wednesday night in aid of the
wounded soldiers’ fund ; and to-night some
of Miss St. Ledger’s pupils are following the
good example.
A committee of women is busy arranging
a scheme to provide Christmas toys for
kiddies whose fathers are too busy at Galli-
poli or Somewhere to attend to the joyous
and ancient ceremony. Lady Goold-Adams
is the patroness, and Mrs. Ryan president.
Freda Sternberg is the sec., and Mrs. Stan-
ley Chapman the treasurer. The latter is
jiist itching to start the receipts side of her
books, so please hurry.
A young Queensland soldier who is in an
English hospital after a bout with enteric
lost the letter which contained the address
of some English cousins* To a visitor he
confided this fact, and the visitor mentioned
it to a pressman, who in turn mentioned ii
in the London Daily Mail. By the next,
post the Australian received news from those
he sought, and each succeeding mail bulged
with letters and parcels. So large became
the number of parcels addressed to him that
the local postmaster sent ;„long a man with
a barrow to deliver them, and at latest
information (1 p.m.) was making efforts to
borrow an elephant and a string of trucks
The overwhelmed invalid was young Ear.
.Tenkinson, son of our last year’s mayor.
Mrs. A. S. Priestley read a paper on the
laying out of garden cities to the Women s
Club on Thursday night. Two mere men
also spoke, their remarks being listened to
with the most flattering attention. But it s
reallv much more important to Queensland
to know how to lay out prickly pear.

North Ipswich, -was one of the most badLj
wounded of our early contingents. He is
being invalided home, and as some compen-
sation for his pains is bringing with lnm a
bride (his own, my dear). She was a medi-
cal missionary in the Soudan, and had been
nursing in one of the military hospitals at
Alexandria. . . '

elect is at present visiting Sydney.

the Loean district, died this week. Her
husband was- an early stock-raiser, and her
sons established the famous Mclntyre herd,
iours affectionately.

Diamonds Rising Rapidly.
Owing to the British Government
prohibiting in June last the importa-
tion of diamonds into England, prices
have risen largely.
The Germans took Antwerp and
were pouring diamonds into England
and taking sovereigns away. The
Government stopped that.
3 Fine-quality
Diamonds, set
i n Platinum,
choicely eased,
£l2.

Fine Sapphires
and choice Dia-
monds, set in

£B7B/-
Then America, with its plethora of gold, rushed the diamond market, and up went prices. As
there was a shortage of trained cutters to cut the gems, there were not enough diamonds to fill
the purchasing orders.
Fortunately for us, when the diamonds slumped on the war’s declaration, we secured an enor-
mous stock, and so are able to continue our famous cut prices for the highest grades of diamonds.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Austin S.” : Let me as a confirmed bush-
whacker discourse on axes, with a special
curse for the American brand. Three men
going on a contract of bush falling on a
couple of hundred acres (about three months'
work) need about a dozen axes and several
cart-loads of patience to grind them and take
out the gaps—an every day, sometimes every
hour, performance. Has the world lost the
art of tempering steel? There are thousands
of bushmen who would give a quid each
for an axe that could be relied upon in any
timber, and there’s a fortune waiting for
an Australian or M.L. co. that will manu-
facture it.
* * * *
“C.P.R.” : I am a young Australian, with
Australia’s interests at heart, on a visit to
America. I made a stop-over in ’Frisco of
a few days in order to go to the Exposition.
It was a fine sight. Of course the thing I
was most anxious about was to see how my
own Australia was represented. That part
of the show was closed. All the rest was
open. I made inquiries and was informed
that the Australian exhibit was always closed
on Sundays. The chief clerk in the Southern
Pacific Railway office (also an Australian)
told me he had been to the show a dozen
times and had not seen the Australian
exhibit once, as Sunday was the only day
he could get away. The U.S.A. is the place
where they work; and my friend is, in the
same position as millions more. Is it wow-
ser-ism or plain tiredness? In any case, see
this: The average attendance at the Expo-
sition is 55,000 per day. On Sunday it is
100,000. The industrial man is the one we
want to encourage into Australia, and he is
just the one who doesn’t see our turnout,
because he is among the extra 45,000 who
go along on Sunday, the only time he can
get away.
* * * *-
“W.C.M.” : Each week we learn, of Aus-
tralian dependence upon Germany for things
of which we took no notice. The latest is
tinfoil —the “silver paper” in which choco-
lates and other goods are wrapped. Buying
the youngsters their penn’orth to-day, I
noticed that the silver paper had given place
to grease-proof paper. Now I am wonder-
ing where the grease-proof paper came from,
and, as I’m assured it “can’t be made in
Australia,” I’m trying to figure out how
much better off we are.
* * * *
“Garrie”: I gloat over the discomfiture
of one of our big English-owned banks*.
Some time ago this institution circularised
its branches stating that it was importing a
six-years’ supply of ledgers of a standard
pattern, so that in future branches would
have to be content with one size only, and
could not order them to the particular size
that would best suit the business done. It
is stated that the bank saved £lOOO on the
difference in cost of six years’ supply of the
old Australian-made ledgers and the new im-
ported line. In one metropolitan branch the
new books have now been in use for three
months, and already leaves are falling out
and backs are loosening. The ruling is also
irregular. Instead of lasting a year or 18.
months, as the old Australian ledgers did,
new ones will have to be opened at the end
of six months, and at that rate the supply
will be used up in three years; ledger-keepers
will have the delight of heading new ledgers
twice a year, and the £lOOO will be in the
end on the wrong side of the profit and loss
account.
“D.F.” : It will take another loan to pay
off what A. O. Norman, of Hobart, reckons
the Commonwealth owes him. He puts it
down at a million or two, but is willing to
take half a million and call things square.
It’s a long, tangled business; but his end
of the story is that four or five years ago
he submitted to the Federal Government a
dead-sure cure for bitter-pit in apples,
that both A. H. Benson, now Director of
Fruit Culture in Queensland, and Professor
Ewart, of the Melbourne University, re-
ported favorably on it, but that the Gov-
ernment has sat tight and done nothing with
it. However, his cure is being tried in
Maoriland and other places, and he pro-
mises that when the evidence of its value
rolls in he will get busy.
* * •* *
“Mil”: “Carfax” (B. 7/10/’l5) apparently
thinks that, because the Germans have cellu-
lose and alcohol, the English stupidity in not
declaring cotton contraband during nearly
nine months of the war doesn’t matter. But
when cotton reached the German it made his
munition manufacture easier, anyhow. Eng-
lish factories fed the Hun with 17,000,000
pounds of cocoa exported through Holland in
three months. Presumably if the cocoa ex-
port had been proscribed the omnipotent Ger-
man could have made a substitute out of
mud. What’s the good of finding excuses for
criminal foolishness and suicidal dritr."
THE FIRST CONSIDERATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have always insisted upon plenty of nourish-
ment. Many doctors, for one motive or another, set
their faces ''against honest eating; but you cannot
restore waste with fresh air, and where the patient’s
welfare is the first consideration starvation in any
shape is criminal foolishness. —Melbourne medico in
protesting print.
He was a genial M.D.,
And Mat and red of fa«e was he;
He liked, I’ve often heard him say,
A six-course dinner once a day;
And I had reason, too, to think
He rather fancied things to drink.
But .when he took in hand my case,
He turned on me a solemn face.
“You must not drink,” he said, “nor eat—
Abjure such stuff as bread and meat.”
And then to save me from despair
He recommended loads of air.
I pulled through with a long, hard fight,
And still am gaunt, and weak, and white.
The treatment now I know to be
Inspired by grasping policy,
Since patients who are frugal will
Save money for the doctor's bill.
VICTORIA.
Y. Z

G overnment Notices.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 19th October, 1910.
ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVAL COL-
LEGE, JERVIS BAY, N.S.W.
Appointment of Two Instructors.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
sons qualified for appointment as Naval
Instructors to teach Physics and/or Mathe-
matics at the Royal Australian Naval Col-
lege, Jervis Bay, N.S.W.
The following qualifications are necessary
| for appointment
j (a) Applicants must be of Australian
birth, or permanently domiciled in
Australia;
1 (b) Must not be more than 45 years of
age;
(c) Must be able to pass a Government
Medical Examination ;
(d) Must be University graduates, with,
preferably, Honors Degrees in these
subjects, and must be experienced
teachers.
RATES OF PAY.—£35O per annum, ris-
ing to £425 per annum by biennial incre-
ments of £25.
Applicants should state whether they are
married or single; should furnish full infor-
mation as to education, University Degrees,
teaching experience, and general qualifica-
tions ; and should also state whether they are
specially qualified to teach these subjects.
Successful applicants will be appointed on
probation for a period of Twelve Months,
and subject to confirmation at the end of that
period.
Applications, accompanied by copies of
credentials, should be addressed to the Naval
Secretary, Department of the Navy, Mel-
bourne, so as to reach him not later than
15th November, 1915.
Further information may be obtained on
application to the Naval Secretary, or from
the Commanding Officer, Royal Australian
Naval College, Jervis Bay, N.S.W.
GEO. L. MACANDIE,
Naval Secretary.

A 10-stone female defendant appeared in
a Melbourne court last week. The charge
was beer and behavior —both bad. “She's
well known as Helen of Troy, your Wor-
ship,” said the presiding policeman. Helen
of Troy? Helen of Avoirdupois!
“The Wanderer”: They cut their teeth on
corn-cobs on the Hawkesbury, live long and
die hard. The billet of postman in Windsor
has passed down to three generations of Cam-
bridges. Tom has been on the job for the
past 24 years. His father before him had it
for 2G years, and his grandfather for 28
years. I reckon that stands alone south of
the line.
Adelaide’s established reputation for com-
mon-sense moved up another one the other
day, when Rabbi Boas spoke from the pulpit
of Stow Church on Hebrew ideas of right-
eousness. Dr. Bevan, the ancient cherub
of the Independents, claimed that it was the
first time in the history of the Congrega-
tional body that such a thing had been done
or allowed, and made a promise to recipro-
cate by speaking in the Synagogue some time.
A startling illustration of the influence of
environment was given Frank Tudor, Minis-
ter for Customs, when he was in Sydney : a
deputation of people who handle hides waited
on him and asked him to lift the embargo
on the export of sheepskins, on the ground
that the “industry” of shipping the raw
material for somebody else to use employed
juite a number of storemen !
“B. Cee”: At the beginning of this war
officers of a Military Department (it was
not in Ecuador) hired six tarpaulins from
i. Railway Department. It still has them
ind has paid over £lOO for the hire, and the
;arpaulins still belong to the railway. Any-
oody but the Military could buy about 25
arpaulins for the money. And probably the
dx are covering some junk everybody has
’orgotten. Do you wonder that money is
'oing out like water, with a mere handful
if trained men to show for it?
Maorila'ud’s Cold Hash Government armed
tself with a sparrow’s tail the other night
ind attacked the cost of living bogey with
?reat fury. The result of the combat was
:hat the municipalities have been given cer-
tain alleged powers to deal with “bread,
milk and bricks.” and a couple of officials
are to be appointed to investigate things
generally and give the Ministry occasional
reports. What is going to happen then
seems to depend on the juxtaposition of the
planets.
“F.H.”: Of agricultural students, The
Bulletin remarks (14/10/’l5) that “the
old man has mostly discovered that, to merely
equip a decent-sized farm and hang on till
it produces something costs as much is it
does to equip a youngster for a profession.”
Here and there you find an “old man” who
decides not to risk the farm, after all. Judge
Winneke, of Victoria, spent a couple of years
at Dookie after leaving Scotch College, and
then he pulled out. In his case, the shortage
of cheap land meant a good thing for him
later on. Not many farmers at 40 have a
judge’s income, no matter how much they
know about silo-construction and soil-bacilli.
Jabez Wright, who the other night offered
to punch the nose of a fellow Labor member
in the Assembly, brought with him from
Broken Ilill solid Unionistic notions, one of
which is a belief in day-work. His idea is !
familiar to people who used to meet him on |
Labor Conferences and Trade Union Con- j
gresses, where he always battled against !
night sittings. Directly he reached Parlia-
ment lie proposed the same scheme: Parlia-
ment should meet at 10.30 a.m. and close at
0 p.m.; and he got it before the House again
the other day. In this matter, at any rate,
Wright is not wrong. How can he decently
ask the mine-owners in his own constituency
to abolish the night shift ivhen his own
crowd in Parliament deliberately chooses it?
The jury in the Kidman conspiracy case
acquitted the accused and found the prosecu-
tor guilty, the prosecutor being the Defence
Department. It decided, in effect, that there
were such tremendous opportunities for loot
in the muddled department that it would be
amazing if Australia hadn’t been rooked. Of
course, the expected Ministerial explana-
tion was at once forthcoming—things were
rotten yesterday, but they are all right to-
day. The public is very tired of that story,
thopgli; there never was a time in Pearce’s
Ministerial history when things weren’t all
right to-day though all rotten yesterday. The
fact is the Defence Department is still put-
ting soldiers to solve business problems—
which is as absurd as it would be to set the
grocer at the little corner store to train an
artillery brigade.
At latest advices, Elwood Mead, formerly
chairman of the Victorian Water Commis-
sion, was in Arizona, seeking to unravel an
irrigation tangle! He had the company of
an Echuca resident named Syd. Smith, who
is honeymooning in the States. This parti-
cular grain of sand on the. seashore of
Smiths is in the seed line, and gave the
Yuma Examiner some facts about the Vic-
torian Government’s helping hand to settlers
that made the Yuma sheet whoop, and now
the print wants-to know when “Dr.” Mead
is going to recommend that some of this
pleasant physic be prescribed to sick Ameri-
can irrigation settlers, of whom, it seems,
there are thousands.
At the Showgrounds camp (Vic.), the
meningitis outbreak was recently introduced
to a new military surgeon, Captain Charles
Johnson, lately of Ivor ng. an Australian-
horn bacteriologist of the first order. At
that time the Showgrounds bacillus was
receiving attention from several medical
officers. On the authorities asking Captain
Johnson his opinion of the treatment given,
he suggested that the subduing of the nest
should be left to one M. 0., who should be
strictly isolated. This was done, aiid John-
son himself was put into close confinement.
The results of his labors have been so com-
forting that he has been called on to bring
his extensive laboratory from Kerang and
place it at the service of his country in a
wider sphere. Captain Johnson is a young
man ot 42, but he has a trunkful of degrees,
which give him the right to wear no less
Ilian 22 letters after his name in case of
i mergency.

THIS instep-strap shoe has been specially
designed to look trim and dainty
beneath those delightfully feminine
frills and flounced hems that have
returned to vogue again. It is a particularly
pretty shoe, with a fine air of individuality
about it. The front part is patent leather,
and the back and strap are black flowered
brocade, set off by an attractive slide and
ornament of dull silver. The picture gives
some idea of the happiness of this combina-
tion, but the shoe itself is far more charm-
ing than the picture. Made in the dainty,
short-fronted shape and with that smart
Cuban heel which makes walking so comfort-
able, it really is a. “find’’ in the way of
shoes. And ladies will see other and equally
delightful shoes in the latest Rostock ship-
ments, especially if they view them at once.

The Inky Way.
More inappropriate propinquity. The S.
M. Herald came out the other day with
these two headlines, one on the other: —
PRICE.OF HAMS.
SUFFERINGS’ OF THE JEWS.
* * * *
“E.K.11.” reports an interchange of trench
courtesies: —
Having captured a line of Turkish trenches, only
10 or 15 yards separated us from the enemy. A
favorite pastime of our friends opposite during the
day was to treat us to a fusillade of Turkish cartoons
taken from their papers. They wrapped them around
stones and threw them into our burrows. One
depicted a mob of Turks contemptuously pelting Aus-
tralian troops with gibbers. But the monopoly of
cartoons was not held by the enemy. A mail
having arrived in which The Bulletin played a
prominent part, we treated them to some of our
own. Norman Lindsay’s “Onward, Christian
Soldiers,” must have created some interest, for a
cautious hand was'seen to emerge front a'loop hole
and. haul in a stone that had fallen short.., Then we
treated them to the spiked helmet spurring on the
Turkish army. That tickled them up. Within 10
minutes they were showering live bombs on our
trenches to such an extent that we had to take
advantage of our overhead cover, and courtesies were
“off” for the day.
* * * *
Pushed on to the Inky Way last week the
first Australian edition of the Triad, a
monthly devoted to music, literature and
the drama, which has for some years been
on a good wicket in Maoriland. The Triad
is a hospitable print to anyone having some-
thing to say that matters, and as it has a
confirmed habit of saying what it thinks
about the famous tenor and the distinguished
pianist and the star act-or, these people will
henceforth find the heat of the Australian
summer agreeably tempered by the breeze
of candor from a new quarter.
* * * *
“8.X.”: My claim to greatness as a
reporter is based on the fact that I once
took a verbatim note of a' speech by John
Duthie, who has just passed out in Welling-
ton (M.L.). Duthie's friends used to re-
gard him as a financial critic; hence it was
liis custom to get up in the House and try
to knock holes in Dick Seddon’s Budgets;
but as he talked in amazing jerks, and in a
Scottish dialect which led him to deplore
the missing twopences as “matther f’ink-
wherry,” the Gallery used to curse these
occasions with enthusiasm. He had the
Aberdonian’s proverbial lack of humor. But
he made one joke. It was after Seddon’s
return from England with an honorary
degree, John called him “the right honor-
able and lairrrned doctor.” It was his one
wonderful stroke of wit, and the effort nearly
killed him.
. * -X- * *
There was a mighty slump of tripe in
Maoriland newspaper officer hen the Gov-
ernment announced that the press telegrrm
rate would be doubled. A circular was sent
from the head olfice of the Pre s Association
to all agents telling them to in future bury
at least three parts of their copy in the
ash heap. One result is that the thief with
the scissors and paste is working overtime
to keep the linos, going.
* * * *
“The Book-shark” : War Fund Gift Books
—still they come. Maoriland, not content
with absorbing huge quantities of the im-
ported variety—Queen Mary’s, King
Albert’s, Scottish, French, and Melba’s—
is bent on having one of her own. It is
modelled on the lines of Melba’s —best of all
to date —and is on the stocks at Whitcombe’s
printery at Christchurch. Local writers and
artists are enthusiastically helping it along,
and it deserves to be the success they’re
working for. Maoriland’s wonderful pro-
sperity, largely war-brought, ought to ensure
that, though.
“Rosna” (London); Katherine Prichard,
who took Plodder and Stoughton’s £250 prize
for an Australian novel, goes homewards
for a visit next month. Her book, “The
Pioneers,” just out, is reaping good reviews.
The clever damsel first received a printed
slip from the publishers telling her the MS.
was no good and she could have it back
on application. When she was properly
subdued, a- note -arrived apologising for the
mistake, and saying -she’d won the £250
instead. Incidentally she resisted an at-
tempt to make her change her artistic end-
ing for one of the popular variety—for which
more power -to her elbow !
, * * * *
“W’lang”: Mention of Davison (Peter)
Symmons (B. 7/10/T5) brings to mind the
fact that Corowa Free Press turned out
Donald McDonald, Symmons and Albert
Goldberg in succession. McDonald left the
border weekly to take on sport for the Argus
evening sheet, the Mail, which, although
retailed at a ha’penny, had a very short life.
Symmons went to the Age first, then to the
Argus. The Corowa paper was run by J. C.
Leslie, founder of N.S.W. Country Press
Association. His “copy” was a joy to
comps. No commercial clerk ever wrote a
tidier fist.
“O’Phimerty”: According to Fenton’s
“History of Tasmania” one of the earliest
papers in the island was the Tasmanian
and Austral-Asiatic Review, edited by
Robert Lathrop Murray, and started in 1828.
I have hunted in every likely spot in Hobart
for a copy, but haven’t found one. Any way-
farer know anything about it?
* * *
“J.F.H.” : Sir Harry Johnston has a yarn
about Lord Salisbury and the press which
he and all the Cecils more or less affect to
despise. Johnston was surprised to see
Salisbury buying newspapers for the journey,
and said so. Salisbury : “I read them for the
news which I very seldom get in despatches.
Commenting on this reminiscence Johnston,
who has himself done a good deal of-writing
since he gave up exploring and governing
in Africa, declares that, in his experience,
the average journalist know’s far more, than
the average ambassador. But the ambassador
scores, because the less he says the more he
is credited with knowing, while if the jour-
nalist is silent it is, of course, because he
doesn’t know anything!

BEAVER
BOARD
Looking Backward.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IF I could write down all the things I’m
dreaming,
And coin my fancies into something real;
If I could be myself, instead of seeming
The pitiful result of all I feel;
If I could stamp from out times gone their
sorrows,
Strangled resolves, and weariness, and
shame:
If I could think the long, long round of
morrows
Would not recall sins dead and all their
blame;
If I could murder Conscience and her off-
spring,
Blaek-draperied Remorse and wild Regret,
And in their place Youth’s joy of living bring,
I might make something of my future yet;
If I could journey back where Hope forsook
me
And fight the battle from the start anew,
Blot out the bitter track that careless took
me
Fast from the zest of things, and —You!
But naked Thought comes out wth gibes and
sneering
To mock the vanitv of further strife ;
The Devil and my Past keep on appearing,
Hot from that wreck of days I call my life ;
My promises unkept, the faith I’ve broken,
Reproaching eyes and all I valued most
Tell what I know without it being spoken :
“You've run your race, poor sinner, and
you've lost!”
x.s.w.
W.W.P.
The Skirmish at Makauri Flat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was a hot February day, and at play-
time the kids had no energy to do more
than bang lazily round the water tank.
But a different spirit obsessed a stout,
whiskered, coatless, corduroy-trousered indi-
vidual who, stick in hand, strode across the
playground with all tin; speed his ungainly
lines and heavy tonnage would allow. Jen-
kins, the recently-arrived sole teacher at
Makauri Elat, was engaged in making ftp
quarterly returns from his attendance regis-
ter as the one exit from the barn-like edifice
was filled by a burly form. The intruder
rapidly closed the door, turned the key
which projected on the inside of it, and
deposited that small piece of metal in a
pocket so far in rear that it took consider-
able reaching. The teacher, surprised
beyond words at this unwarrantable intru-
sion, and anticipating the man to be a
member of the school committee, thought to
preserve his dignity by remaining seated.
He always felt that short stature and
spectacles were more effective in a chair.
Three strides sufficed to bring a bulky form
towering all over and round him.
“Yer blarsted bully, yer!” burst from a
cavity among the whiskers. “Yer glass-
eyed, snivelling cur, yer! Yer walloped me
.Timmy, did yer! Then, be cripes, I’m ago-
in’ ter wallop yous.”
The stick flourished above the head of the
astonished Jenkins. A method of dealing
with situations of this sort had not been
taught at the Auckland Training College.
"I—l—er —had to,” came the plea. “You
—er —see —er —discipline must be —er —
maintained.”
“Discipline be damned,” was the allitera-
tive retort. “Yous beaten me Jimmy so
hard ’e carn’t milk me blarsted cows. Now,
'oo’s goin’ ter milk the blamed creatures, I’d
like ter know? D’ye expec' me ter do ’is
work, yer blessed coward ?”
No mental effort on Jenkins’s part could
produce a suitable reply. “Six cuts yer give
'im,” roared the whiskers. “Yous’ll ’ave the
same.” Whack! The stick emphasised the
last remark by a rapid descent upon the
meek one’s shoulders.
“Wow!” he yelled, springing from his four-
shilling chair, and hastily beating for cover
of the more substantial desks.
“One, yer crawler!” shouted corduroy-
trousers, starting in pursuit, and barking
his shins over the first form. A torrent of
invective burst forth, and the mercury in the
school thermometer hit the top of the tube.
The kids, aroused to excitement at the
unusual appearance of .Timmy Jinks’s father,
had collected in the porch, all ears to catch
what eventuated within. At the opening
outburst there were smothered chuckles, but
when actual hostilities commenced discretion
was thrown to the winds, and they screamed
with delight. Billy Jones saw the strategic
advantage of the keyhole as a view point,
and bending down attached his eye thereto.
This was too much for Timms, who produced
a pin from his coat and applied half an inch
of it. Jones’s head and the doorknob re-
ceived mutual impressions, and, during the
reprisals upon the venturesome Timms,
Brady occupied the coveted post, graphically
describing all that came within his limited
field of vision.
“The old bloke’s after him.” gasped Brady
excitedly. “Gee whiz! He’s fallen over a
form !” A crash and further expletives bore
out this statement to the delighted scholars.
“Goggles is too quick for him,” communi-
cated the keyhole look-out. “My, that was
a close go! Whiskers nearly got him. Oh.
hang it! Now they’re in the far corner, and
I can’t see.”
But unmistakable sounds bore testimony
to the continuance of the chase, Jenkins
perceived the advantage of his superior
mobility, and kept his pursuer moving in
the hope of tiring him out, or of finding some
opportunity of escape from the small room
and its rapidly collapsing furniture. But
the parent would not tire, and the teacher,
in flying-column fashion, started to harass
him. The first effort was successful. A
well-directed inkwell produced upon the
farmer’s left Optic a splash of black that
was rapidly heightened by a sub-cutaneous
discoloration. The attacking force wa&
roused to language and a desperate effort at
a flanking movem&at. Jenkins skipped round
the room, lightly hurdling a capsized desk.
The pursuit had to clamber over this
obstacle, and the chance for further guerilla
warfare presentee] itself. Whiz went another
inkwell, but at the same moment the coat*
less individual tripped and fell with a yell
like that of a dozen Maoris finishing a war*
cry. The bottle passed harmlessly over him.
and a map of Africa hanging on the wall
showed indications of once again being the
Dark Continent.
With his adversary rising slowly, and
invoking curses on all the school desks in
creation, Jenkins made a hurried bound for
the door, and for one brief instant tugged
furiously at the handle. Brady, seeing this
intention, hung on fiercely outside till the
enemy’s forces within were again mar-
shalled and the pursuit had recommenced.
Whiskers now copied the teacher’s tactics.
Grabbing an armful of articles from the
school table, he waded in with an attempt
to close under cover of his own fire. Jenkins
ducked his presentation inkpot, hurled at
his head, and a portrait of the King and
Queen immediately assumed deep mourning.
The ebony ruler, from a closer range, nar-
rowly missed, while the teacher’s reply with
a pencil case failed to disconcert the attack.
Rapid flight was the master’s only chance,
and he caught the hand-bell fairly on the
back of the head in attempting this.
Smarting with rage at being hit on the
head with a school bell, Jenkins lost that
coolness which was essential to his safety.
Seizing a large slate he tised it as a shield,
successfully stopping the flight of the dic-
tionary and a geography book as he replied
with Doolan’s parcel of lunch and an
enamel mug from the capsized desk. But
the farmer was within range. The stick
smote fiercely, and a sho tit of “Two 1” whs
greeted by cheers from the listeners without.
“Cur!” shrieked the teacher. Biff! And
the slate descended upon a bullet head, an
astounded face and bush of whiskers appear-
ing above il frame which hflng effectively
round a thick neck.
“Damnation! Tons’ll stiller fet this!”
Corduroy panted. But Jenkins had snatched
the window pole, and mounting a desk in the
far corner, set himself, bayonet-drill fashion,
to the “ready.”
The parent manoeuvred for an opening in
this effective guard. Meanwhile Ginger
Thomson and Porky Edwards had realised
the fine View that might be obtained from
the windows. Mounted on Porky’s shoul-
ders, Ginger could easily look over the sill.
Whiskers had just hurled the bottie of red
ink, but ill holding it up preparatory to
firing, had poured a liberal quantity of the
fluid over his oWn shirt.
“Gee!” exclaimed Ginger, “Goggle’s
stabbed him in the Weskit with the winder
pole, an’ he’s bleedin’ like a stuck pig.” Porky
begged “swaps” so that lie could see. Ginger
implored him to be patient. Just then, to
divert the direction of the window pole, the
invader essayed a shot with the sixth stan-
dard reader. Ginger saw the fault in the
elevation, and ducked as the book flew out
of the window. Porky wobbled beneath
him, and the tower of observation collapsed
instantly.
“You sneak, you!” roared Ginger. “Just
’cos I wouldn’t swap places at once, you let
me down.”
“Liar!” replied his erstwhile support.
“You fell and pulled me with you.”
“Who’s a liar?”
“You are, an’ there’s yer blow,” and the
exasperated Porky lashed out. Billy Jones,
hearing the additional row, looked round -the
corner of the porch.
“Hey!” he called. “Here’s Ginger and
Porky a-scrappin’ under the window.”
There was an immediate rush to see a fight
of which a good view could be obtained.
Only Brady, at the keyhole, stayed in the
porch.
“’E let me down,” explained Ginger as
the kids gathered round.
“ ’E’s a liar!” gasped Porky, lunging
fiercely at Ginger. The boys immediately
took sides.
“ ’lt ’im low, Ginger!” urged Timms, see-
ing his classmate’s features rapidly changing
under Porky’s attentions.
“Shut yer gob!” retaliated Jones.
“Shan’t. Go an’ stick pins in yerself!”
This reminder of a previous insult was too
much for anyone of the name of Jones. In
a second another “mill” was in progress, and
the delighted spectators forgot all about the
proceedings within.
The Reverend Waters, commonly rejoicing
in the nickname of “Milk-and-Waters,” was
doing the round of his Presbyterian flock on
a nag borrowed from his deacon, and as he
approached the school was pondering over
whom he might call upon for a midday meal.
The sight of sanguinary contest in the play-
ground caught his eye, and the man of
peace cantered up to the scene.
“Hey ! Look out! The preacher bloke !”
cried one of the boys.
Hostilities ceased instantly. Timms
crawled under the school and the other com :
batants dodged round the corner of the build -
ing.
“Children ! children ! Why this shocking,
this awful behavior? Remember your Sun-
day-school lesson last week about”-—-
A string of oaths and blasphemies came
from the window. The teacher had got
home with the window pole. A crash of
furniture followed. The minister gazed in
pious horror. Timms’s head peeped out from
under the school.
“Please, sir, Jimmy Jings’s father’s locked
teacher inside, an’s killin’ ’im.”
“Oh! Awful! Horrible!” gasped the
entablature of truth. “Run away, children.
Run away from such frightful contamina-
tion.” The minister rode up to the window,
through which, from the vantage of his
safldle. he surveyed a scene of wreckage that
froze his soul. Beneath him the teacher
stood on one of the few upright desks bran-
dishing a window pole, a scarlet weal across
one cheek. The farmer, opposite, flourished
a stick in one hand and a box of chalk in the
other, cursing in furious tones the while.
“Brethren ! Brethren ! Stop”
He did. An ineffectual reach with the
pole caused the wild discharge of the clialk-
box. The minister’s face arrested its im-
petus. The children outside were treated to
an exhibition of descent frbfil horseback that
would have proved a star turn in Wirths’
Circus, and were next aware of an animal
careering through the gates, and a figure in
clerical garb but minus clerical dianity sit-
ting on the ground spitting invocation's.

DROPSY
Watery Swellings under the Eyes and in the Ankles,
The fluid you take with your food,
in a single month , weighs as much as
your entire body.*
Most of this fluid has to pass out
through the kidneys, bladder and
urinary system.
Imagine what would happen if the
outlets for this water were stopped up !
In a few hours the limbs and body
would swell to a great size with the
collected water.
That is what actually takes place in
dropsy.
Dropsy is the name given to the
soft, watery swellings which appear in
the ankles, limbs and body, under the
eyes or in the eyelids, or on the backs
of the hands. If you press your finger
into the swollen flesh the pitting slowly
fills up with the water again.
*(i.e. —If you are of average weight.)
f
F
DROPSICAL EYES.
DROPSY is not a disease in itself,
but it is serious because it indicates
serious disease.
It may show that the kidneys are
failing to separate the waste water from
the blood, or that there is something
obstructing the urinary canals and
preventing the waste water passing out;
or it may be caused by defective action
of the heart.
Tapping takes away the water from
under the skin, but more water soon
collects, because tapping cannot reach
the cause of the dropsy, and as long
as the cause remains the dropsy will
continue.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have
a quick and direct action on the kidneys
and bladder. They promote a free flow
from the system, washing out clogging
impurities from the passages, and
draining out the collected water through
the natural channels. They gently
lead the kidneys back to health and
activity, and thus reach the cause of
most cases of dropsy.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have
no action on the heart, nor on the liver,
stomach or bowels ; they are solely for
the kidneys and urinary system, and
are, therefore, of the highest value in
dropsy, gravel, stone and all diseases
arising from kidney and bladder trouble.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are sold by all
chemists and storekeepers at 2/9 per bottle, or
post free by Foster-McClellan Co., 76 Fitt Street,
Sydney.

“ ’Oo ’it yer, sir?” gasped Ginger, running
up. “Which one was it?”
“Go away, confound you !” And the par-
son expectorated a half-tooth. A minute
later Brady, still at the keyhole, was pushed
roughly aside. A force hurled itself against
the door; the cheap lock gave, and the Rev.
Milk-and-Waters precipitated himself, like
Bayard or Goliath, against a mass of flesh
as this mass was preparing to proceed in a
direction at right angles to the impact. Down
went the two over a Avreckage of forms and
desks. The farmer, furious, smote with
curses upon the ucav arrival. The latter
responded with a kick to the stomach that
silenced the invader. The Rev. Milk-and-
Waters fought like an army Avith banners.
Jenkins rushed to the door, and fell over an
inrush of school children. Then, realising
the probable plight of his rescuer, he seized
the extinguisher which a thoughtful school
committee had hung in the porch in case of
fire, and returned to the scene. Black
cloth and corduroy trousers still re-
mained entangled in the wreckage of the
furniture. The preacher Avas now doing
battle after the manner of one inspired.
“Stop, or I fire!” cried Jenkins dramati-
cally, pointing the nozzle of the extinguisher
at the whiskered face.
“Damn you!” roared its owner, and then
for the first time he saw Avith whom he was
struggling. “Hell! It’s the parson, and
I’ve blacked his eye.”
Ere the other two could say anything, the
huge form of Farmer Jinks had precipitated
itself through the doorway, and a dishevelled
object Avas making its way as rapidly as
possible along the road, a Avake of jeering
school kiddies following. Behind ran a
preacher with murder in his eye.
It took the Makauri Flat Committee three
meetings at the local pub to assess the
damage done, and two school concerts had to
be organised to raise the cash for repairs.
A relieving teacher acts at present in the
place of Jenkins. The Reverend Waters
possesses a presentation front tooth, the
faintest evidence of a once-very-black eye,
and a gift horse that is worth looking in the
mouth. A prominent member of his flock
has not since appeared at service, but the
collection-plate has not suffered, and the
pastor is hopeful of the eventual effect of the
prayers of the righteous.
A. A. HINTZ.
A Letter from Benno.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is written “On the Way to the lilanhy
■ Front,'' from “Marine Terrace, Any Old
Date .” You lenow Benno, our old
friend whom ive met in Elder Man’s
Lane.
“ 'P\EAB ’ARRY, —I can’t tell you much,
I W because, in the first place, I don’t
know much, and, in the second place,
I’m not allowed to tell the little I do know.
You’ve often complained that you’ve been
like that yourself in days of peace, so you
knoAV what it is in days of war.
“I might be able to tell you more when I
land. And then, agen, I mightn’t. You
see, there’s always some risk in landing in
a foreign country with unfriendly intentions.
There’s often some risk in landing in a for-
eign country even with friendly intentions;
and, as far as / can make out, our inten-
tions are most unfriendly. No. Consider-
ing the reception the fellows ahead of us got,
and the way they responded to it, ive’re not
likely to be met by the Mayor and Aldermen
with an Address of Welcome and a free feed
afterwards.
“Something similar —or not similar —to
being at home in times of peace. For
instance, I once went into the back yard of
an old pal with the fixed intention of stoush-
ing him, and his pretty sister gave me as
many empty bottles as I could hump to the
cart in one load for nothing. She didn’t
know that anything had come between us.
And the very next day I went into the back
yard of a new pal I liked (you, in fact)
with the friendliest intentions imaginable,
combatable with business, and his fool-dog
nearly et me. He was as big as the ass he
was. Dogs are mostly conservative about
their own back yards—especially mongrel
dogs—and their front gardens, too, when
they’ve got one. They don’t take the slight-
est notice of intentions. In fact, signs of
friendship will often make them madder than
the sight of a stick. It’s just the same
with the Front, as far as I can make out.
“The only difference between soldiers on a
transport and passengers on another trans-
port is that one lot do things because they
don’t have to do them, and the other lot do
things because they do have to do them.
And I think the passengers are the worst off.
“After you’re three or four days out
there’s nothing doing. One wave looks just
like another, except when one, bigger than
the rest, slaps you in the ear, and then it’s
just like the third one behind it, or the third
one in front of it. They seem to go by threes.
And in the flat, lazy, hazy days —you know
them—Sydney’s thousands of millions of
miles away, and in another world, and there
never was no Blue’s Point-road. And” ——
here Benno lapses into his half-pathetic, half-
comical bad grammar—“and it seems as if
the Old Uns never was.
“The ship is rolling lazy-like, and I lean
back comfortable against something, and
look sleepy and half stupid into the haze,
and doze off”—there’s Benno trying sleepily
to reconstruct the Old Uns, and being over-
powered by the tropical languor of the sea,
and dozing off —“and I wake, with a jerk
like, when the ship rolls over a bit
extra, and reach for old Geddup’s reins;
for I think I’m coming down Blue’s Point-
road with a load of empty bottles, on a
sleepy September day, and just struck the
steep place below the butcher’s shop.
“I left some of my pay to be drawn by old
Geddup. Y’ou see, I had no one to draw it
after the Old Uns was gone. And, anyway,
it was up to me to pension off old Geddup.
He’s Avith my brother-in-law. Jack Parker—
the contractor I told you about—who’s got
a bit of land and a little, nlace of his ,own
out Middle Harbor way. He’s a good, easy-
going fellow, Jack is, like most brothers-in-
law, but, like most easy-going chaps of his
sort, he ain’t easy-going when it comes to
sticking to his word. He'll look after old
Geddup, and just give him as much exercise,
under pretence of work, as is good for his
health; and Geddup’ll wonder, perhaps,
what’s become of Benno. No. I've got no
Arab-to-his-Steed ideas about horses. It
was just a sort of friendly partnership
arrangement between me and old Geddup,
though we got along a lot smoother than any
other two partners I ever knew.
“Well, and what do we think about, and
what do we do? Well, there’s this differ-
ence between us and ordinary steerage pas-
sengers : we’re got no anxiety about drop-
ping into work when we land. So we’ve got
nothing much to think about.
“The chaps talk about almost anything
except the war. Two or three of those long,
loose, lanky, easy-going, freckled Out-
backers of yours will drift together on
deck, and find out that they’ve been at some
place called Bungaroo, shearing or droving
or something, and though it’s been at differ-
ent times they’ll get excited about it. With
this kind of Australian, lankiness and
loose-builtness and freckles and easy-going-
ness all seem to go together. And something
else. Their great bony shoulders and arms
and hands, and their loose, nobby joints and
wrists, and their big, loose hips—and their
freckles —seem to me to be held together by
nothing but good nature. But I don’t sup-
pose they’d be extra-easy-going—and per-
haps they’d lose a lot of their good nature—-
if they were raked up a bit too much. Y’ou
can’t call them boys ; nor yet grown-up boys.
They’re just—well, they’re just men-boys.
With all the manliness of men and none of
the littleness, and all the boyishness of boys
and none of the ‘bigness.’
“Well, and then they’ll talk about Jack
W ilson the storekeeper, and his calico signs
on the trees along the tracks for miles out:
and ‘Ratty Bob’—he’s the town crank—and '
his politics; and how they sent letters to
him inviting him to stand for the next elec-
tions. And ‘Dick the Devil,’ who was always
in trouble with the police ; and ‘Mad Mick’
Martin the shearer; and they’ll wonder
where poor ‘Dick the Devil’ and ‘Mad Mick’
are now, and whether they’ve enlisted or are
already at the Front. And Mary Jane Hill,
or Mary Ann McKay, who kept the Post
Office Hotel on the bank of the river. I
always think of Mary Jane Hill or Mary
Ann McKay as a big, bony, grindstone-
faced woman with a sore about her face
somewhere. But she must be a real good
sort underneath. And they’ll talk about
fights and practical jokes and horse-racing,
and get excited and joyful, till you’d think
they were on their way back to Bungaroo
to see some life and excitement, instead of
being on the way to the biggest and blazing-
est war that ever was in this world.
“Ah well, these hazy, lazy, sleepy days!
Sometimes the Old Uns seem deader than
ever they were—just as if they’d never
been, I mean. And sometimes it seems as if
I’ll wake up out of this dream and find
myself on some bags and rags on top of the
load going across on the old Blue’s Point
Horse Ferry, to find the Old Uns well and
hearty.
“Ah well again—I suppose this dream
will end up, like all others ; and I suppose
it’ll end up suddenly, shortly after we land.
Perhaps shortly before. Anyway, I’ll do
my best to keep alive. I’m used to tUjs
world, but I ain't used to any of the others.
So good luck, Harry Lawson.—From Benno”
HENRY LAWSON.
The Ferry Damosel.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I FANCY it was twelve o’clock—
’Twas after closing time. I know —
The silver night had tongues to mock
My errant heart that trembled so.
And all the wide seas seemed to rock
My tangled fancy to and fro.
My heating pulses were a-spin,
My intellect was but a thread
Through kissing the delightful sin
Of that warm opening in her head—-
I dreamt her love was good to win
Because her pleasant lips were red.
The wetness of the harbor broke
In foamy bubbles shining white
Before the engine’s throbbing stroke
That drove our keel across the night;
Her hair was soft as funnel smoke,
And curled and waved for my delight.
Beneath her velvet lids I saw
Tier soft eyes full of blackest fire;
Iler satin cheek against my jaw
Stirred all the whiskers of desire,
As Orpheus moved the tender raw
First catgut raptures of his lyre.
Short is the good the gods ordain,
And she was short as maidens go.
So was the time, I must explain,
And ferry-boats, though passing slow,
Are all too swift to ease the pain
Of love that may not overflow.
We reached the wharf, we said good bye;
We went across a cold, faint plank;
The little stars that ruled the sky
I neither sought to blame nor thank;
They winked, she went —the world was dry
As leaves of ledgers in a bank.
But still I think about that girl
Whenever I am very late,
Whene’er I see the crayfish curl
His rosy fingers round my plate;
I know that somewhere planets whirl
To weave my horoscope of fate;
I know that somewhere I may see
Iler soft eyes full of darkest night,
The while she gently leans to me
And stirs the whiskers of delight;
And when like smoke her hair shall be
Around me, I skull feel all right.
N.S.W.
